opportunity to work on this project. Especially since it was a self-designed project of interest and is
in a new ﬁeld of mine. A great thanks goes to Arto Heiskanen, Senior Researcher for all the help with
theory and expertise throughout the project. I would like to give a great thanks to Claudy D’Costa,
PhD student for all the help and guidance in the laboratory during the entire thesis. For the laboratory
space, materials and chemicals used, I would like to thank the departments on DTU and the companies
Abvigens Inc for providing the antibody and the tracer, and Fermentek for providing the analyte for the
alternariol part. In this project, I have learned the ﬁeld of biosensors, new laboratory skills and how to
make good experimental data, but I have also learned many soft skills that is needed behind the scenes
of a project. Projects are about learning by doing, learning from your mistakes and maintaining your
optimism.
General Information
This project is based on the previous special project ’The Establishment of a Theoretical Biosensor Set-
up and Analysis for the Small Analyte Alternariol’ by Laura Kastell and text and information can be
reused in this master thesis. Especially, in the Introduction and in the section Theory and Background
Information. In the ﬁeld of biosensors, there is no overall textbook available, therefore a lot of the
information is given by the supervisors, especially by Arto Heiskanen, Senior Researcher, and is learned
through the course Development and analysis using Biosensors at DTU.

ABSTRACT
The focus of this thesis was to make basic research on alternariol detection using antibody based
biorecognition. Initially, the behaviour of the biological elements for alternariol were tested using ELISA
and afterwards applied on an electrode chip to construct an electrochemical biosensor. The focus was
on determining the setup parameters: sensitivity, i.e.
IC50 and the limit of detection, the matrix
eﬀect of tomato juice based on diﬀerent preparations, and the location of the analyte alternariol in
the product samples. The biosensor setup was characterised electrochemically, using electrochemical
techniques, impedance spectroscopy and cyclic voltammetry, in order to assess the successfulness of
electrode cleaning and modiﬁcations. Amperometric detection was used to determine the maximum
signal together with an analyte exposed in a competitive assay to validate the biosensor design and try
to form a calibration curve. In the project two analytes, cortisol and alternariol, were investigated and
it appeared that the behaviour of the two arrangements were quite diﬀerent. The results demonstrated
that even though the biosensor concept is theoretically a universal one, the regeneration process, the
behaviour of the analytes and their localization in the product samples diﬀered. This project showed
that the commercial antibody and tracer from Abvigen Inc could be used for a potential biosensor. This
project is a proof of concept and forms a starting point of the research leading to a potential alternariol
biosensor for applications in food industry, as a lot of interesting research was uncovered during the
thesis project. This project merely represented preliminary research, and there is still a long way to go
in the development of an industrial biosensor for the market.

INTRODUCTION
1
INTRODUCTION
Fungi are found throughout the world and have the main function in nature of facilitating the decaying
process of organic matter. In biotechnological industry, fungi are deﬁned as cell factories that produce
a wide variety of important compounds, we humans beneﬁt from.
Yet in other industrial sectors
such as the agricultural industry, fungi are viewed as a pestilence, since the decay causes reduced
crop yields, which results in lower income [1]. Fungi can also be deﬁned as a risk to human health if
consumed, due to their production of harmful secondary metabolites, called mycotoxins. Mycotoxins
are secreted from the fungi and can cause a signiﬁcant contamination of raw materials used in the food
industry, therefore there have been a large focus on how to detect them [2]. Alternariol is an example
of a mycotoxin which is mainly produced by the fungi group Alternaria and is reported to cause severe
health risks since it exerts the properties of mutagenicity, hemotoxicity and carcinogenicity [1, 3–6].
Alternaria spp. are ubiquitous and alternariol can therefore be found in a large range of foods and
feeds, among others tomatoes, which the fungi is a frequent invader of [3, 7]. The possibility of eating
a mouldy tomato is low, but due to limitations in the industry to sort rotten vegetables from the
production line, the occurrence of alternariol is probably more frequent in processed tomato products
[5, 8].
This is exacerbated, since the fungi infection can occur pre- and postharvest [9] and since
mycotoxins are kept very stable during the food processing [10]. In 2019, it has been stated by the
European Commission that the current maximum allowed concentration of alternariol is between 5
to 30 µg/kg depending on the food group, for instance, in processed tomato products the maximum
concentration is 10 µg/kg [1, 11]. Hence, analysis of alternariol in raw materials and processed food is
of utmost importance to ensure the products meet the current food safety standards. Alternariol is
not the only mycotoxin produced by Alternaria fungi, and has not recently been discovered but the
restriction in food-products are still under evaluation and not yet oﬃcially deﬁned.
Traditionally, detection of alternariol and other mycotoxins is done by HPLC or LC-MS, which requires
extensive sample pre-treatment and employment of expensive equipment. These analytical procedures
have high speciﬁcity and sensitivity, but they are also time-consuming, expensive, labour-intensive
and have to be performed in an advanced laboratory by trained personnel [2, 10].
Even though
electrochemical immunosensors have been studied and developed for many small toxins, a biosensor
speciﬁc for alternariol has not been found, only an immunosensor for alternariol monomethyl ether,
a derivative toxin of alternariol, has been discovered [12]. Biosensors have the potential to facilitate
faster and less labour-intensive detection and can be used in conjunction with raw material handling
or in food processing lines. It also has the advantage, that it can be operated by the staﬀon-site,
which makes the detection easier and more eﬃcient. The application of a biosensor will thusly free
companies from the costs associated with chromatography and the immediate result of the test will
ease supply lines, which makes the development of this type of biosensor of large commercial interest.
Potential customers are all companies in Europe that work with raw material, which can be infected
by the fungi, since they now have to control the level of alternariol in their products.
Page 1 of 151
==PAGE:7==
INTRODUCTION
Companies like Heinz, Knorr, Mou and others, that produce processed tomato products, such as
ketchup, tomato juice, tomato purée, tomato sauce and canned tomatoes could have special interest
in such a technology due to the new guide lines.
1.1
Aim and Purpose of the Project
The aim of this project is to develop an electrochemical immunobiosensor for detection of alternariol
in food samples with an industrial perspective and the project will serve as a proof of concept to
see if it is possible to make alternariol detection easier for the industry, than using the traditional
analytic methods such as HPLC and LC-MS. To develop a good biosensor, many separate parts such
as sampling, sample handling and detection are involved in the entire process and determines the value
of the biosensor. As this project has a short time frame, it is mainly focusing on the detection part
and on how to develop a biosensor setup, but some aspects like the matrix eﬀect are also considered
and tested, as it gives an idea of how important and crucial the other parts are for the detection. The
entire process is brieﬂy described and discussed as all parts are dependent on each other to make a
good industrial biosensor system.
The project comprises an initial ELISA-based testing of the available antibodies and tracers and the
function of a competitive assay for a small-molecular analyte. The project focuses on deﬁning the
starting information that is needed to understand how the biosensor setup works and behaves, which
includes testing the commercial antibody and tracer and attempts to locate where the analyte could be
in a commercial product sample. The project contains biosensing assays preformed with ELISA, that
can evaluate the balance of the biosensor setup, the crucial performance parameters, such as sensitivity,
i.e. IC50, limit of detection, and the matrix eﬀect from a tomato product, before testing the biosensor
setup with electrochemical detection. As the future vision is the development of an industrial biosensor,
it will include discussions of how sampling and sample handling including preparation methods and
pre-concentration are important in order to utilize a biosensor-based monitoring at a manufacturing
site. This thesis is considered as a starting point of the research and development, that can give rise to
other projects that all can be combined and ensure that a potential biosensor can come to the market.

==PAGE:8==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
2
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
In this section, all of the theory and background information for the project are described to get an
overview of all the aspects from the analyte, biorecognition element, analytical approach, transducer,
and detection technique to the sample matrix and preparation.
This section provides all of the
necessary knowledge to start the project from scratch and to develop the necessary experiments and
carry them out.
2.1
Signiﬁcance of Fungal Toxins for Food Industry
2.1.1
Alternariol
The analyte of interest alternariol (AOH), has the chemical name 3,7,9-Trihydroxy-1-methyl-6H-
dibenzo-[b,d]pyran-6-one. It is classiﬁed as a mycotoxin and is among one of the major toxins produced
by the fungi group Alternaria, including the species A. alternata, A. arborescens, A. brassicae, A. bras-
sicicola, A. infectoria, A. radicina and A. tenuissima. AOH is a small compound of 258.3 Dalton,
within the class/group Benzopyrones/pyranones, more speciﬁcally deﬁned as a dibenzo-alpha-pyrone
(see the chemical structure of AOH in Figure 1). The toxicity of AOH has been proposed to be a
potential threat to human health and the degradation of the compound is diﬃcult due to its stability.
Detailed data is still missing on this subject, but it has currently been reported that AOH is stable
between room temperature and up to 100°C in a time period from several weeks to several minutes
depending on the temperature in both solids and liquid media, such as ﬂour, wine, and juice. Other
experiments showed that AOH can remain stable at 230°C in bread baking, but it has also been
proved, that the concentration can be signiﬁcantly reduced by applying a heat treatment at 121°C for
60 minutes [3, 4].
Figure 1
The molecular structure of AOH. Picture from Escrivá et al. [4].
Page 3 of 151
==PAGE:9==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
Source of Alternariol
The main producer of alternariol is Alternaria, which is categorized as a fungal genus that is widespread
in nature and contains both pathogenic- and saprophytic species. What makes this genus so diﬃcult
to control is its ability to grow and produce toxins at a large range of temperatures and under
conditions that would be unfavourable for most fungi, including low water activity (aw), that refers to
“free” water available for microbial growth, and low temperatures. Even though, the optimal growth
temperature lies between 22-30°C, it has been reported that the fungi can grow in the range of 6.5 to
-5°C. Thereby, the Alternaria species can grow on commodities and cause spoilage during the storage
and transportation even if the commodities are kept cold e.g. refrigerated. This makes the fungi
diﬃcult to deal with when producing food products [4–6, 13, 14]. The Alternaria species have optimal
growth on pH between 4 to 5.4, are able to grow in low oxygen conditions (conc. 0.25%) and can
grow at a minimum aw down to 0.86. However, the growth rate of the fungi is proportional to higher
oxygen concentration and higher water activity (aw on 0.98) [5]. A normal strategy for a fungi is
to protect its survival by either having the ability to grow in harsh conditions or protecting itself
from other fungi and microorganisms by producing biologically active compounds such as mycotoxins.
As the Alternaria species have the ability to survive at low temperature, low oxygen level and low
water activity, it normally only spends its energy to produce toxins at a temperature between 15
to 30°C and at limiting water activity on 0.90. It has been reported that the optimal mycotoxin
production for Alternaria species depending on the strain and substrate was at around 25°C at an
aw on 0.98 [5]. The Alternaria species A. alternata is reported as the most important for mycotoxin
contamination of vegetables and fruits as it can infect both living plants and the harvested products
depending on which genetic form is active, pathogenic or saprophytic, respectively. In regards to food
safety, its saprophytic stage is considered as the main concern, since it is in this form it can colonize
the harvested commodities and produce large amounts of mycotoxins, which can end up in the ﬁnal
processed products without knowing [4, 15]. Even though, the main source for AOH is within the
Alternaria genus, it has been reported that other fungi have the ability to produce AOH as well, such
as Phomopsis spp. [3].
Colonization, Contamination and Toxicity of the Alternaria Fungi
The Alternaria species colonize a large range of plants and is capable of producing more than 70
mycotoxins, which play an important and positive role in the fungal pathogenicity towards the plants
and in commodities, but they also play a central adversarial role in food safety for both animals and
humans [4]. An important documented information is that the fungi can cause decay of both pre- and
post harvest [9] even under harsh conditions, which makes the control and prevention even harder to
handle. Alternaria mycotoxins, including AOH, are categorized as food contaminants and have been
found in a large range of food groups in both raw and processed form: grains and grain-based products,
vegetables and vegetable products, fruit and fruit products, vegetable oils, alcoholic beverages and
food for infants and small children (see the diﬀerent food groups that can be contaminated with AOH
in Appendix A.1 Various Food Groups - Contaminated with AOH, Figure 40) [1]. The presence of the
Alternaria fungi and the metabolites produced by it can cause problems both for agriculture, since
the fungi is a plant pathogen that can reduce the crop yields and thereby have a negative eﬀect on
Page 4 of 151
==PAGE:10==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
the economy of the farmers and the food processing industry, as well as for humans and animals,
since Alternaria mycotoxins have been documented to be harmful or have negative health eﬀect. The
toxicity of AOH is noted to cause mild acute eﬀects to mammalian cells, since the LD50 is higher
than 400 mg/kg of body weight for animals, where for unborn animals the mycotoxin is lethal at a
concentration level of 100 mg/kg of body weight [7]. The long term eﬀects are more serious as the
compound has adverse consequences on the genetics of the consumer, which deﬁnes how dangerous the
toxin is. Alternaria mycotoxins are reported to be cytotoxic, teratogenic, fetotoxic and carcinogenic
and thereby can contribute to cancer, more speciﬁcally human esophageal cancer in Africa and China,
where the contamination is high. They are also reported to be clastogenic, estrogenic and mutagenic
to both mammalian- and microbial cells and being tumorigenic in an experiment with rats [4].
AOH is documented to have a low acute toxicity. However, it is reported to be mutagenic, hemotoxic
and carcinogenic. Additionally, AOH can also act as an inducer of DNA double stand-breaks and cause
notable cytotoxicity to both mammalian- and microbial cells [1, 3–6]. Furthermore, AOH is a potent
inhibitor of the enzymes topoisomerase I and II, which handle over- or underwinding of DNA in the
cell [3] and is reported to have an estrogenic potential and have inhibitory eﬀect on cell proliferation
[4, 16].
2.1.2
Toxins Produced by the Alternaria Fungi
Alternariol is not the only mycotoxin produced by Alternaria spp.
and it is frequently found in
combination with other mycotoxins especially with alternariol monomethyl ether (AME), since they
share the same biosynthesis pathway. The group of Alternaria mycotoxins are very diverse and belong
to diﬀerent chemical classiﬁcations among which are steroids, pyranones, terpenoids, quinines and
other compounds containing phenolics or nitrogen. Within the group the major and most studied
mycotoxins are AOH, AME, altenuene (ALT), tenuazonic acid (TeA) and tentoxin (TEN). However,
in recent years the research has extended and other toxins have been discovered and identiﬁed (see
the chemical structures with names in Appendix A.2 Alternaria Mycotoxins - Name and Chemical
Structures, Figure 41). One group in particular is interesting, the perylenquinone derivatives such
as altertoxin (ATX) I to III, alterperylonol/alteichin (ALTCH) and stemphyltoxins (STE) I to III,
since it includes minor metabolites but yet is reported to be more toxic to mammalian cells than
AOH or AME. Alternaria spp. produce more compounds than shown in the Appendix A.2 Alternaria
Mycotoxins - Name and Chemical Structures, but due to lack of pure substances, no report on them
have yet been found and only the most studied toxins are available on the market.
Statistically,
according to around 60 studies, AOH and AME were analysed and detected in 80%, TEN in 50% and
ALT in 36% of the studies. The ATXs were analyzed and detected in 16%, where only a few studies
documented other metabolites such as asmacrosporin in 9%, and 3% included ALN, infectopyrone,
monocerin and AALs in total. In general, the statistical trend and most common toxins observed in
vegetables, fruits and derived products are AOH and AME, followed by the toxins TEN, TeA, ALT
and ATXs [4, 15], showing that AOH and AME the predominant ones.
Page 5 of 151
==PAGE:11==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
2.1.3
Alternaria, Mycotoxins and Tomato
Tomato is one of the most vulnerable vegetables/fruits to fungal decay due to their soft tissue and
thin skin [5, 8, 10, 17], and Alternaria is the fungi which is most frequently found on mouldy tomatoes
and creates black splotches on the surface of the tomatoes (see Figure 2) [5]. The fungal invasion
often appears during the ripening stage and the infection can occur either at the stem end or on the
commodity itself. If it is on the commodity it is most likely due to cracking of the skin either from
excessive moist in the growth phase, from chilling by storage or from a mechanical injury. Various
Alternaria species have been found to infect tomatoes, such as A. alternata, A. longipes, A. tenuissima
and A. arborescens (see the structure and spores of the A. alternata in Figure 2). Especially the A.
arborescens which causes stem canker, has been proved to genetically carry a pathogenicity factor
towards tomatoes [5, 15].
Tomatoes have beneﬁcial conditions for the fungi, since they have high
water content and slightly low pH value. The only factor, except for a rapid farm to market strategy
that can prevent or slow down the fungal growth and especially the production of mycotoxins, is
the temperature during transportation and storage. It has been reported that the toxin production is
limited at a temperature of 6°C and therefore is considered as the most safe and controlled temperature
to maintain for storage and transportation [5]. The main issue is not directly consumption of the
mouldy and toxin ﬁlled tomatoes, but rather their usage for processed products, such as juice, sauces,
pulp, puree, paste and concentrate, since there are limitations that prevent proper removal of the rotten
tissue in the current industrial process and it is too time consuming to remove decayed tomatoes before
the process [4, 5]. Even though some tomato products are put through a heating process, the toxins
are quite stable and can therefore be found in the commercial end product and thereby end up in the
human diet. It has been reported that 92-93% of analysed tomato products were contaminated by
AOH, AME, TEN and TeA [15].
Figure 2
A mouldy tomato and the fungi under a macro-and microscope. The fungi Alternaria creates black
splotches on the surface of the tomato (left). The fungi A. alternata under a macroscope (middle) and a microscope
(right), which shows the structure and spores. Pictures from Patriarca A. and Pinto V. F. [5].
2.1.4
Criteria and Maximum Concentration Level in Food Products
Alternaria mycotoxins are not newly found since EFSA has reported in 2011 [1] the potential health
risks for both humans and animals in food and feed, but due to lack of quantitative data on the risk
assessment related to the food safety, the maximum concentration levels have not been given until the
Page 6 of 151
==PAGE:12==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
European Commission proposed current indicative levels for AOH, AME and TeA in 2019 [11] (see
Table 1 for a range of food groups). It has to be said that these criteria on the maximum allowed
concentrations are not oﬃcial and are still under evaluation, thereby regulation is subject to change
when more data is made available. It has been stated that the concentrations of Alternaria mycotoxins
(AOH, AME and TeA) in commercial food products were between 1 to 8700 µg/kg, where the highest
levels of AOH and AME were among other products found in tomato paste [1, 4]. According to the
current data, the maximum concentration level for AOH in processed tomato products is 10 µg/kg,
while for AME and TeA it is 5 µg/kg and 500 µg/kg, respectively. These criteria sets new requirements
for the manufacturers and force them to develop new control protocols on their products to keep the
concentration levels below the allowed values.
