2.2
Choice of Biorecognition Element And Analytical Approach
2.2.1
Biorecognition Element Options
The key element in a biosensor is the biorecognition element, since it determines both selectivity and
speciﬁcity and thus directly impacts accuracy and sensitivity of measurements. Other concerns when
designing a biosensor are: the commercial availability of the biorecognition elements, whether or not
they can be bought or have to be specially produced for the purpose. How to make the design as
simple as possible and in the case of a small analyte and how to achieve high enough signal to ensure
a suﬃcient output. In this project, the goal is to keep the speciﬁcity and selectivity high, and thereby
only respond to the analyte of interest without being inﬂuenced by other interfering molecules. Since
the fungi Alternaria spp. are able to produce a lot of diﬀerent mycotoxins besides AOH, it is important
to choose a biorecognition element that can distinguish between them, to ensure that measurement
of the analyte of interest is as accurate as possible. Some mycotoxins are structurally very similar to
AOH, namely AME, ALT and altenuisol (AS) since they only diﬀer by a few functional groups, as can
be seen in Figure 3.
Page 7 of 151
==PAGE:13==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
Figure 3
Toxins structurally similar to AOH. The toxins alternariol monoethyl ether (AME), altenuene (ALT)
and altenuisol (AS) shown together with alternariol (AOH) to see the structural similarity between them. Assumed
carbon numbers are included on all molecules. Picture adapted from Escrivá et al. [4].
To this analyte AOH, three diﬀerent kind of biorecognition elements have been considered for the
biorecognition, namely a receptor, an enzyme or an antibody.
Estrogen Receptor
Receptors are used everywhere in cellular systems. They are proteins which recognize and bind ligands,
typically small compounds. The binding between them initiates conformational change to the receptor
and typically leads to other biochemical reactions, also called signal transduction. It has been reported
that AOH can exhibit an estrogenic potential/activity and can bind to the human estrogen receptor
(ER) in both the α and β form, with a slight preference for the ER β. AOH can function as an agonist
towards the ER because it is structurally similar to certain estrogen-mimicking substances, as it is
a diphenolic compound. AOH can thereby mimic the estrogen steroid hormone estradiol (E2), and
trigger the estrogenic pathway. A study by L. Lehmann et al. 2006 showed that the binding of AOH
to the ER has a lower aﬃnity towards the receptor compared to E2, about 10,000-fold lower binding
to ER α and only 2500-fold lower binding to ER β. These results showed that AOH can function as
a pure ER agonist [1, 16], but that the sensitivity of such a biosensor would probably be quite low.
By using the ER as the biorecognition element in a biosensor, the presence of the AOH is detected
by it binding to it. The ER is normally present in a cell system, but since it has been possible to
isolate the ER and make it function properly, the design of a biosensor is simpler and faster, than
involving a whole cell on a transducer. One concern is that the ER has not been reported to have been
tested against the similar mycotoxins AME, ALT and AS. However, since the main factor is to have a
Page 8 of 151
==PAGE:14==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
diphenolic structure, the risk of binding aﬃnity for AME, ALT and AS is high. Theoretically, the ER
would not be speciﬁc enough for this biosensor design, since the speciﬁcity has not been experimentally
veriﬁed.
Enzyme Acetylcholinesterase
Historically, the enzyme Acetylcholinesterase (AChE) has widely been used to detect among others
nerve agents, insecticides, mycotoxins and drugs. It has been reported that AOH is a competitive
inhibitor towards the enzyme and thereby can decrease its activity depending on the concentration,
thereby, reducing the conversion of the substrate acetylcholine [18]. To use AChE as the biorecognition
element in a biosensor, the enzyme has to be connected with another enzyme that can convert the
product from AChE since it cannot be detected on its own. A possible helper enzyme could be Choline
oxidase (ChOX), which converts choline and oxygen to betaine and hydrogen peroxide respectively,
of which hydrogen peroxide could be detected electrochemically, as seen in the reaction of Equation 1
[19]
Acetylcholine AChE
−−−−→
H2O
Choline + Acetic acid →Choline ChOX
−−−−→
O2
Betaine + H2O2
(1)
Diﬀerent designs with the two enzymes AChE and ChOX could be made by using amperometry
as the electrochemical detection technique.
One design could detect the H2O2 production, either
with or without the use of Prussian Blue depending on what potential to detect at. Another design
would use a mediator for the detection, which is connected to the ChOX enzyme. When the enzyme
oxidizes choline, the oxidized form of the mediator is concomitantly reduced, and then reoxidized on
the electrode. See both designs in Appendix A.3 Design Setups with the Enzyme Acetylcholinesterase
and Choline Oxidase, Figure 42. One thing to remember is to choose a potential where the risk of
interfering reactions/compounds is low. The same issue occurs with this biorecognition element as
for the ER, because AChE has not been reported to have been tested against the similar mycotoxins
AME, ALT and AS. The risk is considered large, since their core molecular structure is the same and
all have hydroxyl groups. Theoretically, AChE would not be speciﬁc enough for this biosensor design,
since the speciﬁcity has not been experimentally veriﬁed.
Monoclonal Antibody
Elements from our own immune system are often used in order to protect us humans. Antibodies
are used widely in medical diagnostics, in immunodiagnostic techniques such as ELISA, for therapy
to treat disease and for biomedical research. They are used because of the high speciﬁcity towards
one compound called an antigen. It has been reported that the monoclonal antibody (mAb) 4G4
(subtype IgG2B) has a high aﬃnity against AOH and can recognize free analytes. It was reported in
an enzyme immunoassay that the mAB had a mean detection limit (IC20) of 35 +/- 6.9 pg/mL and a
mean IC50 of 108 pg/mL, with a quasilinear measurement range of 20-300 pg/mL. This actually makes
this method equally sensitive as the HPLC-MS/MS method when using stable isotopes [3]. Another
article reported three mAb strains that also speciﬁcally recognizes AOH, namely 8A7, 10B3 and 1D1,
where only the IC50 was measured for 1D1 and was reported as 4.2 ng/mL [20].
Page 9 of 151
==PAGE:15==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
By using an antibody as the biorecognition element in a biosensor, the presence of the AOH is detected
by its aﬃnity. Since the other Alternaria mycotoxins are structurally very similar, there have been
reports on cross reaction. In the case of mAb 4G4, it has a weak cross reaction with AME at 0.9%
determined by an enzyme immunoassay, but none with ALT or TeA [3]. In the case of mAb 1D1, it
was reported to have no cross reaction with AME, but has not been tested against ALT [20]. None of
the two antibodies have been tested against AS.
Due to the high speciﬁcity of the antibody as compared to the enzymatic reactions and receptors, the
antibody was selected as the preferred biorecognition element for further exploration.
2.2.2
Antibody as the Chosen Biorecognition Element
Antibodies (Ab), also known as immunoglobulins, are deﬁned as large proteins (150 kDa, size of 10
nm) that are shaped like a Y. They are deﬁned as host proteins that are produced and used by the
immune system as a response to intruders [21, 22]. These intruders that have the potential of harming
the body are called antigens and include pathogenic organisms like viruses, bacteria, fungi, and toxic
compounds like mycotoxins. What makes this strategy so eﬀective is that the production of antibodies
is very selective towards a speciﬁc antigen, thereby the intruder can be identiﬁed in the body for the
immune system and the T-cells or macrophages can destroy it. Antibodies are produced by B-cells and
are composed of two identical heavy chains and two identical light chains, which both have constant
and variable regions. It is these combined (heavy and light) variable regions that form the two speciﬁc
antigen-binding sites [22, 23]. There are ﬁve antibody isotypes divided into three groups in mammalian
cells, dimer IgA, pentamer IgM and monomer IgD, IgE and IgG, where the IgG is the most common
and represent approximately 75% of the antibodies [22].
The speciﬁcity of the antibody and the ability to produce and isolate them have opened the opportunity
for high speciﬁc tools that can be used in research and diagnostic applications. Antibodies can be
produced both as monoclonal and polyclonal depending on the production method. In general, the
production involves injections of the antigen into animals to evoke and thereby produce a high level
of antigen-speciﬁc antibodies in the bloodstream. When producing polyclonal antibodies, the blood
containing the antibodies is collected from the animal after several weeks of regular antigen injections.
After which the blood is separated into blood cells and plasma, which contains the antibodies and other
proteins. Finally, the plasma is heated to 65°C to destroy the contaminating proteins and isolate the
antibodies. Monoclonal antibodies can be produced in two ways either by the Hybridoma technology
or Recombinant technology. In the Hybridoma technology, antibody producing cells from the spleen
are isolated from an animal and fused with myeloma cells creating a hybridoma. The hybridoma
cells are then observed and the ones producing the correct antibodies are selected, grown and the
antibodies are harvested from the supernatant. In the Recombinant technology, the gene sequences of
the variable regions (heavy and light chains) are isolated and inserted into a plasmid system, which is
then transferred into mammalian cells like Chinese Hamster Ovary (CHO) and later puriﬁed [22]. No
matter what the choice of production is, it is important to make a characterization of the produced
antibody, which includes screening, where the antigen-binding antibody is identiﬁed, titering, where
the antibody concentration and assay titer are measured, and ﬁnally isotyping, where the class and
Page 10 of 151
==PAGE:16==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
subclass of the antibody are clariﬁed [21]. The antigen-antibody aﬃnity reaction can be aﬀected by
several factors, like temperature and pH. The optimal temperature depends on the chemical structure
of the epitope and which type of bonds that are involved in the interaction. The pH optimum for the
complex is more precisely between 6.5 to 8.4. If the pH is under or above these values, the complex
is heavily inhibited as, under extreme pH conditions, the antibody can have conformational changes
[24].
In an immunoassay as well as in an immunobiosensor, the fundamental factors that aﬀect the quality
are the aﬃnity and the speciﬁcity of the antibody and these properties depend on the structure of
the antigen [20].
When producing the speciﬁc antibody needed to detect the analyte of interest,
the analyte has to activate an immune response, but sometimes a small molecule like AOH cannot.
AOH can, however, form a hapten (<1 kDa), that is deﬁned as an incomplete antigen and has the
ability to form covalent bonds to a large carrier molecule, like a protein and thereby elicit an immune
response. Only when the small molecule and the carrier molecule are fused together, they can elicit
an immune response [22, 25, 26].
A hapten can be formed by a reaction between AOH and ethyl
4-bromobutyrate, which forms a butyrate spacer arm (lenght of 4 carbons) or between AOH and
formaldehyde. Depending on where the reaction occurs on the analyte, diﬀerent parts of the analyte
are exposed and diﬀerent antibodies can be produced [3, 20]. The Alternaria mycotoxins are very alike
in their core structure, but they are slightly diﬀerent due to the location of the functional groups. AOH
has three hydroxyl groups and one methyl group located on C3, C9, C11 and C1 respectively (see
Figure 3). Comparing AOH to AME and ALT, the hydroxyl group on C9 (carbon number in red in
Figure 3) of AME and ALT is methylated (-OCH3), where the two hydroxyl groups on C3 and C11 on
both are maintained. To produce a speciﬁc antibody for AOH, it has been suggested that the carrier
protein should be bound to either the hydroxyl group at C3 or C11, so the hydroxyl group on C9 is
exposed (see Figure 3). However, with this strategy there could be a cross reaction with the toxin AS,
since the toxin has a hydroxyl group on C9 [3, 20]. The toxin AS has not been reported to be produced
in high concentrations, like AME and ALT, by the fungi according to the research. Therefore, cross
reaction with AS may not cause a signiﬁcant error as with a cross reaction with AME or ALT.
The only factor that is signiﬁcant for the biosensor design is that the antibody is available and is
preferably commercially available. The described antibodies, mAb 4G4 (subtype IgG2B) and mAb
1D1, are not commercially available and can therefore not be bought for this project. However, two
monoclonal Anti-Alternariol antibodies have been found, both of which are produced in mice and have
reported functionality in ELISA assays and both produced in 2019. One antibody is sold from the
ﬁrm Creative Diagnostics [27], while the other is sold from the ﬁrm Abvigen Inc [28]. These antibodies
can both be used in this project.
2.2.3
Analytical Approach
In general when designing a biosensor, there are label- and label-free detection approaches. The label-
free techniques are Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy (EIS), where the change in impedance
is generated by the analyte binding to the antibody, and Surface Plasmon Resonance (SPR) an op-
tical technique that detects the change in angle using polarized incident light. The label approaches
Page 11 of 151
==PAGE:17==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
comprise colorimetric sensing, ﬂuorescence sensing or electrochemical techniques like amperometry
or voltammetry, that measures an electrochemical response [22]. In this project, both the label-free
technique Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy and labeled techniques amperometry and cyclic
voltammetry were chosen.
The method Enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay (ELISA) is used to test the available antibodies and
the function of the chosen format of the immunoassay. ELISA is regarded as one of the most sensitive
immunoassay platforms [29] and is good for an initial start. It was used to evaluate the compatibility
of commercially bought antibody with the antigen, but also to test the optimal concentrations and
dilutions needed of all the elements used, for instance the antibody and tracer (analyte with enzyme
label). Usually, immunobiosensors are heterogeneous, which means that either the analyte or antibody
is immobilised on the electrode. They can be direct or indirect, which both have their advantages
and disadvantages. The format of the immunoassay can be both non-competitive and competitive
depending on which design is achievable. The non-competitive method could be a sandwich assay,
where one antibody is immobilised on the surface, the analyte attaches to it and then another antibody
with a label can bind to the analyte. The competitive method could be either with the analyte or
the antibody immobilised on the surface. With the analyte on the surface, the labelled antibodies are
either bound to the free analytes provided by the sample or to the analytes on the surface. With the
antibody on the surface, the analyte in the sample and the labelled analyte (tracer) competes for a
place on the antibody. In either case, the higher the analyte concentration in the sample, the lower is
the measured signal output [22, 30].
Since AOH is a very small molecule and needs a carrier protein to elicit an immune response, the chance
of it having more than one binding site towards the antibody is very low. Thereby, the sandwich assay
is not applicable here. However, the competitive method with the immobilised antibody could be
suitable since the exact binding site on the antigen has not yet been veriﬁed, which could cause issues
if the antigen was immobilised instead. This general method is shown in Figure 4 with both the
principle of ELISA and the principle of an electrochemical immunobiosensor. Both principles have
the same core where the antibodies are immobilized on a surface either in the bottom of a 96-well
plate or on an electrode, and the analyte present in the sample is competing with a tracer to get
a spot on the antibodies. The concentration of the tracer is known and theoretically, if there is a
high concentration of the analyte in the sample, the tracer constitutes a lower amount of the overall
concentration in the reaction mixture. Thereby, the tracer gets fewer spots on the antibodies. In the
principle of ELISA, the enzyme is converting a substrate into a product which changes the colour of
the ﬂuid, that can be detected by absorbance. If the level of analyte in the sample is high, the number
of enzymes (tracer) are low, thereby also the colour change will occur more slowly, and therefore the
colour after a predetermined time is lighter correlating with a low absorbance. The principle of an
electrochemical immunobiosensor is the same as ELISA, where an enzyme is converting a substrate
into a product.
However, instead of measuring the colour change, it is based on electrochemical
detection of the end-product on the electrode.
Page 12 of 151
==PAGE:18==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
Figure 4
The competitive analytical approach. The analytical approach shown both with the principle of ELISA
and the principle of an electrochemical immunobiosensor. Theoretical picture made with Biorender.com.
2.3
Biosensor Construction and Function
2.3.1
Immobilization of the Antibody and Biosensor Setup
When designing a biosensor, the crucial parts for its performance are the orientation and the at-
tachment of the immobilized biorecognition element on the electrode [31]. However, depending on
the biorecognition element e.g enzyme or antibody, the distance between it and the electrode and the
distance between the biorecognition elements also have an inﬂuence. It is important that the biorecog-
nition element is at the right distance from the electrode and from the other biorecognition elements,
so that the measured molecule can reach the electrode and be detected. Therefore, the immobilized
molecules should not be too dense. To make a great biosensor all of these parts have to be optimized
and tested.
When designing an immunobiosensor, the sensitivity is highly dependent on the presentation of the
chosen antibody, and the best orientation is to expose the antigen binding sites towards the solution
containing the analyte and avoiding not covering them up. Antibodies have structurally two diﬀerent
ends, a Fab-end that contains the two antigen binding regions and a Fc-end, which is the crystallized
Page 13 of 151
==PAGE:19==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
and constant region. Like any other protein, antibodies are one long chain of residues and when folded,
the hydrophobic amino acids are internal, creating disulﬁde bridges and the hydrophilic amino acids are
external on the surface, exposing some functional groups including carboxylic, amino and hydroxylic
groups [31]. The goal is to achieve the right orientation of all the antibodies on the electrode, of which
the ideal is that the Fc of the antibody is faced towards the surface, also called "end-on".
Antibodies can be immobilised in various ways which both include random and speciﬁc orientation.
In general, there are three types of immobilization: adsorption, covalent coupling and aﬃnity.
• Adsorption is the simplest way of antibody attachment, but it is also uncontrolled and im-
mobilise the antibodies randomly. Adsorption occurs either by electrostatic interactions or hy-
drophobic interactions. A good example are microtiter plates, which utilise polymers to increase
the hydrophilicity and thereby binding antibody to the surface. One method used is the forma-
tion of self-assembled monolayers (SAM), where the goal is to either modify the surface chemistry
to enhance antibody adsorption or to form functional groups to use in a covalent attachment.
SAMs are usually created by molecules, which contain active functional groups in both ends [31].
• Covalent coupling is considered to ensure a robust immobilization combined with high density
and in some cases speciﬁc orientation and site-directed immobilisation. The covalent coupling
can be achieved through interactions by functional groups like amino, carboxylic, thiol or gly-
can moieties. Amino and carboxylic groups are essentially ubiquitous in an antibody and are
very common on the surface, thus making site-directed immobilization diﬃcult. This coupling
is normally made by the method N-ethyl-N-(3-dimethylaminopropyl) carbodiimide (EDC)/N-
hydroxysuccinimide (NHS) and results in a robust amine bond formation. Since the antibody
has disulﬁde bridges in the hinge region, these can be reduced by TCEP or 2-MEA to reactive
thiol groups and make the immobilisation very speciﬁc. However, this reaction disrupts the
conformation of the antibody and divides it into two. The antibody is attached "side-on" on the
surface and can actually directly interact with a gold electrode. The coupling can also be made
by the glycan moiety on the Fc region through periodate oxidation [31].
• Aﬃnity is considered as an alternative and favourable immobilisation technique to promote a
speciﬁc orientation of the antibody. Some of the methods are using short nucleotide sequences
or Fc-binding aptamers and peptides for the attachment. Another strategy is to use proteins
(protein A and G) which can bind speciﬁcally to the Fc region of the antibody and thereby
achieve a speciﬁc orientation. Even though this method can create the desired orientation, there
are some issues using it as well. First of all, the method is reversible and it has been reported
that the protein can bind to Fab region in some cases and second, it has also been proved that
the proteins are not applicable to all antibodies. Another aﬃnity method is using the interaction
between biotin and avidin/streptavidin. Here the antibody is conjugated with biotin that can
bind to avidin/streptavidin, which is connected to the electrode and thereby form a speciﬁc
orientation [22, 31].
In this project, it is not feasible to test diﬀerent immobilization methods to determine which one is
the best, since the goal is to make a robust model that can be used as proof of concept. The only
Page 14 of 151
==PAGE:20==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
methods that can meet the criteria at this point are covalent coupling by the EDC/NHS reaction or
aﬃnity with protein A/G, since the available Anti-Alternariol antibody is not fused with biotin. The
covalent coupling by the EDC/NHS could form the wrong orientation as there are many amino groups
on the antibody other than those on the Fc end, whereas aﬃnity coupling with protein A/G could
be preferable since it speciﬁcally binds to the Fc region of the antibody. Using ELISA the antibody
is attached randomly to the well by absorption or physisorption. This is also the case when using
protein G to improve the orientation of the antibody, since the protein can attach randomly to the
well likewise by absorption. To immobilise the antibody on a gold electrode the two methods covalent
coupling and aﬃnity should be combined. Protein G can be attached to the gold electrode by a linker
like 11-mercaptoundecanoic acid (MUA) or 3-mercaptopropionic acid (MPA) which facilitate the link
between the gold electrode and protein G that is bound to the antibody (see Figure 5). Since the
linker molecules (MUA and MPA) have a thiol group (-SH), they can bind to the gold electrode.
Additionally, both molecules (MUA and MPA) have a carboxylic acid group at the opposite end that
can bind to protein G through the protein’s amine groups via an EDC/NHS reaction. Thereby, both
the aﬃnity method with protein G and covalent coupling by the EDC/NHS are used in the biosensor
design for this project.
Of utmost importance for the sensitivity of the biosensor is the distance
between the antibodies to allow for binding site availability. If protein G is not a part of the setup,
this can be solved by adding molecules, like 6-mercapto-1-hexanol (MH) with a hydroxyl group (-OH)
at the end, to the mixture along with the MUA molecules to allow for spacing between the MUA
molecules, so that the antibodies are more spread out and there is room for the tracer and analyte.
When using protein G, the proteins form a layer where the antibodies can attach. The layer of protein
G prevents dense packaging and ensures that the antibodies are still spread out. Therefore, when
using protein G only one type of linker like MPA should be used. MPA is shorter than MUH, which
ensures that the enzyme reaction is closer to the electrode surface.
Figure 5
Theoretical biosensor design. A. MPA is attached to the gold electrode and can through the EDC/NHS
reaction connect to protein G via the carboxyl acid group on MPA and the amine group on protein G. B. The biosensor
design, with the dense protein G layer linked by MPA, where there is space for the antibodies and analyte/tracer. The
overall construction of the biosensor with the immobilisation, orientation and competitive assay. Theoretical picture
made with Biorender.com.
Page 15 of 151
==PAGE:21==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
2.3.2
Tracer Choice
The labelled analyte used in the competitive analytical design is also called a tracer and is deﬁned
as the analyte of interest conjugated with a label, such as an enzyme. In this project, the tracer is
the analyte AOH conjugated with the enzyme Horseradish peroxidase (HRP), since the enzyme has
a fast catalytic rate, is very stable, cheap and is acknowledged as an industrial enzyme with great
potential [32, 33]. The analyte and the enzyme have to be conjugated by a chemical reaction, but what
is important is the location of the enzyme on the analyte. The enzyme should be placed such, that the
binding site towards the antibody is not blocked. Thereby, the ideal location of the enzyme should be
at the same place as where the carrier protein was connected during the antibody production, as the
same part of the analyte would then be exposed. Occasionally, the tracer can be bought commercially,
which is easier than doing the conjugation yourself, but there can still be complications. One problem
can occur if the antibody and the tracer are produced/bought from diﬀerent ﬁrms, since the binding
site then can vary or mismatch and thereby disrupt the analysis.
Two monoclonal Anti-Alternariol antibodies have been found but from two diﬀerent ﬁrms Creative
Diagnostics and Abvigen Inc. However, at the ﬁrm Abvigen Inc, a possible tracer, AOH-HRP, can be
bought, where the ﬁrm proposes a match between the Anti-Alternariol antibody ABN3459 and the
tracer AOH-HRP ABGH3459 [34]. It is reported by the ﬁrm that the antibody has a cross reaction
with AME at 5 ppb determined by ELISA, but that the antibody have not been tested against ALT
or AS. The Anti-Alternariol antibody ABN3459 from Abvigen Inc is therefore chosen for the biosensor
design.
The HRP Enzyme
HRP is ubiquitous in nature and has been found in animals, fungi, bacteria and plants. However,
the most used enzyme is known to be isolated from horseradish roots with a molecular weight of
approximately 44 kDa. It has at least seven isozymes depending on the isoelectric point and is deﬁned
as a glycoprotein as 18% of its structure contains carbohydrates. HRP is reported to be most stable
in the pH range of 5.0 to 9.0, with a pH optimum between 6.0 to 6.5. HRP is in the enzyme group
peroxidases which are classiﬁed as hydrogen peroxide degrading enzymes that in parallel oxidise a
wide range of both phenolic and non-phenolic substrates (R-H). The reaction of the enzyme is shown
in the Equation 2 [33, 35].
2R −H + H2O2 →2R◦+ 2H2O
(2)
HRP is widely used as a label in various applications, including ELISA, since it is small, stable and
has a low non-speciﬁc binding due to its glycosylation. However, the enzyme can also be inhibited
by various compounds: p–aminobenzoic acid, cyanide, sodium azide, dichromate, vanadate, hydroxy-
lamine, L–cystine, ethylenethiourea, sulﬁde, and the minerals/heavy metals Ni+2, Cd+2, Cu+2, Co+2,
Fe+3, Pb+2 and Mn+2 [35, 36].
HRP can be used as the label in both ELISA and the electrochemical detection and even use the same
substrate. In literature, the exact composition of the substrate solution may slightly vary but one
example, which is used in this project, is composed of 3,3’,5,5’-tetramethylbenzidine (TMB) combined
Page 16 of 151
==PAGE:22==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
with hydrogen peroxide (H2O2) in citrate buﬀer (pH 5.6). When using this solution in electrochemical
applications a counter electrolyte solution has to be added such as 0.1 M KCl. The detection is based
on the redox reaction, and at the beginning the TMB is in a reduced form and colourless, where
the hydrogen peroxide is in an oxidized form. When the substrate solution (TMB, H2O2 and citrate
buﬀer) is mixed with the enzyme, the enzyme converts the hydrogen peroxide to water (see Equation
2), thereby reducing it, and at the same time, the TMB is oxidised and turning blue. Since the product
TMBox turns blue it can be determined by spectrophotometric detection, but due to it being oxidised,
it is also electrochemically detectable, as the TMB can be reduced at the working electrode [37].
2.3.3
Electrochemical Detection and Diﬀerent Detection Techniques
An electrochemical biosensor is basically a chemical sensor, where the recognition utilises a biochem-
ical mechanism on an electrochemical transducer. It is comprised of two components; a recognition
element i.e a biological receptor and a transduction element i.e a physico-chemical transducer also
called a detector, a sensor or an electrode. The recognition system converts the biochemical signal
from the receptor domain into an electronic signal, which is transferred to the electrical domain by the
transducer. The International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry (IUPAC) has deﬁned a biosensor
as a self-contained integrated receptor-transducer device composed of a biological recognition element
i.e bioreceptor in direct spatial contact with a transduction element, which is providing quantita-
tive and semi-quantitative analytical information [22, 38]. The electrochemical signal transduction is
dependent on the transduction element and the transduction mechanism.
The Basics of the Electrochemical Techniques
In general, the study and application of chemical reactions takes place at the interface between an
electron conductor (electrode) and an ionic conductor (electrolyte solution). The reactions are pre-
dominantly driven by an applied potential and generate a current response. The principle is to measure
the transfer of electrons, which is equal to the generated current and the ﬂow of charge per time unit.
The current is a result of the electrochemical oxidation (donating electrons) or reduction (accepting
electrons) of electroactive species.
In a biosensor setup, there are three electrodes: a working electrode (WE), a reference electrode (RE)
and a counter electrode (CE), which are connected to an electrochemical instrument called a poten-
tiostat and are enclosed by an electrolyte solution, often a salt buﬀer. WE represents the construction
of the biosensor, where the biorecognition element is immobilised and the analyte concentration is de-
tected either directly or indirectly at a given potential. On the WE the chemical species can undergo
either an oxidation or a reduction reaction. The more negative the electrode potential is, the higher
is the electron energy of the electrode, which increases the driving force for reduction of a species.
The opposite applies to more positive potential, leading to oxidation. The CE, also called an auxil-
iary electrode, functions as the opposite of the WE and has complementary electrochemical reactions.
Thereby, if a species is oxidized at the WE any arbitrary ions or molecules are reduced on the CE.
The CE is made signiﬁcantly larger (around 10 times) than the WE to maximise the surface area for
the reaction and thereby avoid the CE becoming the bottle neck in the circuit. The potential drives
the oxidation/reduction, and the potential of the CE has to be lower than the WE when oxidation
Page 17 of 151
==PAGE:23==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
takes place at the WE and higher when reduction takes place on the WE. Both the WE and the CE
have to be made of inert material, to ensure that the material does not interfere and participate in
the electrochemical reaction. The WE can be made of diﬀerent materials either metals like Au or
Pt, carbon or graphene, or carbon-like-graphite or conducting polymers. However, the metal-like-Au
and carbon are most commonly used and especially metal is applied when the biosensor is used more
than once. The RE has its own well-deﬁned potential and should be kept very constant to maintain
biosensor functionality. The RE adjusts the potential of the WE and the potential of the WE is always
measured in respect to the RE. The RE uses the universal scale made from the standard hydrogen
electrode (SHE), which is used to calculate the potential and represents the standard electrode poten-
tial and the value of 0 V. The potentiostat allows adjustment of the potential at the WE and records
the electrons accepted or donated by the WE and measures the current. The CE balances the circuit,
where the electrolyte solution creates a ﬂow of ions and closes the electric circuit. All these parts are
packed together in the same unit and are usually of small sizes [22, 38].
The mass transfer of the species is very important for the measurement, since it can inﬂuence the
distribution towards the electrodes. The mass transfer is deﬁned by the Nernst-Planck equation, see
Equation 3, where the ﬁrst term is diﬀusion, second term is migration and third term is convection
[22].
Ji(x) = −Di
∂Ci(x)
∂x
−ziF
RT DiCi
∂φ(x)
∂x
+ Civ(x)
(3)
Diﬀusion is movement of species inﬂuenced by a gradient of a chemical potential. Migration is the
movement of charged species inﬂuenced by a gradient of an electrical potential. Convection is either
occurring naturally due to a density gradient, which can be caused by a concentration gradient or
temperature gradient, or by force due to a pressure gradient.
In a biosensor, the transfer modes
migration and convection should be eliminated or minimized or be used as a beneﬁt for the biosensor.
Where migration should be minimized as much as possible, convection can be controlled and thereby
be an advantage. Controlled convection is used in Flow-injection analysis (FIA) or in Lab-on-a-chip
(LOC) devices [22].
Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy
The principle behind Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy is to measure the impedance in a
given system, and thereby record the change in, for instance, the charge transfer resistance when a
new element is binding to the surface of an electrode. In this technique, a redox reaction is connected
to the working electrode and the presence of other elements is determined by the hindering of the
reaction and thereby increasing the charge transfer resistance to the redox reaction at the electrode
surface. In general, the electrode is a part of a circuit and the output is represented by a Nyquist plot
[39] (see Figure 6), where the semicircle represents the charge transfer resistance (Rct), that changes
in diameter in relation to the resistance detected in the system.
Page 18 of 151
==PAGE:24==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
Figure 6
Impedance technique in theory. Showing a potential circuit model and output (the Nyquist plot), where
a possible redox reaction could be with the compound ferricyanide or hexacyanoferrate II/III. Figure information: Rs =
Resistance, Rct = Charge transfer resistance, ZW = Warburg impedance and mass transfer due to diﬀusion of reactants
and Cdl = Capacitance. Picture made with BioRender.com.
Impedance Spectroscopy can be used when modifying the biosensor setup, as each part has to be
established one part at a time. Using impedance measurements on the electrode, each attachment can
be attempted to be detected and validated before attaching the next element, which can contribute
to ensure that the biosensor setup is modiﬁed in the right way and order. For each attachment step,
the resistance of the system should theoretically increase, meaning that the diameter of the semicircle
increases. However, this is only valid if the immobilized molecule has negative charge or causes a
signiﬁcant steric hindrance for access of the redox species to the electrode surface. It has to be said
that outputs can diﬀer from the theory, as each biological element can aﬀect the resistance diﬀerently,
as it is not only the size of the element that counts, but also the presence of negatively and positively
charged groups.
Amperometry
In amperometry, the applied DC potential is kept constant while the current is measured as a function
of time with diﬀusion as the mass transfer mode. When a potential is poised at the WE, an initial
current peak appears charging the double layer capacitor. After the charging a decay of the capacitive
current takes place. The exponential decay of the capacitive current is fast and the current approaches
0 after a certain amount of time (see Figure 7). The residual remaining current that forms the so called
baseline is represented by ionic current, i.e. movement of ions in the electrolyte. The Faradaic current,
that represents the ﬂow of electrons or in other words is the current caused by the electrochemical
oxidation/reduction reaction of the molecules, is the measurement of interest, where the unwanted
side eﬀect is the capacitive current that is caused by ions accumulating close to the electrode. To
get a quantitative detection, the Faraday current is customarily determined after 5 time constants
has passed. This is done to ensure that the capacitive current does not inﬂuence the measurements
[22, 40] (see Figure 7). When the concentration of the electroactive species is increased the current
is also increased and vice versa (see Figure 7). The behaviour shown on Figure 7 is only when the
electroactive species is already present when the potential is applied.
Page 19 of 151
==PAGE:25==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
Figure 7
Amperometry output for an oxidation. Output when using the Amperometry method showed both
for the capacitive current (red), the Faraday current (blue) and for increasing concentration of electroactive species.
For an output of reduction the curve is simply reversed and have a negative current. Theoretical picture made with
Biorender.com.
If the substrate are being pipetted in and the electroactive product is formed, i.e. there is no elec-
troactive species present prior to that, a ‘peak’ is obtained due to the transient appearance of the
electroactive species.
Meaning that there is no oxidation or reduction ongoing prior to that and
thereby no Faradaic current.
Voltammetry
In voltammetry as opposed to amperometry, the potential is changed continuously during the mea-
surement. The goal is still to measure the current caused by the electrochemical redox reaction of
the molecules. There are diﬀerent types of voltammetry: linear sweep voltammetry (LSV), cyclic
voltammetry (CV) and pulse voltammetry (PV). In LSV, the potential goes from low to high or vice
versa and forms a straight line, but as time passes it results in an i-E curve. When the potential
increases the current also increases, but only to a certain value, where the current decreases due to
mass transfer limitation (diﬀusion limitation) [22, 40, 41] (see Figure 8A and 8B). CV is very similar
to LSV, but is dependent on a switching potential. The forward sweep is identical to LSV, but at
a certain point the current is reversed and is swept back to the starting point, creating a “duck”-
shaped voltammogram (see Figure 8C). In CV, the performance diﬀers depending on the dimensions
of the electrode. When the electrode is smaller, there is the advantage that there is no mass transfer
limitation, the capacitive current is almost eliminated and small volumes can be used for the measure-
ment. In PV, one technique uses the principle where the potential is increased for each pulse (short
potential steps) and where the current is measured at a predetermined point during a pulse. The
method can create conditions where the capacitive current has a minimal eﬀect and where diﬀerent
oxidised molecules can be separated in the output. There are diﬀerent pulse voltammetry techniques
including normal pulse voltammetry (NPV), diﬀerential pulse voltammetry (DPV) and square wave
voltammetry (SWV). Each have their diﬀerences, advantages and disadvantages. NPV has a linear
baseline, where the potential is increased for each pulse and the current is measured at the end of
each pulse. DPV has a linear sweep, a constant pulse and the current is measured twice during each
Page 20 of 151
==PAGE:26==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
pulse period and this is plotted in peaks. SWV is very similar to DPV but has a reversed pulse. All
the techniques, principles and outcomes can be seen in Appendix A.4 Pulse Voltammetry Methods -
NPV, DPV and SWV, Figure 43 [22, 40, 41].
Figure 8
Voltammetry output. A. Shows the output for Linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) in the beginning, where
the potential is increasing. B. Shows the i-E curve for Linear sweep voltammetry (LSV), where both the capacitive
current and the Faraday current are marked together with the mass transfer limitation. C. Shows the output for Cyclic
voltammetry (CV), with ﬁrst the reduction and after the oxidation. Theoretical picture made with Biorender.com.
Which Detection Technique for What
In general, amperometry, linear sweep voltammetry and cyclic voltammetry are good methods when
working with proof of concept projects as they have high eﬃciency and are simple to use. They can
all be measured in stagnant solutions, but the outcome is analysed diﬀerently. To create a better
laboratory model or a biosensor that could be used in the industry, the methods Pulse voltammetry
(Normal, Diﬃcult, or Square) in stagnant solutions or Amperometry in a Flow-injection analysis (FIA)
would be preferable. In this project, impedance spectroscopy is used to characterise the biosensor
modiﬁcation together with cyclic voltammetry, since the techniques can test-control the electrode
cleaning process, the modiﬁcation and are able to justify whether the biosensor is constructed properly.
Amperometry is chosen to prove the concept, since the method can show how the biosensor performs
and functions when detecting the formed product with and without analyte present in a batch system.
If optimized, any pulse voltammetry method and amperometry could be used for the industrial design,
but for it to be feasible, the system has to be in a ﬂow system, so that the peak detection can occur
without stopping the enzyme reaction, as it will destroy the biointerface.
2.3.4
Regeneration of the Biosensor with Protein G
When designing a biosensor it could be of future interest to be able to reuse the sensor multiple
times. This method is called regeneration and is in principle a dissociation method, where the antigen
(analyte and tracer) are removed from the antibody, so that the antibody once again is ready to
detect a new sample. For the regeneration diﬀerent strategies can be used either highly basic (pH
9.5-11) or highly acidic (pH 1.8-3.8) buﬀers, or buﬀers mixed with an organic solvent or enzymes like
pepsin. It has been reported that solutions like 0.1 M glycine/HCl, 0.04 M NaOH or 0.1 M NaOH were
suitable buﬀers for an eﬀective regeneration. A speciﬁc example is a 1% DMSO glycine/HCl buﬀer
Page 21 of 151
==PAGE:27==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
(pH 2.3) which was reported to have 97% dissociation eﬀect and retained the binding aﬃnity of the
antibody for 14 assays [42, 43]. Adding glycine to the acidic buﬀer solutions helps the separation and
also minimizes the damage to the biorecognition element. This is due to glycine being a zwitterion in
solution with both negative- and positive charged regions, therefore it can reduce the thermodynamic
favourability of the binding in the bound state of the analyte and biorecognition element, and thereby
help breaking the bond between them. Even though the glycine separates the biological elements in
the biosensor, it can also protect them from the harsh acidic environment caused by the regeneration,
since it can bind to their surface and stabilize them [44].
The regeneration is diﬀerent when using protein G, since the dissociation is not only happening on the
antigen-antibody complex, but also can happen between the protein G and the antibody, since protein
G temporarily can lose its binding aﬃnity towards the antibody in harsh environmental conditions,
like low pH [45, 46]. It has been reported that incomplete regeneration can occur with protein G,
since the binding between the Ig antibodies and the protein G are very strong and not all of the
bound antibodies are removed in the regeneration [47, 48]. It has been reported that the impedance
baselines are not identical after removal of the antibodies [46], which could be due to this incomplete
dissociation between the protein and the antibody, but it could also be due to protein-parts from
denatured antibodies or protein G. To ﬁnd the correct reason, the surface on the electrode should
be investigated both to see if any antibodies are still bound to the protein G and to see if other
biological elements are stuck on the electrode. This could be done by either looking at the surface
of the electrode to identify the biological elements, or by deﬁning which bonds there are between the
elements. Protein G is a cell wall protein in the Streptococcus bacteria and have a wide reactivity
proﬁle since it can bind to a range of antibodies except for Ig class IgM. Protein G is speciﬁc for
antibodies in the IgG group with high binding aﬃnity for human IgG, rat IgG2a and mouse IgG2a
[48]. One goal with protein G could therefore be to create a universal electrode, since it is possible
to regenerate the electrode with only protein G attached. Since protein G can bind to many diﬀerent
antibodies, it is therefore possible to only use one electrode to detect many diﬀerent compounds.
Protein G is then not only used to optimise the orientation, but can also be the universal layer on
the electrode when talking about reused biosensors. Another goal could be to use the strong aﬃnity
binding between the antibody and protein G to make an reusable electrode, where the antibody stays
on and only the bond between the analyte and tracer is regenerated. Such a design could be well
optimised and be very speciﬁc towards one analyte which could make the detection even better.
2.4
Product Sample - Tomato Product
To ensure that the detection is as sensitive and accurate as possible, it is important to know what
the sample contains other than the analyte of interest, to ensure that any noise in the output, or
compounds that may impair the performance of the biosensor, can be removed. It is also essential to
know if the analyte of interest sits in a matrix with other compounds or is freely suspended in the
sample, since this can aﬀect the detection.
Page 22 of 151
==PAGE:28==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
Moreover, the pH of the sample is an important factor to consider for the biological elements in the
biosensor and the concentration of the sample has to be in the range of detection. All of this can
determine if the detection needs a pre-step, like a pre-treatment or a pre-concentration regulation or
a pre-pH regulation.
2.4.1
Sample Content
The sample content is important to know as detailed as possible to determine which compounds that
could be a problem to the biosensor. Since the samples in this project are tomato and tomato products,
the contents are found for a raw tomato and for ﬁve diﬀerent products: peeled canned tomatoes, tomato
purée, tomato juice, tomato ketchup, and tomato soup (ready to eat/canned condensed), where the
compounds and their amounts are shown in Appendix A.5 Contents of Tomato and Diﬀerent Tomato
Products, Table 29, data from the databases Frida (DTU fooddatabase) [49] and MYFOODDATA
(Online database) [50]. In general, the contents are divided in vitamins, minerals including heavy
metals, carbohydrates, fats, amines, amino acids and organic acids, but not all of the compounds or
compound groups aﬀect the biosensor.
• One vitamin to be aware of is vitamin C/ascorbic acid since it can interfere and aﬀect the
biosensor negatively when working with enzymes. However, even though tomatoes have a content
of 13.7-15 mg vitamin C, this can be solved by detecting at a low positive potential or slightly
negative potential.
• Minerals including heavy metals can aﬀect the biological elements i.e. the antibody and the
enzyme, since they can bind to any available protein. They can thereby bind to the binding sites
of the antibodies and the active site on the enzymes which either can inhibit or block the process.
Especially, when tomatoes contain 6 out of 7 minerals/heavy metals reported to be inhibitory
for the HRP enzyme. The compound lycopene is put under this group even if it is categorised as
a carotenoid and is a natural pigment found in tomatoes. It can vary a lot in amount depending
on the product, since the concentration during the food process either decreases or increases
depending on the temperature used. Lycopene is reported to range from 2.5 to 200 mg per 100
g in raw tomato [50, 51]. Lycopene does not inﬂuence the biosensor or the biorecognition, since
it is not electrochemically active. However, it could have an eﬀect on the transducer by reducing
its reproducibility.
• Carbohydrates and fats can be a problem for the biosensor, since they can adhere to the
electrode surface and change the behaviour of the electrode. This is only a problem when the
electrode is reused, not if the electrode is disposable, i.e. for one-time-use.
• Amino acids are not considered to be a signiﬁcant problem, only cysteine can aﬀect the biosen-
sor negatively if gold is used as the electrode material. Cysteine can self-attach to the electrode
due to its thiol group (-SH) and maybe block the passageway for the electrochemical compound
towards the electrode. Some amino acids may be electroactive but as in the case for vitamin
C/ascorbic acid, they do not interfere when applying a slightly negative potential.
Page 23 of 151
==PAGE:29==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
When compounds in the sample have a negative eﬀect on the biological elements in the biosensor,
it also aﬀects the IC50 and the limit of detection (LOD) of the biosensor, which thereby aﬀects the
sensitivity and accuracy of the measurement.
2.4.2
Matrix, Handling and Preparation
In general, one of the biggest issues in food analysis are the eﬀects caused by the matrix interference.
The food matrix is often very complex and system speciﬁc, as every single biological matrix creates
diﬀerent challenges for each type of analytical approach. The matrix varies for each sample type and
it therefore demands diﬀerent preparation and management methodologies. The components present
in the matrix can aﬀect the result of the analysis, since they can inﬂuence the sensitivity, accuracy
and even the reproducibility, thereby, aﬀecting the response of the analysis, leading to inaccurate
quantiﬁcation and creating false positive and false negative results [52, 53].
To reduce the matrix eﬀect several preparation methods can be used like dilution or removal of the
interferences, where the unit operations centrifugation, ﬁltration and precipitation can clean-up the
sample. Using dilution as the preparation is by far the most common procedure, as it is the simplest
method that can easily be automated and integrated systematically. However, when using dilution as
the preparation strategy, the assay has to be highly sensitive, since the procedure as well as reducing
the interferences also reduces the concentration of the analyte of interest [53]. When deciding which
preparation method to use, it is important to know where the analyte is in the sample, and if it is
bound to other components. Since the current detection method for AOH is to extract the analyte
and measure the concentration by HPLC using methanol as the organic solvent [8, 54], alternariol
does not appear to be trapped in a matrix, but probably is dissolved freely in the sample. However,
it is a possibility that methanol acts as a solvent on the matrix particles and releases AOH to the
surrounding medium and isolates it. When using an HPLC, the sample has to be very clean to get
reliable results and this procedure could beneﬁt the detection in the biosensor as well. A pre-cleaning
in the preparation could make the biosensor more sensitive and more accurate, since most of the
matrix interferences are removed before the measurement on the electrode. Although the extraction
by an organic solvent is common and eﬀective, it is also highly toxic. The future goal is therefore to
try to avoid using solvents or ﬁnd another method which is more environmentally friendly.
The sample matrix and thereby the sample preparation varies from product to product mostly due to
the viscosity and particles, but the main goal is in any case to extract or isolate the analyte from the
rest of the sample matrix, lower the viscosity, and be able to measure the correct analyte concentration.
Therefore, preparing the product sample before injection by for example diluting it or centrifuging it
could be necessary, since the tomato product most likely contains many diﬀerent compounds and has a
thick consistency. This is done to ensure sensor-solution-contact, but one has to keep in mind that the
measurement has to be in the analytical range of the biosensor. One way of preparing the sample for
an antibody analytical method from tomato juice, tomato paste, tomato ketchup and whole tomatoes
are described below. The more simple the preparation is, the better for the industry, but it has to be
stated that these methods are not said to be the optimal choice of sample handling for an industrial
scale, as these methods are from scientiﬁc papers.
Page 24 of 151
==PAGE:30==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION
• Tomato juice is deﬁned as a liquid with low viscosity. Therefore, the sample preparation is
very simple and only needs a dilution with PBS (Phosphate buﬀered saline) before analysis [3].
Another way of preparing a liquid product like orange juice, is to ﬁrst centrifuge (13000 rpm, 5
min) the sample, adjust the pH of the supernatant to around 7 and dilute 1:1 with PBS solution
before analysis [55].
• Tomato sauce, with the same viscosity as applesauce, needs more preparation. First it is mixed
by magnetic stirring with PBS for several minutes. Afterwards an aliquot is centrifuged and the
supernatant is analysed, either directly or in a diluted form [3].
• Tomato paste and tomato ketchup have high viscosity and have the same preparation as the
tomato sauce except for one step i.e ﬁltration of the supernatant after a centrifugation. A portion
of the product is mixed with either methanol or PBS, whereafter it is pH adjusted and is set for
magnetic stirring. After that the mixture is centrifuged and the supernatant is either ﬁltered
(tomato paste) or not (tomato ketchup) whereafter the portion is mixed with distilled water
and extracted with ethyl acetate. The organic solvent portion is put under heat to evaporate
the solvent and subsequently the residue is dissolved with PBS. The solution is analysed either
directly or in a diluted form [3].
• Whole tomatoes are cut into small pieces and homogenised. The preparation is the same as
for the tomato paste, except for the centrifugation before the ﬁltration. The sample is analysed
either directly or in a diluted form [3].
The pH of the sample could have an inﬂuence on the elements in the biosensor and should therefore
be adjusted before entering. Since the pH of tomatoes lies between 4 to 5 [56], the product samples
are probably too acidic for the biological elements, since the pH optimum for the HRP enzyme and
for the antibody-antigen complex is between 6.0 to 6.5 and between 6.5 to 8.4 respectively. A test
with diﬀerent pH buﬀer solutions would be preferred. If the sample is too acidic, the binding strength
between the antibody and antigen could be weakened, which can be exploited for biosensor regeneration
using highly acidic environments of around pH 2 [22].
When using the method ELISA, the 96-well plate is rinsed and the rest of the dirt and other harmful
compounds are removed before adding the enzyme, but when using a ﬂow based system the biosensor
cannot be rinsed in the same way. One way to clean the sample and maybe minimize the manual
preparation is to use a supported liquid membrane (SLM). The SLM roughly separates the "dirt"
from the analyte of interest, because the analyte AOH can diﬀuse through the membrane where other
compounds cannot. The pKa is important for the application of a SLM, and requires that the analyte
of interest is in its neutral state (uncharged) [57]. AOH should be neutral (uncharged) at a pH below
7 [58] and to have the pH limit at 7 is perfect in this biosensor setup, since the enzyme HRP and
the antibody-antigen complex have optimum under 7, which enables the use of SLM for pre-cleaning.
However, some of the compounds i.e small compounds can go through the membrane as well like the
similar toxins to AOH and can aﬀect the biosensor negatively. For an industrial biosensor, a minimum
degree of cleanness of the sample should be tested experimentally, so that the measurements are as
easy as possible.
Page 25 of 151
==PAGE:31==
THEORY AND BACKGROUND INFORMATION

to know the maximum allowable concentration level from the European Commission, but also what
values to expect to ﬁnd if testing commercial products. From the EFSA Journal 2011 [1], diﬀerent
tomato products like tomato juice, tomato purée and tomato ketchup were tested for AOH in various
samples, which gives an interval of the AOH concentrations in each product.

MATERIALS AND METHODS
3
MATERIALS AND METHODS
3.1
The General Approach
In general, this project proceeds as ELISA experiments that forms the basic conditions for the exper-
iments with the electrochemical detection on an electrode. Performing the ELISA experiments ﬁrst,
many conditions can be determined like the optimal concentrations of the antibody and tracer, how the
matrix of the sample can inﬂuence the detection range e.g. IC50 and the LOD, and how a regeneration
works and where the solution cleaves. ELISA can provide the basic knowledge and understanding of
the biosensor setup before introducing the electrode and the world of electrochemical detection.
The chosen Anti-Alternariol antibody and tracer are new on the market and has not yet been re-
ported in any literature. Additionally, they are, together with the analyte alternariol, quite expensive.
Therefore, a good strategy is to ﬁrst build up the experiments and protocols with other biological
elements that are well established. This is also done to learn the methods and the theory, but it was
also done to form good experimental protocols before experimenting with the elements for alternariol.
The optimal strategy would be to ﬁnd an analyte very similar to alternariol, as the chance of them
preforming almost identically in the experiments are quite high. One analyte, with associated anti-
body and tracer, was already purchased in the department and is structurally similar to alternariol,
namely cortisol. Cortisol is deﬁned as a stress hormone and is secreted in urine, sweat and saliva by
humans and animals. Alternariol and cortisol have more in common than their similar structures,
since they can bind to receptors in the same category, namely steroid receptors [59]. Alternariol can
mimic the compound estradiol and can therefore bind to the estrogen receptor [1, 16], where cortisol
can bind to the glucocorticoid receptor [60] (see the compound structures for alternariol, estradiol
and cortisol in Figure 9). Cortisol is a ﬁne choice, since the analyte with related antibody and tracer
are well known, well optimized and have been reported in many scientiﬁc papers even in relation to
lab-on-a-chip experiments [61]. Therefore, the experiments with cortisol should give promising results.
Figure 9
The structures of alternariol, estradiol and cortisol. Shows the structural similarity between alternariol
(AOH), estradiol (E2) and cortisol (CS). It can be seen that the three compounds have the same core structure. Pictures
from Escrivá et al.[4] and online sources.
Page 27 of 151
==PAGE:33==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
The project starts with experiments with cortisol to get an understanding of how the protocol and
experiments works, and how to design the experiments in the best way possible, to ensure that when
the protocols have to be transferred to AOH, the experiments have the highest chance of success. When
it comes to the biosensor, the setup is the same for both analytes, since the detection is measured for
the end-product of the HRP enzyme reaction and is not deﬁned by a speciﬁc antibody or analyte.
3.2
Experimental Equipment and Materials
3.2.1
Materials
ELISA
A 96-well Maxisorp plate from Thermo Scientiﬁc was used to perform the ELISA tests. SealPlate
ﬁlm from Excel Scientiﬁc delivered by Sigma-Aldrich was used to cover the plate while incubating.
Tomato juice (Batch no. H3451:661425 used for cortisol experiment and Batch no. H4021:661425
used for alternariol experiments) bought at the supermarket from Rynkeby was used to perform the
matrix test. Two diﬀerent tomato juices had to be used, since the expiration date of the ﬁrst juice
passed between the experiments.
Electrochemical Detection
The AQ electrodes were made in the clean room at DTU Nanolab. A poly(methyl methacrylate)
(PMMA) holder with a printed circuit board (PCB) of copper with pogo pins made at the department
was used to hold the electrodes and connect the potentiostat.
3.2.2
Biological Elements and Chemicals
Anti-Cortisol monoclonal antibody (10-C30D, IgG2a), stock 1.2 mg/mL obtained from Fitzgerald
stored in aliquots at -20°C. Tracer (65-IC08, C-HRP), stock 1 mg/mL with Horseradish peroxidase
(HRP) obtained from Fitzgerald stored at 4°C. Cortisol, stock 10 mM in DMSO, obtained from Sigma-
Aldrich stored at 4°C. Anti-Alternariol monoclonal antibody (Anti-AOH AB, clone: BE329), stock 4.5
mg/mL, obtained from Abvigen Inc stored in aliquots at -20°C. Tracer (AOH-HRP) with Horseradish
peroxidase (HRP), stock 1.2 mg/mL, obtained from Abvigen Inc stored at -20°C for long term and 4°C
for short term. Alternariol, stock in DMSO, obtained as powder (5 mg) from Fermentek, Cfm Oskar
Tropitzsch GmbH, main stock 40 nM stored at -20°C and working stock 1 nM stored at 4°C. Protein
G (P4689 stock 1 mg/mL, P19459 stock 2 mg/mL) obtained from Sigma-Aldrich and Thermo Fisher
stored in aliquots at -20°C for long term and at 4°C for short term. It is proven by the department
that the diﬀerent protein G have the same eﬀect. The preparation of the biological elements are shown
in Appendix A.6 Preparation and Storage of Biological Elements, Reagents and Chemicals.
Analyte Solubility - Use of Diﬀerent Organic Compounds
When the analyte arrived, it came in powder form and had to be mixed with a liquid to be used
in the experiments. Often the analytes are soluble in diﬀerent organic compounds like DMSO, pure
ethanol, methanol and dimethylformanide (DMF). However, the solubility also determines the end-
concentration of the mixed solution.
Each organic solvent has its advantages and disadvantages.
DMSO can be used, but it has potential risks, since it is suspected of damaging reproduction and
Page 28 of 151
==PAGE:34==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
organs, and readily penetrate skin and may carry other dissolved chemicals into the body. DMSO is
ﬂammable and it freezes at 4°C, which can be a disadvantage. However, DMSO is least ﬂammable
among the solvents mentioned. Ethanol can also be used, but methanol should be avoided, due
to the high safety risk, as methanol is extremely toxic to humans. Ethanol and methanol have the
disadvantage that they can evaporate, which can change the analyte concentration in the solution.
Both compounds have the advantage that they can be stored at -20°C without freezing, but are both
ﬂammable. DMF can also be used, but like methanol, DMF also has a high risk of potential health
eﬀects like liver injury and cancer [62]. Acetone could also be used, but it has potential risks, since
it is suspected of damaging reproduction and organs.
It is ﬂammable and can form an explosive
atmosphere, since the ﬂash point is below 30°C, but it does not freeze at -20°C.
The organic solvent can inﬂuence the ﬁnal concentration of the analyte solution, for example the
solubility of alternariol is 0.5 mg/mL in ethanol, 5 mg/mL in methanol, 30 mg/mL in DMSO and
in DMF and 5 mg/mL in acetone [63, 64]. Thereby, the ﬁnal concentration for the solution can be
higher when using DMSO or DMF compared to ethanol, methanol and acetone. DMSO was decided
to be the solvent for both cortisol and alternariol. In general, since the analytes is highly insoluble
in aqueous solutions, it is possible that when an aliquot of the stock solution in DMSO is added
into a buﬀer solution, the analytes may partially stick to the surface of the pipette, thereby causing
concentration errors in the prepared solutions. With this knowledge, it has to be mentioned that the
analyte concentrations determined in this project are theoretical, since it was not possible to check
the standard deviation of the concentrations along the x-axis and thereby the real concentrations.
3.2.3
Reagents and Chemicals
PBS (Phosphate Buﬀered Saline, pH 7.4) from Sigma-Aldrich was stored at room temperature. PBST
(Phosphate Buﬀered Saline (pH 7.4) from Sigma-Aldrich and 5% Tween20 from Sigma-Aldrich) mixed
to a concentration of 0.5% stored at room temperature and was primarily used to minimise non-speciﬁc
binding. Coating buﬀer (mix of Na2CO3, NaHCO3 and MilliQwater) 0.25 M pH 9.6, citrate buﬀer
0.04 M pH 5.6, citrate buﬀer 0.04 M with 0.1 KCl from Sigma-Aldrich and hydrogen peroxide (H2O2)
1%, all stored at 4°C. 3,3’,5,5’-tetramethylbenzidine (TMB) from Sigma-Aldrich, stored under vacuum
in the dark at room temperature. Dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO) from Sigma-Aldrich, that was used to
mix the TMB substrate and the two analytes (cortisol and alternariol), was kept at room temperature.
Sulfuric acid (H2SO4) 4N from Sigma-Aldrich was stored at room temperature. Sodium hydroxide
solution (NaOH) 1 M from Sigma-Aldrich was kept at room temperature. Regeneration solution 0.4
M glycine-HCl pH 2.2 was freshly made for the purpose and kept at 4°C for short term storage. Ferri-
Ferrocyanide solution prepared by mixing K3[Fe(CN)6] and K4[Fe(CN)6] in PBS, both from Sigma-
Aldrich, was covered with aluminum foil and stored at room temperature. 3-mercaptopropionic acid
(MPA) from Sigma-Aldrich was stored at room temperature.
MES buﬀer pH 6 (mix of MES (2-
(N-Morpholino) ethanesulfonic acid from Sigma-Aldrich and NaCl from Sigma-Aldrich) was covered
with aluminium foil and stored at 4°C. N-ethyl-N-(3-dimethylaminopropyl) carbodiimide (EDC) and
N-hydroxysuccinimide (NHS) both from Sigma-Aldrich were stored at -20°C under vacuum.
Page 29 of 151
