MATERIALS AND METHODS
The preparation of the reagents is shown in Appendix A.6 Preparation and Storage of Biological
Elements, Reagents and Chemicals.
3.2.4
Chemical Risk Assessment and Waste
In this project, the chemical risk assessment is highly important, especially since it involves working
with a mycotoxin in concentrated amounts, that can have serious consequences for one’s future health,
and many other chemicals and materials like silicon that can be dangerous to handle. Therefore, it
was required at all times when working to wear a lab coat, gloves and goggles. To wear a mask was
only necessary when mixing the analyte alternariol due to the risk of inhaling, since it was in powder
form and could not be mixed under a fume hood, as the powder was too close to the bottleneck. If
a fume hood had been used, it could have resulted in product loss and dispersion of the mycotoxin,
that could become dangerous for others. As many of other chemicals used in the project are health
hazards, the handling when using them and the chemical waste disposal were important matters to
ﬁnd information about before starting the experiments.
3.2.5
Instrumentation
ELISA
Plate washer (Wash 12) from CAPP was used for emptying the 96-well plate through suction. Shaker
(OS-10) from Biosan and shaker (PMS-1000i) from Grant-bio were used in the washing procedure.
Microplate reader Spark from TECAN was used to read the absorbance. Centrifuge (rotor FiberLite
ﬁxed angle) from Thermo Scientiﬁc was used to centrifuge the tomato juice. A pH meter (MA 5736)
from Iskra was used to measure the pH of the prepared tomato juice samples. The program GraphPad
Prism Version 9.0.0 was used to generate the graphs for the report and make the analysis to determine
the IC50 and the limit of detection. The analysis of the sigmoidal calibration curves obtained in ELISA
is made by a 4-parameter logistic equation in GraphPad Prism Version 9.0.0.
Electrochemical Detection
Electrochemical measurements were performed using a PalmSens4 potentiostat with associated equip-
ment and controlled in a computer through PSTrace 5.5 for the data collection software. A plasma
surface cleaner from diener was used to clean the electrode surfaces. Microplate reader (EL800) from
BIO-TEK Instruments INC. was used to read the absorbance.
The program Excel were used to
generate the graphs for the report.
3.2.6
Short term- and Long term Storage
ELISA
If the enzyme reaction was not stopped with sulfuric acid, the 96-well plate containing the biological
elements could be kept with PBS buﬀer for long term storage at 4°C.
Electrochemical Detection
When the electrodes were modiﬁed it was important that they were kept moist as much as possible.
When performing experiments with the electrodes, they were kept in the PMMA holder and covered
Page 30 of 151
==PAGE:36==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
with PBS for short-term storage at room temperature and for long-term (overnight) at 4°C. If the
electrodes were kept for later experiments but not used in current experiments, they were stored at
4°C in a plastic petri dish covered with PBS and wrapped in paraﬁlm. In both cases the liquid was
covered so that no evaporation could occur. PBS was generally kept in long-term storage but not
PBST, as the added Tween20 could act as a detergent, get stuck to the chip and interrupt in the next
experiment.
3.3
ELISA experiments - Cortisol and Alternariol
The ELISA experiments were ﬁrst done for cortisol to get an idea of the procedures which could then
be transferred to ELISA experiments with alternariol. The ﬁrst experiments were to ﬁnd the best
antibody-tracer concentrations and to see if protein G had a positive inﬂuence. The next experiment
was made as a competitive ELISA to produce the calibration curve and determine the value of IC50.
Subsequently, the matrix eﬀect was tested with a tomato product to see what preparation that was
needed before injection.
Finally, the regeneration of the biosensor setup was tested to see where
in the setup the regeneration solution was cleaving. The product (TMBox) could be determined by
spectrophotometric detection since it changed colour from colourless to blue, which could be detected
at 630-650 nm, but the enzyme reaction could also be stopped with acid turning the colour from blue
to yellow, which could be detected at 450 nm.
3.3.1
Optimization of Antibody- and Tracer Concentrations
An ELISA was run to see which antibody- and tracer concentrations were optimal to use. A series of
antibody concentrations were used as well as a series of tracer concentrations. This experiment was
done in triplicates and a "blank" solution was included to deﬁne the background.
Protocol
The Maxisorp plate was coated with antibody (10-C30D or Anti-AOH AB). Coating buﬀer and anti-
body were mixed and diluted into diﬀerent concentrations in tubes and 100 µL of each dilution was
transferred to the plate except for the blank, where 100 µL of PBST was added instead. The plate
was covered and kept overnight (17 hours) at 4°C. PBST and tracer (65-IC08 or AOH-HRP) were
mixed and diluted into diﬀerent concentrations in tubes. The plate was washed four times with PBST
by the standard procedure: throw liquid out, wash with 200 µL of PBST, shake for 2 minutes at 180
rpm and remove buﬀer using suction by the CAPP plate washer and tap 3-4 times on paper to dry.
Afterwards, 100 µL of each tracer dilution was transferred to the plate. The plate was covered and
kept at room temperature for 30 minutes. Within the 30 minutes the substrate solution H2O2-TMB
was made by mixing citrate buﬀer (98%), H2O2 (0.4%) and TMB (1.6%). The substrate solution was
mixed by vortex and kept in the dark until use. The plate was washed four times with PBST by the
standard procedure. Subsequently, 100 µL of the substrate solution was added to each well, covered
and kept under aluminum foil at room temperature for 30 minutes. Lastly, 50 µL of H2SO4 4N was
added to each well to stop the reaction. The absorbance was measured at 450 nm by a TECAN Spark
microplate reader.
Page 31 of 151
==PAGE:37==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
3.3.2
Orientation Optimization with Protein G
An ELISA was run with protein G to see if the orientation of the antibody could be used to optimise
the antibody- and tracer concentrations. The most promising tracer dilutions from the experiment
described in 3.3.1 Optimization of Antibody- and Tracer Concentrations were used. A broader series
of antibody concentrations were used to get a saturation in both ends of the scale. The protein G
optimization had already been investigated at the department and one concentration (1/160 ∼6.25
µg/mL) were chosen for the experiment. This experiment was done in triplicates and a "blank" solution
was included to deﬁne the background.
Protocol
The Maxisorp plate was coated with protein G (P4689 or P19459). Coating buﬀer and protein G
(1/160 ∼6.25 µg/mL) were mixed for the speciﬁc dilution and 100 µL was transferred to the plate.
The plate was covered and kept overnight (17 hours) at 4°C. PBST and antibody (10-C30D or Anti-
AOH AB) were mixed and diluted into diﬀerent concentrations in tubes. The plate is washed four
times with PBST by the standard procedure: throw liquid out, wash with 200 µL of PBST, shake for
2 minutes at 180 rpm and remove buﬀer using suction by the CAPP plate washer and tap 3-4 times
on paper to dry. Afterwards, 100 µL of each antibody dilution was transferred to the plate except
for the blank column, where 100 µL of PBST was added instead. The plate was covered and kept at
room temperature for 30 minutes. PBST and tracer (65-IC08 or AOH-HRP) were mixed and diluted
into diﬀerent concentrations in tubes. The plate was washed four times with PBST by the standard
procedure. Subsequently, 100 µL of each tracer dilution was transferred to the plate. The plate was
covered and kept at room temperature for 30 minutes. Within the 30 minutes the substrate solution
H2O2-TMB was made by mixing citrate buﬀer (98%), H2O2 (0.4%) and TMB (1.6%). The substrate
solution was mixed by vortex and kept in the dark until use. The plate was washed four times with
PBST by the same procedure. Afterwards, 100 µL of the substrate solution was added to each well,
covered and kept under aluminum foil at room temperature for 30 minutes. Lastly, 50 µL of H2SO4 4N
was added to each well to stop the reaction. The absorbance was measured at 450 nm by a TECAN
Spark microplate reader.
3.3.3
Analyte Calibration Curve
A competitive ELISA was run to get the range of the detection and to get the IC50 and the limit
of detection of the biosensor design. This experiment used the optimal protein G concentration and
the optimal antibody concentration and tracer concentration found at around 0.70 absorbance in the
previous experiment in section 3.3.2 Orientation Optimization with Protein G to ensure that the
values were in the useful absorbance range. This experiment was only made with protein G, since it
was proved that the protein had a signiﬁcant impact on the assay performance. This experiment was
done in triplicates and a "zero" solution and a "blank" solution were added, since the "zero" gives the
maximum signal obtained without analyte and the "blank" deﬁnes the background. If the signal of
the competitive assay did not reach the same absorbance as for the "zero" solution, the concentrations
of the analyte, antibody or tracer could be unbalanced. To bring it into balance, one had to go a step
back and optimise either the antibody concentration or the tracer concentration or change the analyte
Page 32 of 151
==PAGE:38==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
concentration range. It is important that the absorbance values were in the useful absorbance range
with a maximum absorbance of 1, where around 90% of the light is absorbed to get a decent signal
for the IC50.
Protocol
The Maxisorp plate was coated with protein G (P19459). Coating buﬀer and protein G were mixed
for the speciﬁc concentrations and 100 µL is transferred to the plate. The plate was covered and kept
overnight (17 hours) at 4°C. Before passing the 17-hour window, three solutions were prepared: PBST
and antibody (10-C30D or Anti-AOH AB) were mixed to the speciﬁc concentration, PBS and analyte
(cortisol or alternariol) were mixed into a series of concentrations, and PBST and tracer (65-IC08
or AOH-HRP) were mixed to the chosen concentration, all done in tubes and kept at 4°C until use.
The analyte concentrations as well as the tracer concentrations were doubled, since they get diluted
by each other on the plate. When the 17 hours had passed, the plate was washed four times with
PBST by the standard procedure: throw liquid out, wash with 200 µL of PBST, shake for 2 minutes
at 180 rpm and remove buﬀer using suction by the CAPP plate washer and tap 3-4 times on paper
to dry. Afterwards, 100 µL of the antibody concentration was transferred to the plate except for the
blank column, where 100 µL of PBST was added instead. The plate was covered and kept at room
temperature for 30 minutes. The plate was washed four times with PBST by the standard procedure.
Subsequently, 50 µL of the analyte were quickly added to the plate, except for zero and blank, 50 µL
of tracer was quickly added to the plate including "zero" and "blank" and 50 µL of PBST was added
to the "zero" and the "blank". The plate was covered and kept at room temperature for 30 minutes.
Within the 30 minutes the substrate solution H2O2-TMB was made by mixing citrate buﬀer (98%),
H2O2 (0.4%) and TMB (1.6%). The substrate solution was mixed by vortex and kept in the dark
until use. The plate was washed four times with PBST by the standard procedure. Afterwards, 100
µL of the substrate solution was added to the plate, covered and kept under aluminum foil at room
temperature for 30 minutes. Lastly, 50 µL of H2SO4 4N was added to the plate to stop the reaction.
The absorbance was measured at 450 nm by a TECAN Spark microplate reader.
3.3.4
Matrix Eﬀect of a Tomato Product
An experiment was conducted to test how the matrix of a tomato product could aﬀect the mea-
surement, the IC50 and the limit of detection. First, diﬀerent preparation methods were tested and
evaluated, and then the best candidate was used for a competitive ELISA to form an analyte calibra-
tion curve identical to the one made with buﬀer solution described in section 3.3.3 Analyte Calibration
Curve, where the samples were spiked with analyte. The same conditions for protein G, antibody and
analyte were used, so that the results would be comparable. The test product was tomato juice from
the brand Rynkeby and the experiment was done in triplicates.
Pretest before Experiment
Diﬀerent preparation methods were investigated and compared to raw tomato juice: 1) tomato juice
diluted 1:10 with PBS, 2) tomato juice supernatant after centrifugation (5 min, 13000 rpm), 3) tomato
juice supernatant after centrifugation (5 min, 13000 rpm) and diluted 1:1 with PBS and 4) tomato
juice supernatant after centrifugation (5 min, 13000 rpm) and ﬁltrated (pore size 0.22 µm). The pH of
Page 33 of 151
==PAGE:39==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
each solution was measured, to determine if the solutions had to be adjusted before use. The solution
diluted 1:10 with PBS was chosen, as it is the simplest preparation and as the whole matrix (particles
and liquid) is included (justiﬁcation in the result section). The pH of the sample is important, since
a low pH could aﬀect the aﬃnity of the biological setup. The product sample should therefore have a
pH around 6.5-7.0, as buﬀer solutions have a pH of 7.3. If the product sample was too acidic the pH
was adjusted with 1 M NaOH.
Protocol
This experiment followed the same protocol as for the analyte calibration curve described in section
3.3.3 Analyte Calibration Curve, but instead of using PSB in the analyte concentration series, 1:5
diluted tomato juice was used. The dilution of the tomato was only 1:5, since the tomato juice was
diluted by the tracer on the plate and would therefore become 1:10 times diluted when mixed. When
tracer and analyte were added to the plate, tracer and pure 1:5 diluted tomato juice were added in
the "zero" and "blank" solutions instead of tracer to create the same conditions. It is very important
to vortex the solutions with tomato juice before use to obtain a homogeneous mixture at all times,
since the particles tend to precipitate with time. The pH of the diluted 1:10 with PBS tomato juice
solution was around 6.5 and was therefore not adjusted with NaOH before use.
The Particle Eﬀect
Experiments were performed to test the inﬂuence of the particles on the biosensor setup. The ﬁrst
experiment tested the eﬀect of three diﬀerent preparation methods: diﬀerent dilutions of raw tomato
juice from 1:1 dilution to 1:100 dilution, supernatant from centrifuged tomato juice (undiluted), and
ﬁltrated supernatant from centrifuged tomato juice (undiluted) on the biosensor setup. The same
conditions for centrifugation and ﬁltration were used as described in the subsection Pretest before Ex-
periment. This experiment was done for cortisol, as it was assumed that cortisol and alternariol would
perform similarly. A second experiment exploring the particle eﬀect was performed with alternariol,
as it looked like the particles could have a signiﬁcant importance on the analyte location of alternariol
in the matrix. In both experiments a "zero" solution and a "blank" solution were included as controls,
and the experiments were performed in triplicates.
Protocol for Experiment with Cortisol
The Maxisorp plate was coated with protein G (P19459). Coating buﬀer and protein G were mixed for
the speciﬁc concentrations and 100 µL was transferred to the plate. The plate was covered and kept
overnight (17 hours) at 4°C. Before the 17-hour window had passed, three parts were prepared: the
antibody (10-C30D) solution, the tracer (65-IC08) solutions and the diﬀerent tomato juice solutions.
Three tracer solutions were prepared, since the supernatant from the centrifuged tomato juice, and
ﬁltrated supernatant from the centrifuged tomato juice should remain undiluted when added on the
plate.
Therefore, one tracer was made with PBST, one with supernatant and one with ﬁltrated
supernatant. Only the tracer with PBST was doubled in concentration. For the tomato juice solutions,
raw tomato juice and PBS were mixed into the diﬀerent dilutions all in doubled concentrations, since
the solutions would be diluted on the plate by the tracer. Here it was assumed that the dilutions
under 1:10 had to be pH adjusted before they were mixed with tracer on the plate, since the 1:10
Page 34 of 151
==PAGE:40==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
dilution had a pH of 6.5. The raw tomato juice used for the 1:1 dilution and the 1:2.5 dilution used
for 1:5 dilution were made and adjusted with 1 M NaOH to a pH between 6.5-7.0. All the solutions
(antibody, tracer and tomato juice) were made in tubes and kept at 4°C until use. When the 17 hours
had passed, the plate was washed four times with PBST by the standard procedure: throw liquid
out, wash with 200 µL of PBST, shake for 2 minutes at 180 rpm and remove buﬀer using suction
by the CAPP plate washer and tap 3-4 times on paper to dry. Afterwards, 100 µL of the antibody
concentration was transferred to the plate except for the blank column, where 100 µL of PBST was
added instead. The plate was covered and kept at room temperature for 30 minutes. The plate was
washed four times with PBST by the standard procedure. Subsequently, 50 µL of PBST was added to
the "zero" solution and the "blank" solution, 50 µL of each of the diﬀerent tomato juice dilutions were
added, 50 µL of the tracer made from PBST were added to the "zero" solution, "blank" solution and
the wells with the diﬀerent tomato juice solutions and 100 µL of the supernatant tracer and 100 µL
of the ﬁltrated supernatant tracer were added to the plate. The plate was covered and kept at room
temperature for 30 minutes. Within the 30 minutes the substrate solution H2O2-TMB was made by
mixing citrate buﬀer (98%), H2O2 (0.4%) and TMB (1.6%). The substrate solution was mixed by
vortex and kept in the dark until use. The plate was washed four times with PBST by the standard
procedure. Afterwards, 100 µL of the substrate solution was added to the plate, covered and kept
under aluminum foil at room temperature for 30 minutes. Lastly, 50 µL of H2SO4 4N was added to the
plate to stop the reaction. The absorbance was measured at 450 nm by a TECAN Spark microplate
reader.
Protocol for Experiment with Alternariol
This experiment was performed in order to conﬁrm a hypothesis on where the analyte could be located
in the sample matrix and if the particles could have a signiﬁcant importance. As the choice of sample
handling was decided to be diluted tomato juice (1:10), since it includes both the liquid and the
particles, an experiment with tomato juice without any particles could give an indication of where
the analyte could be located in the sample matrix and if the particles could have an impact, as the
sample was spiked after the preparation.
This experiment followed the same protocol as for the analyte calibration curve described in section
3.3.3 Analyte Calibration Curve, but instead of using PBS in the analyte concentration series, ﬁltrated
supernatant made from tomato juice was used without any dilution. The tracer solution was also made
with the ﬁltrated supernatant, as the solution should not be diluted on the plate. When tracer and
analyte were added to the plate, the tracer and pure ﬁltrated supernatant were added in the "zero"
and "blank" solutions instead of tracer to create the same conditions. It is very important to vortex
the solutions with tomato juice before use to obtain a homogeneous mixture at all times, since the
particles tend to precipitate with time. The pH of the ﬁltrated supernatant solution was adjusted
with 1 M NaOH to around 6.5 before use.
3.3.5
Regeneration of the ELISA Setup
A regeneration on an ELISA plate was done to investigate which bond the solution would cleave, since
it could be either between the analyte/tracer and the antibody or between the antibody and protein
Page 35 of 151
==PAGE:41==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
G or between the protein G and the plate. This experiment was done to conﬁrm the theory and to
determine how the biosensor setup could be reused maybe for multiple tests instead of one-time-use.
This experiment was done by ELISA, as it is easier to track the signal and thereby, determine which
eﬀect the regeneration has on the setup. In this experiment, it was important to ﬁnd the similarities
between the ELISA plate and the biosensor, so that the results could be transferred between the
two methods. The decision was to use 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 as the regeneration solution on the
biosensor setup.
The analyte was not included, since the tracer and analyte should have similar
binding towards the antibody. However, there could of course be some diﬀerence due to the presence
of HRP. This experiment was only done for cortisol with protein G (P4689), antibody (10-C30D)
and tracer (65-IC08). The experiment had the following conditions: protein G:1/160 ∼6.25 µg/mL,
antibody: 1/800-1/51200 ∼1.5 µg/mL-0.023 µg/mL and tracer: 1/9000 ∼0.11 µg/mL. These values
were chosen based on the previous experiments described in section 3.3.2 Orientation Optimization
with Protein G. The experiment was done in triplicates and a "blank" solution was included.
Protocol
The ﬁrst part of the experiment followed the same protocol as described in section 3.3.2 Orientation
Optimization with Protein G except for the last step and the absorbance conditions, since the reaction
was not stopped before the measurement of the absorbance and the absorbance was measured at 650
nm instead of 450 nm, as the colour remains blue. After 30 minutes of substrate incubation, the
absorbance was measured and used as reference point. The second part of the experiment was the
regeneration. After the control measurement, the plate was washed four times with PBST by the
standard procedure: throw liquid out, wash with 200 µL of PBST, shake for 2 minutes at 180 rpm
and remove buﬀer using suction by the CAPP plate washer and tap 3-4 times on paper to dry. Then
100 µL of the regeneration solution (0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2) was added to each well, covered and kept
at room temperature for 30 minutes. Within the 30 minutes the substrate solution H2O2-TMB was
prepared by mixing citrate buﬀer (98%), H2O2 (0.4%) and TMB (1.6%). The substrate solution was
mixed by vortex and kept in the dark until use. The plate was washed four times with PBST by the
same procedure. Afterwards, 100 µL of the substrate solution was added to each well and the plate
was covered and kept under aluminum foil at room temperature for 30 minutes. The absorbance was
measured at 650 nm. This measurement showed if the regeneration was successful. If the regeneration
was favourable the experiment could go on. The third part of the experiment was to see where the
acid was cleaving and the plate was divided into three sections: protein G (1-3 well), antibody (4-6
well), and tracer (7-9 well) to represent each cleaving site. This plate setup is shown in Figure 10.
Three diﬀerent solutions were added at the same time to diﬀerent parts of the plate multiple times.
The entire procedure is shown in Table 3 including what was added, the waiting period and when
the absorbance (650 nm) was to be measured. Subsequently, 100 µL was added for every solution in
each step and the plate was washed with PBST by the standard procedure after each step. When the
procedure was fulﬁlled with the last measurement after step number 8, the plate was washed by the
standard procedure and PBST was added to each well and stored at 4°C to withhold the setup intact
if reused.

The Potential Regenerative Eﬀect of Substrate
This experiment was done in continuation of the regeneration experiment to see if the substrate could
assist in regenerating the biosensor since it contains two weak acids at a ﬁnal pH of 5.6. The hypothesis
was that the substrate solution could regenerate the setup (cleave oﬀthe tracer) and thereby lower
the absorbance, if measured again.
Protocol
Since it was an experiment that was done in continuation of the regeneration experiment, the same
plate setup was used in this experiment, but only the columns representing the tracer were used (7-9
well). The absorbance measurement made after the regeneration was used as the starting-point for this
experiment. The plate was washed four times with PBST by the standard procedure: throw liquid
out, wash with 200 µL of PBST, shake for 2 minutes at 180 rpm and remove buﬀer using suction
Page 37 of 151
==PAGE:43==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
by the CAPP plate washer and tap 3-4 times on paper to dry. Afterwards, 100 µL of the substrate
solution was added to the plate, covered and kept under aluminum foil at room temperature for 30
minutes. At this time, the plate had been through two rounds of substrate addition. The absorbance
was measured at 650 nm by a TECAN Spark microplate reader. The plate was washed four times
with PBST by the standard procedure. Subsequently, 100 µL of the substrate solution was added
to the plate, covered and kept under aluminum foil at room temperature for 30 minutes. At this
time, the plate had been through three rounds of substrate addition. The absorbance was measured
at 650 nm by a TECAN Spark microplate reader. To see if the signal could be restored, 100 µL of
tracer was added and the plate was covered and kept at room temperature for 30 minutes. The plate
was washed four times with PBST by the standard procedure. Afterwards, 100 µL of the substrate
solution was added and the plate was covered and kept under aluminum foil at room temperature for
30 minutes. The absorbance was measured at 650 nm by a TECAN Spark microplate reader. The
plate was washed by the standard procedure and PBST was added to each well and stored at 4°C to
hold the setup intact if reused.
3.4
Electrochemical Detection - Cortisol and Alternariol
3.4.1
The Manufacturing Process of the Chip
The design of the AQ chip is made by the department and was chosen, as it could provide triplicates
in one measurement, since it has three working electrodes (see Figure 11). In this project, it was not
possible to fabricate the chips myself, as the training had been too time consuming. Therefore, the
chips were produced by Claudy D’Costa, Ph.D student at the department. However, to understand
how the chips were fabricated, the fabrication procedure is reviewed here. The ﬁnal chip design and a
sketch of it with relevant markings of each electrode part are shown in Figure 11 and has the reference
code "3E_SUAQ_17jul20_01".
Figure 11
The chip design. The AQ chip design showed both as real life picture and as a sketch with marked parts
of the working electrodes, the counter electrode and the reference electrode. The counter electrode and the reference can
shift places. Drawing of the chip is made with BioRender.com.
Protocol
In general, the fabrication procedure is based on the method lithography, using a negative tone pho-
toresist to create the main pattern for metal deposition to deﬁne the electrodes, connection leads and
contact pads for interfacing to a potentiostat, as well as to pattern openings in the passivation layer
Page 38 of 151
==PAGE:44==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
to deﬁne the active electrode areas and contact pads. Electron-beam (E-Beam) evaporation was used
to deposit the chromium adhesion layer (20 nm) and gold (200 nm) as the chosen metal to cover the
working-, counter- and reference electrode as well as leads and contact pads. The chips were made in
the clean room at DTU Nanolab and the protocol for the fabrication is described below.
• 1. Spin Coating. The spin coating of photoresist is done on a substrate, which has been pre-
treated to make it more hydrophobic that ensures good adhesion of the resist. Resist deposition,
spin coating and a post-coating bake (also called softbake), which is applied in order to drive out
solvent from the resist ﬁlm. The used substrate is silicon wafer (single side polished; diameter
of 100 mm, thickness of 525 +/- 25 µm) covered with 1 µm of SiO2, which are pre-treated with
1,1,1,3,3,3 Hexamethyldisilazane (HMDS) vapour priming. The resist, AZ nLOF 2020, is spin
coated at 3300 rpm for 30 seconds. The post-coating bake is performed at 110°C for 60 seconds
to obtain a ﬁnal resist thickness of 1.5 µm.
• 2. UV Exposure. This step includes both a mask alignment followed by UV exposure, where
the mask is ﬁrst aligned properly on the resist and then the resist coated wafer is exposed
with UV at the wavelength of 365 nm. The mask is covering the areas to be metallised. The
parameters used for the process are the following: exposure mode soft contact, exposure dose
104 mJ/cm2 and exposure time 9.4 seconds at 11 mW/cm2.
• 3. Post Exposure Bake (PEB). As a negative tone photoresist is used, a post exposure bake
is needed to thermally activate/assist the catalytic process of the resist. The PEB is performed
at 110°C for 60 seconds immediately followed by development.
• 4. Development. As a negative tone photoresist is applied, the exposed resist becomes insol-
uble in the development solution. The process consists of three steps: a developer, a rinse with
H2O and a spin drying. As the mask has covered the electrode pattern with connection leads
and contact pads, the resist in those areas remains soluble and disappears during the develop-
ment, whereas the surrounding resist becomes insoluble and remains on the wafer. The used
development method is single puddle for 60 seconds with the developer Tetramethylammonium
hydroxide (TMAH).
• 5.
Metal Deposition – Chromium and Gold. Electron-beam (E-Beam) evaporation is
applied for the metal deposition (Cr – 20 nm; Au – 200 nm). E-Beam heats up the solid metal,
e.g. gold and turns it into vapour. The vapour of gold is led to the wafer and adheres on the
previously deposited chromium, covering the entire wafer surface, i.e. aeras where the photoresist
was removed during development as well as the surface of the remaining photoresist.
• 6.
Lift-oﬀ.
The lift-oﬀprocess is applied to remove the unwanted metal that covers the
remaining photoresist. It is done by dissolving the remaining resist that lies under it. It is
during this process that the electrode pattern with connection leads and contact pads of gold
will appear as seen in Figure 11. This is done in acetone by using ultrasonic agitation in intervals
of 3x20 minutes.
Page 39 of 151
==PAGE:45==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
• 7.
Passivation.
The passivation is used to cover the connection leads that should not be
exposed during the experiments so that they are not involved in the measurements. This is done
as it is very important that the electrode area is well deﬁned. This part is using a negative tone
photoresist and comprises the same steps described above except for metal deposition and lift-oﬀ.
In this case, the mask is covering the areas for the working electrodes, the counter electrode, the
reference electrode and the contact pads, but not the connection leads, as they have to be passive
during the measurements. The resist on the electrode areas and contact pads will be dissolved
during the development and disappear, whereas everything else remains insoluble and covered.
The process conditions are as follows: a spin coating process using resist SU-8 at 2000 rpm for 30
seconds, a post-coating bake at 50°C for 15 min with cooling down to 20°C, a UV exposure with
the parameters mode soft contact, exposure dose 250 mJ/cm2 and exposure time 22.72 seconds
at 11 mW/cm2, a post-exposure bake at 50°C for 60 min and a manual development step of 3.5
minutes or less with the developer mr-Dev 600 for both the ﬁrst dip and the ﬁnal dip.
• 8.
Inspection. At the end, the wafer is inspected using an optical microscope, where the
pattern of metallised structures are inspected.
• 9. Wafer dicing. Each wafer contains 12 electrode chips with dimensions 13 x 18 mm2. In
order to obtain the individual chips, the wafers are diced using a Disco dicing saw equipped
with a rotating diamond blade. The chip dimensions to be diced can be programmed using the
computer interface of the system.
3.4.2
The Physical Construction of the Biosensor System
The physical construction of the biosensor is separated into diﬀerent parts that all together create
the biosensor system from the electrodes (transducer) to the data recording, which can be seen in
Figure 12. The chips were placed in a specialised poly(methyl methacrylate) (PMMA) holder with
room for up to four chips. For each chip, there is a well for electrolyte, which is designed such that
all the electrodes, reference, counter and working, are present in the same well at all times. Silicone
rubber O-rings were used to form a liquid tight sealing between the chips and the holder in order to
eliminate leaking of the electrolyte to the contact pads. To connect the potentiostat and measure the
electrochemical output, a printed circuit board (PCB) with copper leads and pogo pins was placed
onto the PMMA holder and tightened with screws to the PMMA holder. This system performs the
experiments in stagnant solutions, also called a batch system, as there is no ﬂow and each injection and
wash have to be done manually, which can give varying results. Since the PMMA holder is constructed
to have room for four chips, more than one experiment can be conducted at the same time.
Page 40 of 151
==PAGE:46==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
Figure 12
Physical construction of the biosensor. The biosensor system connected from the chips to the data
potentiostat and via Bluetooth connection to a laptop computer. The electrode chips are shown in the PMMA holder
with and without the PCB. Drawing of the whole biosensor system is made with BioRender.com with some online
elements.
3.4.3
Electrode Cleaning, System Check and Output Observations
When the chips are coming out from the clean room, the chips are usually very clean. However,
before using a chip for testing, it is a good procedure to clean it, so that the performance is as good
as possible. Together with the cleaning it is also good to check the overall function of the biosensor
system before performing the experiments, as there are many separate parts that have to work together
to give proper data.
Electrode Cleaning
The primary purpose of the electrode cleaning is to remove any organic contamination from the surface
that can interfere with its performance. There are diﬀerent ways of cleaning clean room fabricated
electrodes: chemical cleaning with organic solvents (e.g. ethanol or acetone) or inorganic oxidising
agents (e.g. hydrogen peroxide/KOH or piranha solution), electrochemical cleaning in acid or base
and cleaning with oxygen or air plasma. Even though the chemical or electrochemical cleaning are the
most popular, they can be either too harsh cleaning procedures, potentially able to harm micro- and
nanoelectrodes, or ineﬀective. Plasma cleaning can however, remove any organic contamination from
electrode surface without aﬀecting its properties and has the beneﬁt of being "all directional", which
means that every part of the surface that is exposed to it is aﬀected by it [65, 66]. As plasma cleaning
Page 41 of 151
==PAGE:47==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
is not using any organic solvents, this treatment is also more environmentally friendly and safer in
terms of health. To clean the electrodes, plasma treatment with air was applied and the eﬀect of
the cleaning was tested electrochemically before and after the treatment by impedance measurements
and cyclic voltammetry. The normal procedure is to softly clean the electrodes with acetone, ethanol
(70%) and MilliQwater after coming out of the clean room and before using other cleaning processes.
The procedure of the plasma treatment was to run for around an hour. This time period was however
excessive, but is done to ensure a proper cleaning.
Protocol
The chips were taken out of the clean room and brought into the laboratory.
A chip to be used
for biosensor development was cleaned with acetone, ethanol (70%) and MilliQwater and dried by
using N2. The chip was then placed in the PMMA holder and the performance of the electrodes was
assessed by applying 300 µL of Ferri-Ferrocyanide solution to acquire an impedance spectrum and a
CV. Afterwards, the chip was washed with MilliQwater, removed from the PMMA holder and dried
by using N2. The chip was then placed on a glass plate and treated with plasma for four runs of 16
min (around 64 minutes). After the treatment the chip was placed again in the PMMA holder and
the performance of the electrodes was again assessed as mentioned above. Impedance should decrease
when applying plasma treatment, showing decrease in diameter of the semicircle. In CVs, the peak
current should increase.
System Check and Output Observations
When the cleaning is checked, it is also possible to check the overall performance of the biosensor
system and conﬁrm that there is no leakage and that the connections work as expected. The question
is how to see if there is an error in the system. To spot possible errors, it is important to know what
to expect from the used electrochemical detection methods like impedance spectroscopy and cyclic
voltammetry, as the outputs can indicate if there is an error or not.
The technique impedance spectroscopy can be used to check if the system and cleaning are ideal,
as the known trend (behaviour and shape) of the output can give a hint of errors and what is causing
them. The trend is related to the semicircle, which is either decreasing or increasing depending on
what is happening. When the electrodes are cleaned with for example plasma treatment, the diameter
of the semicircle should decrease, but when adding biological elements in the modiﬁcation, as for
example the protein G, the diameter of the semicircle should increase. The trend of the plot is not
just dependent on the size of the modiﬁcation, but it is also dependent on how many negative charged
groups that are present, which can have an inﬂuence on how the plot is behaving. It is not only the
diameter of the output that is important, also the shape of the tail, the starting point of the semicircle
in the diagram and the form of the semicircle can indicate if there are errors. The tail represents
diﬀusion impedance (Warburg impedance) and if the tail in impedance spectra is not visible, it means
that the electrode is not functioning well because the it is either dirty or otherwise malfunctioning.
If the plot is shifting, so that the starting point of the semicircle is shifting during the experiment, it
can be due to contact errors in the system. However, it is normal that the detection starts a bit from
zero, as there is a solution resistance in the system.
Page 42 of 151
==PAGE:48==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
The technique cyclic voltammetry can also be used to check the system as the shape of the output
and its location in the diagram indicates whether something is wrong. The expected output shape is
the "duck" formation, with a the peak to peak separation not far away from the theoretically expected
value of about 59 mV at 25°C derived from the Nernst equation. This theoretical value is hard to
achieve in reality, but the value can be used as a rule of thumb, and values around 100 mV to 150 mV
are still acceptable when measuring on an unmodiﬁed electrode. The location of the output in the
diagram depends on what the reference electrode is made of. Since the electrode surface is made of
gold, the middle of the plot is expected to occur at around zero potential [V]. Indications of possible
errors can be; if the plot does not have any peaks or if the plot is shifted a few hundreds of mV and is
not located around zero potential [V]. However, the exact reason for the error can not be determined
directly from the plot and with CV one has to trouble shoot a bit to ﬁnd the cause of the error. The
error could be due to not proper cleaning or loose connections in the system or a leak that interferes
with the connections. One should however expect that the scans are overlapping and that during
the modiﬁcation of the biological setup, the CV is most likely moving away from the optimal peak
conditions and the "duck" shape is ﬂattened out, meaning that the peaks are decreasing, the peak to
peak separation is increased and the "duck" shape disappears. This tendency is normal and the peaks
should decrease and the shape deform with the additional modiﬁcation.
Even though the system is checked, ﬁxed and functions as expected, it is always good to be aware of
errors, as they can appear with time and give incorrect data.
3.4.4
Biological Modiﬁcation of the Biosensor Setup on the Electrode
When the biological modiﬁcation is built up on the electrode, it is modiﬁed in individual steps, that in
the end form the biosensor setup. Each modiﬁcation step, except for the modiﬁcation with EDC-NHS,
was characterised by impedance spectroscopy and cyclic voltammetry so that each modiﬁcation could
be observed and documented. They were only documented once for each analyte, as it is believed
that all the measurements in between could have an eﬀect on the ﬁnal performance for some analytes.
There is no direct evidence for this hypothesis, but it is seen as a precaution as well as that it saves
time and eﬀort.
Practical information
The modiﬁcation of the biosensor setup was using MPA to form the self-assembled monolayer on the
electrode, as the compound is soluble in both ethanol and aqueous solutions compared to MUA/MH,
which are only soluble in ethanol. As MPA is soluble in water, it makes the modiﬁcation procedure
easier. However, this does not imply that MPA is the optimal linker to use for a ﬁnal biosensor. In
this modiﬁcation, no blocking was used like ethanolamine, as it is considered as a precaution step
and is therefore not necessary when only proving the concept of the biosensor. However, when using
blocking the risk of getting false positives decreases, as the tracer cannot stick to something other
than the antibody. The concentrations used for the antibody and tracer modiﬁcations were increased
compared to the ELISA experiments, as the modiﬁcation on the electrode has not been tested in detail
before and the surface behaviour is not fully known.
Page 43 of 151
==PAGE:49==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
The experiments were divided into three parts: electrode preparation, electrode modiﬁcation and
control test of the biosensor setup by absorbance measurement. For each washing step, suction by
vacuum was applied, except for the modiﬁcations of ﬁrst chips.
It was proved in a regeneration
experiment of the ﬁrst chips that removing the solutions and washing by a pipette gives large variations
in the outcomes.
Protocol
Electrode Preparation
Each chip was prepared by plasma cleaning for around 1 hour and the electrodes together with the
system were checked by impedance spectroscopy and cyclic voltammetry by using 300 µL of Ferri-
Ferrocyanide solution.
Electrode Modiﬁcation
The MPA solution is prepared and kept at 4°C until use. The electrodes on the chip to be used for
modiﬁcations and system were tested and if they functioned as intended, the chip was washed with
MilliQwater and 250 µL of 80 mM MPA was added. The chip was covered with a lid, so that no dirt
could get on it and so that the solution would not evaporate during the incubation. The electrode
was kept for a minimum of 2 hours at room temperature or overnight (around 18 hours) at 4°C.
From experience it was known that the MPA layer is formed within the ﬁrst 2 hours. However, to
ensure the attachment the chip can be kept overnight in the refrigerator. The EDC-NHS solution
was prepared in MES buﬀer (pH 6) with the ﬁnal concentrations of 200 mM EDC and 50 mM NHS,
mixed by vortex and kept at 4°C until use. It is important that the solution is freshly prepared and
that the mixing is done just before adding the solution onto the electrodes. When the incubation was
ﬁnished, the MPA was removed and the chip was washed with MilliQwater. Salt formation from the
MPA could appear on the chip and to ensure that all the salt is removed, the chip was taken out
from the PMMA holder and washed more thoroughly. Before putting the chip back into the PMMA
holder, it was important that the chip was dried as thoroughly as possible without touching the gold
electrode parts. The MPA formation on the electrode was characterised by impedance spectroscopy
and cyclic voltammetry by adding 300 µL of Ferri-Ferrocyanide solution. Before going on to the next
modiﬁcation step, the electrodes were washed with MilliQwater and lastly with MES buﬀer, as the
next solution added was prepared in MES buﬀer. Afterwards, 300 µL of EDC-NHS solution was added,
and the chip was covered and kept for 45 minutes at room temperature. As the EDC-NHS formation
is not that stable, this step was not characterised electrochemically. When the incubation was done,
EDC-NHS solution was removed and the chip was washed with MES buﬀer and lastly with PBS, as
the next solution to be added was prepared in PBS buﬀer. The protein G (P4689 or P19459) solution
was prepared and kept at 4°C until use. Protein G solution was added, and the chip was covered and
kept for 45 minutes at room temperature. After the incubation, the protein G solution was removed
and the chip was washed with PBS. The protein immobilisation on the electrodes was characterised
by impedance spectroscopy and cyclic voltammetry by adding 300 µL of Ferri-Ferrocyanide solution.
Before going on to the next modiﬁcation, the chip was washed with PBST, as the next solution to
be added was prepared in PBST buﬀer. PBST was also used to ensure that excess protein did not
adhere on the sides of the well. The antibody (10-C30D or Anti-AOH AB) solution was prepared and
Page 44 of 151
==PAGE:50==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
kept at 4°C until use. The antibody was added and the chip was covered and kept for 30 minutes
at room temperature. After the incubation, the antibody solution was removed and the chip was
washed with PBST. The attachment of the antibody molecules on the electrodes was characterised
by impedance spectroscopy and cyclic voltammetry by adding 300 µL of Ferri-Ferrocyanide solution.
Before going on to the next modiﬁcation step, the chip was washed with PBST, as the next solution
to be added was prepared in PBST buﬀer. PBST was also used here to ensure that excess antibody
did not adhere on the sides of the well. The tracer (65-IC08 or AOH-HRP) solution was prepared
and kept at 4°C until use. The tracer was added and the chip was covered and kept for 30 minutes at
room temperature. After the incubation, the tracer solution was removed and the chip was washed
with PBST. The attachment of the tracer was characterised by impedance spectroscopy and cyclic
voltammetry by adding 300 µL of Ferri-Ferrocyanide solution.
Control Test of the Biosensor Setup by Absorbance
To check the modiﬁcation on the electrode and check if it is properly built, the biosensor setup was
tested with the substrate by measuring the absorbance. The substrate solution (mixture of citrate
buﬀer (98%), H2O2 (0.4%) and TMB (1.6%)) was prepared and the tube containing the solution was
covered with Al foil and kept at room temperature until use. The chip was washed with PBST, and
300 µL of substrate solution was added. The chip was covered with aluminium foil and kept for 30
minutes at room temperature. A 96-well plate and the spectrophotometer were prepared to measure
the absorbance at 630 nm, when the solution had turned blue. When the incubation was ﬁnished, the
substrate solution including the product was mixed in the well by pipetting and 250 µL was transferred
to a 96-well plate. The absorbance was measured at 630 nm and if the colour of the solution was
blue, the biosensor setup had tracer attached, indicating that the modiﬁcation had been completed
correctly.
Conditions for Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy and Cyclic Voltammetry
• The impedance spectroscopy conditions: Scan type ﬁxed with an E dc of 0.00, an E ac of 0.01
V and a frequency scan at 71 frequencies with 10 points/decade in the frequency range from 0.1
Hz to 1000000 Hz.
• The cyclic voltammetry conditions: Various potential range were applied from -0.25 V - 0.25 V
to -0.6 V - 0.6 V depending on the behaviour of the output. E step of 0.01 V with a scan rate
of 0.1 V/s. The number of scans was 3 as a minimum.
3.4.5
Biosensor Detection - Reproducibility, Regeneration and Analyte Detection
When the biosensor setup was fully constructed and control tested, the next step was to get the
maximum signal obtained with only the tracer present, as it would be used as a reference point when
the electrode is exposed to analyte. Even though the regeneration of the electrode was not considered
as the essential part of this project, as the biosensor would not necessarily have to be reused in
industry, its regeneration was important when collecting the fundamental data for the calibration
curve of the biosensor. As the electrode can act diﬀerently, due to variation in the modiﬁcations on
the surface, it could give more realistic results if using the same electrode to detect the maximum
Page 45 of 151
==PAGE:51==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
signal when only applying the tracer and later detecting the signal when the biosensor is exposed
to the analyte in a competitive assay. That way, the data would then be more connected, since the
decreased signal in the competitive data could be directly compared with the reference point of the
maximum signal of the same biosensor. The goal is to ﬁnd stable signals for both the maximum
signal and each analyte exposure, and achieve repeatability. However, as the chance of getting exact
repeatable signals is low, the goal for this project is to obtain a repeatable signal range. This is a harsh
goal to reach for an immunobiosensor, as it depends highly on the regeneration process, that has the
tendency to vary in output. To examine if there is a repeatable trend, the colour of the product was
ﬁrst used, as was also used in ELISA, since it is an easy approach to evaluate the performance of the
electrodes with constructed biosensor, before moving on to electrochemical detection. However, it has
to be noted, that the area that contributes to the absorbance measurements is larger than the area of
one individual electrode used in electrochemical detection, since the colour formation includes all the
electrodes, whereas the area included in the electrochemical detection is only one working electrode.
As was also applicable for the biological modiﬁcation, suction by vacuum was applied for each washing
step.
Protocol - Reproducibility and Biointerface Behaviour when Regenerated
The ﬁrst task was to obtain a stable signal range for the maximum signal, as it is used as reference
point before moving on to the analyte detection. After the modiﬁcation of the biosensor setup and
the control test of the modiﬁcation, the goal was to deﬁne the value for the maximum signal of the
electrode. To ﬁnd the maximum signal, the value was tested multiple times, which included that
the setup was regenerated after each detection step.
Here diﬀerent regeneration procedures were
investigated using both the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 and the substrate solution
(pH 5.6), as they both showed regeneration eﬀect during ELISA for cortisol. It was assumed that the
tracer and later the analyte were primarily removed and that only new tracer needed to be added to
re-establish the maximum signal.
The general approach was to ﬁnd the maximum signal and investigate how the regeneration and setup
behave for the two analytes cortisol and alternariol. To understand the behaviour of the biointerface,
the performance was ﬁrst tested by absorbance using the colour formation of the product, as the
method could give a more deﬁned value before testing the performance by electrochemical detection.
Diﬀerent strategies were used to investigate the behaviour of the biointerface on the electrode and the
regeneration possibilities. As this project is a proof of concept, the conditions were not optimized, but
the focus was on investigating the biosensor potential.
Repeatability of the Maximum Signal by Absorbance - Regeneration Solution
This procedure utilised the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 and absorbance measurements
to validate the outcome. The electrodes were modiﬁed with the tracer for 30 minutes at room temper-
ature before the experiment was started. The tracer was removed and the chip is washed with PBST,
whereafter 300 µL of the regeneration solution was added for 30 minutes at room temperature. The
amount added and the time were not optimised. The substrate solution was prepared (mixture of
citrate buﬀer (98%), H2O2 (0.4%) and TMB (1.6%)), covered with aluminium foil and kept at room
Page 46 of 151
==PAGE:52==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
temperature until use.
After the incubation, the regeneration solution was removed and the chip
is washed with PBST, whereafter 300 µL of the substrate solution is added. The chip was covered
with aluminium foil and kept for 30 minutes at room temperature. The substrate solution and the
formed product were mixed in the well by pipetting, 250 µL was transferred to a 96-well plate and the
absorbance was measured at 630 nm. This procedure was repeated at least three times, to see if there
was a trend of stability. The baseline for the regeneration was also investigated by adding substrate
solution again after one of the measurements.
Repeatability of the Maximum Signal and Baseline by Absorbance - Substrate Solution
This procedure utilised the substrate solution (pH 5.6) for the regeneration and absorbance to validate
the outcome. The electrode was modiﬁed with the tracer for 30 minutes at room temperature before
the experiment was started. The substrate solution was prepared (mixture of citrate buﬀer (98%),
H2O2 (0.4%) and TMB (1.6%)), covered with aluminium foil and kept at room temperature until
use. After the incubation, the tracer was removed and the chip was washed with PBST, whereafter
300 µL of the substrate solution was added. The chip was covered with aluminium foil and kept for
30 minutes at room temperature. The substrate solution and the formed product were mixed in the
well by pipetting, 250 µL was transferred to a 96-well plate and the absorbance was measured at
630 nm. After that, the rest of the substrate solution was removed and the chip was washed with
PBST, whereafter a new substrate solution (300 µL) was added for 30 minutes to see how eﬀective the
substrate was compared to the regeneration solution and to deﬁne a possible baseline. The substrate
solution and the formed product were mixed in the well by pipetting, 250 µL was transferred to a
96-well plate and the absorbance was measured at 630 nm. This procedure was repeated at least three
times, to see if there was a trend of repeatability.
Protocol - Maximum Signal and Analyte Exposure by Electrochemical Detection
This experiment was only performed for the analyte alternariol, as it is the primary analyte of the
project. The regeneration strategy was to use both the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2
and the substrate solution, as it was proved that the regeneration solution could not fully regenerate
the alternariol biosensor by itself, as the biointerface of the alternariol biosensor was observed to be
more rigid than the biointerface for cortisol detection. Amperometry was used as the technique for
the electrochemical detection, as the reaction of the product formation is continuous and is happening
in a batch system. The goal was to measure the maximum signal for the chosen working electrode and
to detect the reduction current (negative) upon addition of the substrate when various concentrations
of the analyte alternariol had been introduced. The purpose was to create a calibration curve deﬁning
the detection range of the biosensor. The main approach was to get a point for the maximum signal
where the tracer dominates, a middle point where the tracer and analyte competes equally and a
point where the analyte dominates. This curve was a simple version of a calibration curve, but it
has the potential of proving that the biosensor could detect that the analyte is present and could
decrease the signal generated by the tracer. This experiment could also provide information regarding
the optimal concentrations (i.e protein G, antibody, tracer, and analyte) for the competitive assay.
The choice of which regeneration method to use was decided during the experiment and do not follow
a deﬁned procedure. During the electrochemical detection with amperometry, the colour formation
Page 47 of 151
==PAGE:53==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
of the product was also determined by absorbance measurements to obtain a reference point for the
obtained electrochemical signal.
Maximum Signal by Electrochemical Detection and by Absorbance
To get a maximum electrochemical response with amperometry, the electrode was modiﬁed with tracer
for 30 minutes at room temperature before the detection was started. During the last 10 minutes of
the incubation, the substrate solution (mixture of citrate buﬀer with 0.1 KCl (98%), H2O2 (0.4%)
and TMB (1.6%)) was prepared, this time also including 0.1 KCl as supporting electrolyte. After
the incubation, the tracer was removed and the chip was washed with PBST. The potentiostat was
connected, citrate buﬀer with 0.1 KCl (200 µL) was added to the chip and the amperometric detection
was started. It was expected that within the ﬁrst 100 seconds a line would be formed near zero current.
When the line was stable, 20 µL of the substrate solution was added and a peak appeared indicating
the formation of the product. The detection was performed for 500 seconds as the product formation
was continuous. After the electrochemical measurement, the substrate solution including the product
was mixed in the well by pipetting and 150 µL was transferred to a 96-well plate and the absorbance
was measured at 630 nm. The amperometric measurements was repeated 3 times to see the trend, as
it was expected that the citrate buﬀer with 0.1 KCl could remove the tracer during the 500 seconds.
Between the measurements, the chip was washed with citrate buﬀer with 0.1 KCl 3 times. To see the
eﬀect of the experiment and to know what the value was before the regeneration, the signal after the
detection was measured by adding 300 µL substrate solution (mixture of citrate buﬀer (98%), H2O2
(0.4%) and TMB (1.6%)). The chip was covered with aluminium foil and kept for 30 minutes at room
temperature. The substrate solution including the product was mixed in the well by pipetting, 250
µL was transferred to a 96-well plate and the absorbance was measured at 630 nm.
Analyte Exposure by Electrochemical Detection and by Absorbance
The basis for this experiment was the calibration curve obtained by ELISA and it was expected that
the same principal in the procedure could apply here. However, the concentration of the tracer was
higher but combined with diﬀerent analyte concentrations. After the maximum signal was obtained,
the electrode was regenerated by applying both regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 and
substrate solution (pH 5.6) multiple times with PBST wash between until the absorbance was as low
as possible, so that the competition could be as equal between the tracer and the analyte as possible.
After that, the chip was washed with PBST, before the analyte and tracer were added and kept for
30 minutes at room temperature. During the last 10 minutes of the incubation, the substrate solution
(mixture of citrate buﬀer with 0.1 KCl (98%), H2O2 (0.4%) and TMB (1.6%)) was prepared. After
the incubation, the analyte and tracer solution were removed and the chip was washed with PBST
to remove excess elements. The potentiostat was connected, citrate buﬀer with 0.1 KCl (200 µL) was
added to the chip and amperometric detection is started. After 100 seconds or when the line was
stable, 20 µL of the substrate solution was added and a peak appeared indicating the formation of
the product. The detection was performed for 500 seconds as the product formation was continuous.
After the electrochemical measurement, the substrate solution including the product was mixed in
the well by pipetting and 150 µL was transferred to a 96-well plate and the absorbance was measured
at 630 nm. The amperometric measurement was repeated 3 times to see the trend. Between the
Page 48 of 151
==PAGE:54==
MATERIALS AND METHODS
measurements, the chip was washed 3 times with citrate buﬀer containing 0.1 KCl. To see the eﬀect
of the experiment and to compare the value to the maximum signal before the next regeneration, the
signal after the detection was measured by adding 300 µL substrate solution (mixture of citrate buﬀer
(98%), H2O2 (0.4%) and TMB (1.6%)). The chip was covered with aluminium foil and kept for 30
minutes at room temperature. The substrate solution including the product was mixed in the well
by pipetting, 250 µL was transferred to the 96-well plate and the absorbance was measured at 630
nm. This procedure was performed for diﬀerent analyte concentrations and also with diﬀerent tracer
concentrations, as it was suspected that the tracer had an advantage compared to the analyte when
competing for the antibody binding sites, as the regeneration could not bring the absorbance down to
under 0.100.
Electrochemical Conditions for Amperometry
• The amperometry conditions: Program chronoamperometry with the E dc at -0.15, as the
product gets fully reduced at this value (data not shown). The current sampling time was 0.1
seconds and the total duration was 500 seconds.
Page 49 of 151
==PAGE:55==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
4
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
All the results and data obtained from both the ELISA experiments and from the experiments on the
electrodes with the electrochemical detection for both analytes, cortisol and alternariol, are presented,
described and discussed in this section. The ELISA experiments form the fundamental data of the
biosensor setup before implementing it on an electrode and testing it by electrochemical detection.
The main results are from the experiments with alternariol, where the experiments with cortisol are
used as reference of what to expect, as the biological elements for cortisol are highly optimised and
used on the market compared to the biological element of alternariol. As the biological elements for
alternariol are very expensive, all of the protocols were ﬁrst done and optimised on cortisol.
4.1
ELISA Experiments - Cortisol and Alternariol
In this section, all of the ELISA results for both cortisol and alternariol are presented and described.
Optimization of antibody- and tracer concentrations together with the orientation optimization with
protein G, the analyte calibration curve by the competitive ELISA and the experiments showing
the matrix eﬀect and the particle eﬀect are performed for both cortisol and alternariol, where the
regeneration experiment only is made for cortisol as the tendency is assumed to be identical for every
setup.
4.1.1
Optimization of Antibody- and Tracer Concentrations
This section presents the results of the optimal antibody- and tracer concentrations, where the anti-
bodies are physisorbed randomly on the bottom of the wells. To evaluate which antibody- and tracer
concentration that would be optimal, a series of antibody concentrations as well as a series of tracer
concentrations were applied.
Results for Cortisol
This experiment was carried out with seven antibody concentrations from 1/500 ∼2.4 µg/mL to
1/32000 ∼0.0375 µg/mL and four tracer concentrations 1/3000 ∼0.33 µg/mL, 1/6000 ∼0.167
µg/mL, 1/9000 ∼0.11 µg/mL and 1/12000 ∼0.083 µg/mL. The overall result is presented in Figure
13.
Page 50 of 151
==PAGE:56==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Figure 13
Optimal antibody- and tracer concentrations with random orientation for cortisol. The graph
presents the data for four diﬀerent tracer concentrations (TR:1/3000 ∼0.33 µg/mL, TR:1/6000 ∼0.167 µg/mL,
TR:1/9000 ∼0.11 µg/mL and TR:1/12000 ∼0.083 µg/mL) in relation to the antibody concentrations from 1/500
to 1/32000 ∼2.4 µg/mL to 0.0375 µg/mL. The results are presented by the absorbance and with standard deviations.
The expected result for this experiment was a smooth curve, where the absorbance would be halved
for every antibody concentration. However, this was not achieved. It can be seen, that a drastic drop
from the antibody concentration 1/500 to 1/2000 ∼2.4 µg/mL to 0.6 µg/mL occurred and that the
rest of the results were all around zero absorbance. This tendency was investigated and the experiment
was remade several times, even with a "zoom in" version of the range from 1/500 to 1/2000, but the
result turned out to be the same (data not shown). Even though all the reagents, biological elements
and chemicals should have functioned properly, there was one concern whether or not the antibody
had been stored properly, since it had been stored at 4°C for several mouths and not kept at -20°C for
long term storage. A new antibody solution was therefore ordered, but the result, nevertheless, still
remained the same. This odd tendency could be due to the orientation of the antibodies in the well,
even though there should statistically be enough binding sites available even in a random orientation.
From this result, using random orientation, the optimal antibody concentration was in the range of
1/500 ∼2.4 µg/mL to 1/1000 ∼1.2 µg/mL and the optimal tracer concentration could be all of the
tested options, since it depends on which antibody concentration was chosen.
For the next experiment with speciﬁc orientation provided by protein G, the two tracer concentrations
1/9000 and 1/12000 were chosen, as it was assumed that protein G could improve the data signiﬁ-
cantly and that the data points obtained with these tracer concentrations would remain in the useful
absorbance range.
Page 51 of 151
==PAGE:57==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Results for Alternariol
This experiment was carried out with an increased concentration for both the antibody and the tracer
compared to the cortisol experiment, since the activity was unknown. The experiment was carried out
with the antibody concentrations from 1/200 to 1/12800 ∼22.5 µg/mL to 0.35 µg/mL, and with four
tracer concentrations 1/1000 ∼1.2 µg/mL, 1/2000 ∼0.6 µg/mL, 1/4000 ∼0.3 µg/mL and 1/8000 ∼
0.15 µg/mL. The overall results are presented in Figure 14.
Figure 14
Optimal antibody- and tracer concentrations with random orientation for alternariol. The graph
presents the data for four diﬀerent tracer concentrations (TR:1/1000 ∼1.2 µg/mL, TR:1/2000 ∼0.6 µg/mL, TR:1/4000
∼0.3 µg/mL and TR:1/8000 ∼0.15 µg/mL) in relation to the antibody concentrations from 1/200 to 1/12800 ∼22.5
µg/mL to 0.35 µg/mL. The results are presented by the absorbance and with standard deviations.
Even though the experiment with cortisol cannot be compared directly to the experiment with al-
ternariol, there is some resemblance in the assay behaviour, as the same tendency for the curves is
occurring. The same "drop" can be observed in the curve which rapidly tends towards zero. One factor
that is very notable is the activity, as the activity of the antibody and the tracer are both lower for
alternariol compared to the biological elements for cortisol. When comparing the result, it can be seen
that the absorbance given by an antibody concentration of 1/500 ∼2.4 µg/mL with the tracer con-
centration of 1/3000 ∼0.33 µg/mL for cortisol gave a value over 2, whereas the absorbance obtained
by the antibody concentration of 1/400 ∼11.25 µg/mL with the tracer concentration of 1/1000 ∼1.2
µg/mL for alternariol gave a value under 1. This indicates that the activity of the biological elements
for alternariol were not as active as the biological elements used for cortisol.
It can be seen from this result, that something was limiting the assay, as the absorbance was under 1
at very high concentrations for both the antibody and tracer, and this limiting factor could be due to
both parts. It can be seen that increasing the antibody concentration did not improve the situation, as
Page 52 of 151
==PAGE:58==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
the absorbance remained the same for the antibody concentrations 1/200 ∼22.5 µg/mL and 1/400 ∼
11.25 µg/mL. However, the signal increased with the increased tracer concentrations. This behaviour
could be due to the low activity for both the antibody and tracer or just one of them, but it could
also be due to the binding aﬃnity between them. This binding aﬃnity could be poor and could result
in losing tracer during the washing procedure.
For the next experiment with speciﬁc orientation provided by protein G, the two tracer concentrations
1/2000 and 1/4000 were chosen, as it was assumed that protein G could improve the data as it did
for the experiments with cortisol. It was assumed that the data points obtained with these tracer
concentrations would remain in the useful absorbance range as they were clearly below 1.
4.1.2
Orientation Optimization with Protein G
Theoretically, protein G binds to the Fc-end of every Ig antibody, but one question remains, namely,
if protein G can improve the results.
In order words, how much less antibody and tracer could
be used to gain the same response. This section presents the results of the optimal antibody- and
tracer concentrations when introducing speciﬁc orientation by protein G. To evaluate if protein G could
improve the result and which concentrations that would be optimal, a series of antibody concentrations
and two tracer concentrations were applied based on the results obtained with the random antibody
orientation presented above.
Results for Cortisol
This experiment was carried out with the protein G concentration of 1/160 ∼6.25 µg/mL, antibody
concentrations of 1/200 to 1/102400 ∼6 µg/mL to 0.012 µg/mL and the tracer concentrations of
1/9000 ∼0.11 µg/mL and 1/12000 ∼0.0833 µg/mL. The antibody concentrations were chosen to
cover a wide range and the tracer concentrations were chosen based on the results presented in section
4.1.1 Optimization of Antibody- and Tracer Concentration, since the tracer concentrations 1/9000 and
1/12000 gave an absorbance around 1. It was assumed that protein G would improve the results, and
that data points obtained with these tracer concentrations together with protein G would remain in
the useful absorbance range. The overall result is presented in Figure 15.
Page 53 of 151
==PAGE:59==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Figure 15
Optimal antibody- and tracer concentrations with speciﬁc orientation by protein G for cortisol.
The data presents two diﬀerent tracer concentrations (TR:1/9000 ∼0.11 µg/mL and TR:1/12000 ∼0.0833 µg/mL) in
relation to the antibody concentrations from 1/200 to 1/102400 ∼6 µg/mL to 0.012 µg/mL. The results are presented
by the absorbance and with standard deviations.
The result conﬁrms that protein G improved the data, which means that the orientation of the antibod-
ies has an impact, since the absorbance around 1 could be obtained at lower antibody concentrations,
such as 1/1600 ∼0.75 µg/mL and 1/3200 ∼0.375 µg/mL, at the same tracer concentration 1/9000
∼0.11 µg/mL. This tendency was the expected output, since the curve is smoother and did not have
that drastic drop as was seen with the random orientation. This result could imply that the tendency
for the previous result, may have been due to the orientation of the antibody. From this experiment,
it can be determined that protein G should be used in future experiments for cortisol in both the case
of ELISA and the biosensor setup on the electrode, as it improved the results signiﬁcantly. To deter-
mine the optimal antibody- and tracer concentration for the analyte calibration curve, a point around
0.70 in absorbance should be a good indication, since the signal most likely will remain in the useful
absorbance range. The optimal conditions chosen for the analyte calibration curve were, therefore,
the antibody concentration of 1/3200 ∼0.375 µg/mL and the tracer concentration of 1/9000 ∼0.11
µg/mL. It was expected that the best output has the highest value at around 1 in absorbance and
that the "zero" solution, where only the tracer is present, could help determine the highest possible
absorbance for the setup.
To see the improvement achieved through the speciﬁc orientation compared to the random orientation,
the data from both experiments with the tracer concentration of 1/9000 ∼0.11 µg/mL are shown in
Figure 16.

the biological elements or poor binding aﬃnity, protein G did enhance the result, which means that
future data could be improved.

around 0.70 in absorbance should be found. However, the only point that is around 0.70 (exact value
is 0.64) in absorbance, is from the tracer concentration of 1/4000 with an antibody concentration
of 1/400 and this was therefore chosen. As a point in between the two tracer concentrations would
be preferable, it was decided to perform the analyte calibration curve experiment with the antibody
concentration 1/400 and the tracer concentrations 1/3000 and 1/4000, since it was believed that the
1/3000 could provide a higher maximum absorbance and thereby give a more decent signal for the
IC50. It was expected that the result obtained with the tracer concentration of 1/3000 would have
