4.1.3
Analyte Calibration Curve
By performing a competitive ELISA with speciﬁc orientation by using protein G, an analyte calibration
curve for the setup could be achieved and the IC50, the limit of detection (LOD), and the sensitivity
of the ELISA design be determined. This analyte calibration curve could also be used to determine
the analyte concentrations of unknown samples.
In general, the lower the value of the IC50 and LOD the more eﬀective the binding and recognition
of the antigen by the antibody are. The low values reﬂect a high sensitivity of the biosensor design,
meaning that the sensor is very eﬀective at low concentrations. The values are correlated with the
competition in the assay and are only reliable if the competition is equivalent between the tracer and
analyte. The values are also correlated with the concentration of the antibody used for the experiment
which has to be taken into consideration, if obtained results are compared. The term IC50 originates
from inhibition or drug eﬀect studies, but is also applied for immunoassays due to the mathematical
resemblance between the obtained response curves, the sigmoidal curve.
In an immunoassay, the
IC50 refers to a concentration that causes 50% saturation of the antibody binding sites. The LOD
is calculated by taken 90% of the maximum absorbance to ensure that the limit is in the conﬁdence
range of the detection and do not include any noise or errors.
Page 57 of 151
==PAGE:63==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
How sensitive should the biological setup be to fulﬁll the given criteria?
When having a maximum concentration level for the analyte present in the sample, it is important
to determine if the biosensor setup can cover the range of detection needed, also when the sample
preparation is considered. The present maximum concentration level from the European commission
for alternariol is 10 µg/kg, equivalent to 38.72 nmol/L ∼38.72 nM.
Results for Cortisol
This experiment was carried out with protein G (1/160 ∼6.25 µg/mL), and an antibody concentration
of 1/3200 ∼0.375 µg/mL, but with diﬀerent tracer concentrations of 1/9000 ∼0.11 µg/mL and 1/6000
∼0.167 µg/mL and a diﬀerent set of analyte ranges, as the assay was improved over multiple attempts
to obtain the best possible calibration curve. The overall result with both tracer concentrations are
presented in Figure 19.
Figure 19
Analyte calibration curves with speciﬁc orientation by protein G for cortisol with diﬀerent
tracer concentrations. The data presented are made from a competitive ELISA with speciﬁc orientation by protein G.
The optimal antibody concentration used is AB:1/3200 ∼0.375 µg/mL and the data is shown for two tracer concentrations
TR:1/9000 ∼0.11 µg/mL and TR:1/6000 ∼0.167 µg/mL, to show that the top of the curve increased in absorbance
with the increased tracer concentration. The results are presented by the absorbance and with standard deviations.
The data obtained with the tracer concentration of 1/9000 made a ﬁne sigmoidal curve, as seen in
Figure 19, but it was believed that the result could be improved, since the maximum absorbance
value only reached 0.7. Therefore, the tracer concentration was increased to 1/6000, which also gave
a ﬁne sigmoidal curve, as seen in Figure 19, but had a higher maximum absorbance value on 0.9,
which increased the absorbance of the IC50 to a decent value. However, as the tracer concentration
was increased, the IC50 and LOD of the ELISA design also increased a bit. The analysis of the data
obtained with the tracer concentration of 1/6000 ∼0.167 µg/mL gave an IC50 of 1.463 nM, and a
Page 58 of 151
==PAGE:64==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
LOD of 0.207 nM, which veriﬁes that this ELISA setup was very sensitive and could determine low
analyte concentrations. In general, this was a very good result, as the shape of the curve is sharp and
the error bars narrow for almost every data point.
Results for Alternariol
This experiment was carried out with protein G (1/160 ∼6.25 µg/mL), and an antibody concentration
of 1/400 ∼11.25 µg/mL, but with two tracer concentrations of 1/4000 ∼0.3 µg/mL and 1/3000 ∼
0.4 µg/mL, as it was believed that a tracer concentration of 1/4000 could give a low maximum
absorbance. The analyte range was chosen based on the experiments made with cortisol, but as the
biological elements are still new, the analyte concentration range was changed from 400 nM - 0.2048
pM to 2 µM - 1.024 pM, to ensure that the curve reaches a plateau in both ends. The overall result
with both tracer concentrations are presented in Figure 20.
Figure 20
Analyte calibration curves with speciﬁc orientation by protein G for alternariol with diﬀerent
tracer concentrations. The data presented are made from a competitive ELISA with speciﬁc orientation by protein G.
The optimal antibody concentration used is AB:1/400 ∼11.25 µg/mL and the data is shown for two tracer concentrations
1/3000 ∼0.4 µg/mL and TR:1/4000 ∼0.3 µg/mL to show that the top of the curve increased in absorbance with the
increased tracer concentration. The results are presented by the absorbance and with standard deviations.
These results showed ﬁrst of all that the protocol for cortisol could be transferred to alternariol, and
that the changed analyte range was not a necessary act, as the other range would also have matched.
It can be seen that the results obtained with the tracer concentration 1/4000 ∼0.3 µg/mL had a low
maximum absorbance, but it was not expected to only reach 0.5. It was expected that the data for
the tracer concentration of 1/3000 ∼0.4 µg/mL had reached a higher maximum absorbance around 1,
but it only reached 0.6. Although the maximum absorbance signal is a bit low, it can be determined
that there is a trend, as the increased tracer concentration increases the absorbance. Hence, a tracer
Page 59 of 151
==PAGE:65==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
concentration of 1/2000 or 1/1000 could give an absorbance closer to 1 and a higher absorbance at
the IC50. When comparing these results to the ones obtained with cortisol, the curves for alternariol
are shifted to the right, which indicates a higher IC50 and a higher LOD.
The analysis of the data with the tracer concentration of 1/3000 ∼0.4 µg/mL gave an IC50 of 10.71
nM, and a LOD of 2.155 nM, which veriﬁes that the ELISA setup is not as sensitive as the one for
cortisol. This biosensor setup can still measure low analyte concentrations, but there is deﬁnitely
room for improvement and optimization of the biological elements for alternariol, as the IC50 is
around seven times higher than that for cortisol. As a biosensor using these biological elements in
high concentrations can have diﬃculties in detecting low concentrations in pure buﬀer solution, it is
interesting to see how the matrix of a tomato product inﬂuences the setup. In general, this was a nice
result, as the shape of the curves are sharp, but some of the error bars could be brought down.
4.1.4
Matrix Eﬀect of a Tomato Product
When designing a biosensor, it is important to investigate how the matrix of the product sample can
aﬀect the biological elements, and hence, e.g. the IC50 and LOD, compared to using a buﬀer solution.
One question is always about which preparation of the product sample that would give the most
correct concentration of the analyte and the minimum matrix eﬀect for the biosensor. One sample
type was investigated namely tomato juice from the brand Rynkeby. This product was chosen, since
it has the simplest preparation process of all the described tomato products, being suitable for this
proof of concept project. It is important to say that a matrix analysis has to be made for every sample
type, even for diﬀerent tomato juices, to evaluate the eﬀect of each type of matrix on the biosensor
setup.
Tomato Juice Preparation
Based on former preparation strategies described in the theory, diﬀerent preparation methods were
investigated and compared to raw tomato juice: 1) tomato juice diluted 1:10 with PBS, 2) tomato
juice supernatant after centrifugation (5 min, 13000 rpm), 3) tomato juice supernatant after centrifu-
gation (5 min, 13000 rpm) with a dilution of 1:1 with PBS and 4) tomato juice supernatant after
centrifugation (5 min, 13000 rpm) and ﬁltration (pore size 0.22 µm), also called ﬁltrated supernatant.
The diﬀerent preparations are presented in Figure 21 and are all compared to raw tomato juice in
viscosity, transparency and colour. The pH of the diﬀerent preparations was measured, since it can
determine if the solution pH has to be adjusted with 1 M NaOH before use. The pH values can be
seen in Table 4.
Page 60 of 151
==PAGE:66==

rpm) and ﬁltration (pore size 0.22 µm) also called ﬁltrated supernatant.

As demonstrated in Figure 21, raw tomato juice is highly viscous, opaque, red and contains many
small particles. When diluting the raw tomato juice 1:10 with PBS, the liquid gets less viscous, more
transparent and yellow, but the small particles remain. The centrifugation separated the liquid from
most of the particles, which made the supernatant less viscous, but still leaving it opaque and red.
When the supernatant was diluted 1:1 with PBS, the liquid became more transparent, the colour
lighter and the amount of particles were minimized compared to the undiluted supernatant. When
the supernatant was ﬁltered the colour turned yellow and the liquid became very transparent and
clean of particles. The pH of the raw tomato juice (pH 4.35) was expected as the pH of tomato lies
between 4-5, but it can be seen that diluting it with PBS increases the pH. The pH of the sample
is important, since a low pH can aﬀect the aﬃnity of the biological setup, as the optimal pH for the
antigen-antibody complex is between 6.5-8.4, the optimal pH for the HRP enzyme is between 6.0-6.5
and since a low pH is used to regenerate the setup. The product sample should therefore have a pH
around 6.5-7.0. This pH range would also form almost the same conditions as used in the experiment
with buﬀer solution (pH 7.30-7.40), and thereby ensures that the results are comparable. Depending
on the preparation, the solution may need pH adjustment with 1 M NaOH before use.
How can the Particles Aﬀect the Measurement?
The particles can inﬂuence the biosensor in various of ways. One could be with the sample colour,
since some molecules and particles can be connected with the colour. Therefore, the colour could
aﬀect the absorbance measurements when using ELISA, if the colour is not washed away properly.
The colour, which is detected, is either blue or yellow, where the colour of the product sample is either
red, light red or yellow depending on the preparation method. As a yellow substance is detected at
450 nm, while a red substance is detected at around 525 nm, the colour of the product does not seem
to be a problem, unless using the ﬁltrated supernatant, as the preparation turns the solution yellow.
Page 61 of 151
==PAGE:67==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
However, this will be reﬂected in the "blank" solution that is included in the experiment. The particles
can also aﬀect the measurement by either blocking the light of the spectrophotometer or by blocking
the binding sites of the antibodies or by blocking the active site of the enzyme, since the particles
can stick to the bottom of the wells or to the biological elements. Another way of aﬀecting is, that
the particles could attract the analyte, as both analytes are hydrophobic, and bind the analyte in
their matrix. If this is the case, it is possible that the diﬀerent preparation methods can aﬀect the
ﬁnal concentration of the analyte, since unit operations like centrifugation and ﬁltration separate the
particles from the liquid that is used as the sample for ELISA and for the biosensor. Thereby, some
of the analyte can be lost and give a wrong indication of the concentration and other preparation
methods have to be used to extract and isolate the analyte. Tomato juice contains a lot of particles,
which is shown by a large pellet after centrifugation and a blocked ﬁlter. The essential questions that
need to be answered are where the analyte is located in the product sample after the homogenising
and food processing, and how it is ensured that all of the analyte present is isolated in the solution,
that is injected into the biosensor.
The Chosen Preparation Method
The chosen preparation method was tomato juice diluted 1:10 with PBS, since both the particles and
the liquid are present, and the eﬀect of both can be investigated. It is also the simplest preparation of
the product as it only takes a dilution and no pH adjustment, which could be useful for the industry,
as it could easily be implemented in an automatic ﬂow system. When using this preparation method,
the dilution could bring the analyte concentration under the limit of detection, which could give false
negatives, as the real analyte concentration cannot be quantiﬁed [53]. This has to be considered and
investigated, as it can cause a signiﬁcant problem, if the biosensor is not sensitive enough, which
could be the case here. Since the maximum allowed level of alternariol is 10 µg/kg equivalent to 38.72
nM, could the dilution factor of 10 be a realistic dilution factor, as a sample with this concentration
can be diluted 10 times and still give a value of 3.872 nM, which is over the LOD obtained with
pure buﬀer solution. Alternariol concentrations found in tomato juice is, however, in the range of
1.1 µg/kg to 2.1 µg/kg, equivalent to 4.26 nM to 8.13 nM. If such sample matrices are diluted 10
times, the concentration may range from 0.426 nM to 0.813 nM, which is most likely under the limit
of detection, when using the present biological elements obtained from Abvigen Inc in a biosensor.
The chosen preparation methods depend on the sensitivity of the biosensor and at which alternariol
concentration it has to respond, but also on the viscosity and matrix of the product.
Results for Cortisol
To test the matrix eﬀect of tomato juice on cortisol detection could be beneﬁcial, as the experiment
will reﬂect the real matrix eﬀect with minimum background response, since cortisol is not normally
present in tomato products and since the analytes are very alike. When the experiment is done with
alternariol and its biological elements antibody and tracer, already existing alternariol (if any) in the
sample could aﬀect the result as the tomato juice is spiked with the analyte after the preparation.
Page 62 of 151
==PAGE:68==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Matrix Eﬀect
The experiment of the matrix eﬀect was performed with tomato juice diluted 1:10 with PBS with the
conditions of a protein G (1/160 ∼6.25 µg/mL), an antibody concentration of 1/3200 ∼0.375 µg/mL
and a tracer concentration of 1/6000 ∼0.167 µg/mL. The result is presented in Figure 22 (left).
Figure 22
Analyte calibration curve with speciﬁc orientation by protein G for cortisol with the matrix
eﬀect of diluted tomato juice with comparison. The data presented are made from a competitive ELISA with
speciﬁc orientation by protein G and tomato juice diluted 1:10 (D:10x) with PBS to determine the matrix eﬀect of
the setup. The optimal antibody- and tracer conditions are used: AB:1/3200 ∼0.375 µg/mL and TR:1/6000 ∼0.167
µg/mL. The ﬁgure shows both the data for the matrix eﬀect to the left, and the compared data with the diﬀerent matrix
of buﬀer solution and diluted tomato juice to the right. The results are presented by the absorbance and with standard
deviations.
In the experiment, some practical diﬃculties were observed, as the particles tended to precipitate
in the solutions and had to be regularly mixed during the experiment, but also since the particles
sometimes blocked the pipette tip and one had to be very attentive to the volume. Even though the
tomato juice gave some practical complications, the result turned out well, with a sharp shape and
small errors bars.
The analysis of the data gave an IC50 of 1.435 nM, and a LOD10 of 0.267 nM, which veriﬁed that
the ELISA setup even in the presence of diluted tomato juice is very sensitive and can determine
low analyte concentrations.
To see where the tomato juice had an eﬀect, the result is compared
with the results from the analyte calibration curve obtained with pure buﬀer solution (see Figure 22,
right). When comparing the results, it can be seen that the matrix of the diluted tomato juice aﬀects
the maximum absorbance, as the maximum of the curve is decreased from around 0.9 to around
0.7, thereby, having an absorbance reduction of around 23%. However, when comparing the IC50
and the LOD, the matrix had almost no eﬀect. Both values were nearly the same as those in pure
buﬀer solution: IC50: 1.463 nM in buﬀer solution and 1.435 nM in tomato juice diluted 1:10 with
Page 63 of 151
==PAGE:69==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
PBS; and LOD: 0.207 nM in buﬀer solution and 0.267 nM in tomato juice diluted 1:10 with PBS.
It can be seen that the LOD increased a bit when using the diluted tomato juice. This result could
indicate that the pH of the diluted tomato juice is in the right range and was not too low, as the
IC50 is almost unchanged compared to the IC50 obtained with pure buﬀer solution. Too low a pH
could have the risk of aﬀecting the binding aﬃnity. Since the IC50 was almost unchanged it indicates
that the binding aﬃnity of the antibody was probably not aﬀected by the diluted tomato juice. The
lowered maximum absorbance could have several reasons. One reason might be that the tomato juice
could contain compounds, that can prevent the absorbance of the product and thereby aﬀect the
measurement by the spectrophotometer, or that a compound reacts with the product and thereby
lowers the absorbance. Another reason could be that the tomato juice contains proteins which block
some of the binding sites on the antibody. It has been proven in literature, that proteins from a
matrix can bind to antibodies and block the binding sites. Such protein-antibody binding has been
reported to interfere with the accuracy of immunoassays [67].
Another reason could be, that the
tomato juice consists of compounds which either aﬀect the enzyme by inhibition, so that the activity
is decreased, or aﬀect the enzyme reaction by acting as the substrate, which competes with TMB,
and the related product cannot be detected at the wavelength of 450 nm. In literature, it has been
reported that a possible reason for reduced absorbance or reduced colour development is caused by
inhibition of the enzyme and its activity [53]. One group of compounds that could be the reason for
the decreased enzyme activity is heavy metals. Heavy metals are proven to exert an inhibitory eﬀect
on microorganisms, where the general consensus states that the toxicity of heavy metals are caused by
their interactions with nucleic acids and proteins, such as enzymes. It has been proven in literature
that the inhibition mode of heavy metals is reversible and uncompetitive, and that diﬀerent heavy
metals have diﬀerent levels of inhibition. Especially Hg+2 causes high degree of enzyme inhibition [68].
Tomato and tomato products have diﬀerent heavy metals in their contents, among them also those that
have been reported to inhibit the HRP enzyme (see the content of tomatoes and tomato products in
Appendix A.5 Contents of Tomato and Diﬀerent Tomato Products). If these heavy metals are present
in the liquid, they could potentially inhibit the HRP enzyme and cause the reduced absorbance.
This hypothesis was questioned, since it was assumed that the heavy metals would also aﬀect the
antigen-antibody complex, thereby aﬀecting the IC50, which is in this experiment unchanged. It has
been proven in literature that heavy metals can induce changes in the aggregation performance and
behaviour of the antibody and the formation of the antigen-antibody complex, as they can aﬀect the
hydrodynamic properties. It is therefore unexpected, that research showed that the antibody-antigen
binding activity is unaﬀected by the presence of the heavy metals even at ’non-physiological’ high
concentrations [69]. It is therefore possible that the heavy metals only aﬀect the HRP enzyme and
not the binding activity of the antigen-antibody complex, resulting in similar IC50, but at a reduced
maximum absorbance. However, this hypothesis could be wrong, and it could be that the heavy metals
would aﬀect the whole biological setup and not only the enzyme. To ensure that the heavy metals are
the main actor in this decreased maximum absorbance, it has to be investigated and validated, since
it also could be a combination of several reasons, e.g. both proteins blocking the binding sites and
inhibition of the HRP enzyme, as the absorbance reduction (23%) is signiﬁcant even when the tomato
juice was diluted 1:10, considering that the heavy metals would also be diluted 10 times.
Page 64 of 151
==PAGE:70==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
If the tomato juice only aﬀects the measurement by the spectrophotometer, this problem would disap-
pear when measuring the product of the enzyme reaction electrochemically on the electrode. However,
if compounds in the tomato juice are blocking the binding sites or inhibit the enzyme or compete with
the substrate, the issue will also appear in the electrochemical detection. One other problem that
could occur when applying the electrode is if some compounds from the tomato juice bind to the
electrode surface and block the detection of the product.
The results showed overall that the matrix of the diluted tomato juice does not aﬀect the biosensor
setup for cortisol as negatively as it could. This result is quite decent.
Particle Eﬀect - Experiment for Cortisol
A particle experiment was performed to investigate the eﬀect of the particles on the biological setup
and if there is a trend in the behaviour when using diﬀerent dilutions of the raw tomato juice and
particle-free solutions like the supernatant and the ﬁltrated supernatant of the tomato juice. The
hypothesis was that the more particles there are present, the more negative is the eﬀect and the lower
measured absorbance.
Before the experiment, the pH had to be adjusted in some of the prepared tomato juice solutions
with 1 M NaOH, so that the average pH was around 6.5-7.0. Since the pH of tomato juice diluted
1:10 with PBS was measured to be around 6.5 (see Table 4), it was assumed that the solutions with
lower tomato juice concentrations would have a pH value in the correct range. Therefore, only the
solutions to create the dilution 1:1 e.g. raw tomato juice, dilution 1:5 e.g. dilution 1:2.5 on the plate,
the supernatant and ﬁltrated supernatant solutions were pH adjusted. Table 5 contains the starting
pH of the diﬀerent preparations, the added amount of 1 M NaOH and the ﬁnal pH. Since this pH
adjustment was necessary, small variations in the dilutions could appear.

The experiment of the particle eﬀect was performed with biological elements of cortisol, as it would
give the minimum background eﬀect. The tomato juice preparations were diluted tomato juice from
1:1 to 1:100 mixed with PBS, supernatant and ﬁltered supernatant. The overall result is presented in
Figure 23, but the data point for dilution 1:5 was excluded, as it was an outlier.
Page 65 of 151
==PAGE:71==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Figure 23
The particle eﬀect of tomato juice with cortisol, the trend of the matrix behaviour with diﬀerent
preparation methods. The data presented for the particle eﬀect includes an interval of tomato juice dilutions from
1:1 to 1:100 mixed with PBS, supernatant and ﬁltrated supernatant. The ﬁgure only shows the ﬁxed data, as the data

The experiment showed some expected and unexpected results. The expected results was to see the
trend of increased absorbance from the least diluted (dilution 1:1) to the most diluted (dilution 1:100)
solution of tomato juice, and to see that the most diluted tomato juice still had an eﬀect compared
to the absorbance obtained with the pure buﬀer solution. This made sense, since the particles and
other compounds that could aﬀect the setup were diluted more and more. The unexpected results
were from the supernatant and ﬁltered supernatant, since they were expected to perform best among
the tomato preparations, as there were no particles present in the solutions. Nevertheless, they both
had the lowest absorbance of around 0.65. This indicates that the particles themselves are not the
main contributors to the reduced absorbance observed in tomato products as compared to pure buﬀer
solution. This behaviour could be due to the solutions. The supernatant and ﬁltered supernatant
were not diluted, which makes the compounds in the sample more concentrated. It could also be due
to the centrifugation, as the used force (13000 rpm) could have forced a release of compounds, that
could aﬀect the setup even more negatively than when they were bound to the particles. Another
reason for the general behaviour of the decreased signal, could be due to proteins in the tomato juice,
since proteins can pass through a ﬁlter with the pore size of 0.22 µm corresponding to a microﬁltration
[70], and block the binding sites of the antibody, causing the low absorbance. The slightly diﬃcult
ﬁltration implies that the supernatant of the tomato juice still contains a large amount of proteins [71]
even after centrifugation. The correct reason for the behaviour is uncertain and has to be investigated.
Page 66 of 151
==PAGE:72==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
If the reason of this trend is not only due to dilution, but also due to the centrifugation releasing
compounds from the particles. The already suspected heavy metals could also be the reason here,
since it has been proved in literature that the metal ion concentration in digested sludge can be
increased as the centrifugal force is increased [72]. The increased metal concentration could cause
increased enzyme inhibition. This hypothesis has to be investigated, since the preparation methods
can aﬀect the biological elements e.g. HRP enzyme and thereby the detection of the analyte. This
result reﬂects that a higher dilution factor gives a higher absorbance, as the particles and other harmful
compounds are diluted, which is closer to the value obtained with pure buﬀer solution. It has been
reported in literature, that less than 10% absorbance reduction could be an acceptable interval [53].
The dilution 1:10 has a absorbance reduction of around 23%, but it seems like a higher dilution factor
like 1:20 or 1:40 could decrease the absorbance reduction to under 10%. However, this is only relevant
if dilution is the optimal preparation method to use. Either way, the sensitivity of the biosensor must
be capable of handling the preparation and a possible dilution factor as the quantiﬁcation depends on
the LOD.
It can be seen that the absorbance of the 1:10 dilution with PBS has a higher absorbance in this
experiment compared to the other experiment examining the matrix eﬀect. This was not expected, as
they should be identical, but the diﬀerence could be due the fact that it was performed in two diﬀerent
experiments with diﬀerent protocols, as the analyte cortisol was applied to create the calibration curve
but was not used in the experiment of the particle eﬀect. It could also be due to the tomato juice
solutions not being identical, since the tomato juice was not eternally homogenized.
Results for Alternariol
Even though alternariol could be present in the sample of the tomato juice and thereby caused dis-
turbance in the experiment, it would be good to determine if the matrix had the same eﬀect on the
biological elements for alternariol as it had for cortisol, when using tomato juice diluted 1:10, as it
is these elements a potential biosensor should use. This experiment would also give a more realistic
picture of how alternariol is behaving in the matrix, i.e. if alternariol is behaving like cortisol and if
dilution is the right sample preparation method.
Matrix Eﬀect
The experiment of the matrix eﬀect was performed with tomato juice diluted 1:10 with PBS with the
conditions of a protein G (1/160 ∼6.25 µg/mL), an antibody concentration of 1/400 ∼11.25 µg/mL
and a tracer concentration of 1/3000 ∼0.4 µg/mL. The analysed data is presented in Figure 24 (left).
Page 67 of 151
==PAGE:73==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Figure 24
Analyte calibration curve with speciﬁc orientation by protein G for alternariol with the matrix
eﬀect of diluted tomato juice with comparison. The data presented are made from a competitive ELISA with
speciﬁc orientation by protein G and tomato juice diluted 1:10 (D:10x) with PBS to determine the matrix eﬀect of the
setup. The optimal antibody- and tracer conditions are used: AB:1/400 ∼11.25 µg/mL and TR:1/3000 ∼0.4 µg/mL.
The ﬁgure shows both the data for the matrix eﬀect to the left, and the compared data with the diﬀerent matrix of
buﬀer solution and diluted tomato juice to the right. The results are presented by the absorbance and with standard
deviations.
In this experiment, the same practical diﬃculties with the particles were observed as in the experiment
with cortisol. As the matrix with alternariol was expected to behave equally to what was observed for
cortisol, it was expected that the matrix of the diluted tomato juice would have the same eﬀect, where
the absorbance was decreased and the IC50 and LOD remained almost unchanged. However, instead
of decreasing the absorbance, the signal increased signiﬁcantly as compared to the analyte calibration
curve made for alternariol in pure buﬀer solution, as the maximum signal went from around 0.6 to
around 1.1 (see Figure 24, right). The increased absorbance, means that more tracer is bound to the
antibody than analyte, which could indicate that the amount of analyte is decreased. If this is the
case, the competition is unbalanced, since the tracer has an advantage in the assay. This result could
indicate that the preparation method, where the tomato juice is only diluted is not the best choice,
as it looks like alternariol is lost somewhere in the matrix.
The analysis of the data gave an IC50 of 28.269 nM and a LOD of 0.874 nM, which diﬀers from
the values obtained with pure buﬀer solution. The IC50 is around 3 times higher, whereas the LOD
is around 2.5 times lower. This analysis seems to be inﬂuenced by mathematical artefacts as the
normal tendency is that when IC50 increases, the LOD increases as well, which was not the case in the
experiment. This statistical inﬂuence, which led to the lowered LOD, could be due to the shape of the
curve. It is not that sharp and the maximum absorbance values of the curve at the low concentrations
do not form a clear plateau. Since the LOD is calculated as 90% of the maximum absorbance, the value
Page 68 of 151
==PAGE:74==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
may decrease. However, the standard deviation for the data points at the upper bound of the curve
do allow for a straight line similar to the one obtained with buﬀer solution. Therefore, this tendency
to a higher maximum top point might just be an illusion from the analysis and the variable data. As
this assay is suspected to be unbalanced and the analysis of the data is suspected to be inﬂuenced by
mathematical artefacts, one should stay critical of the results, especially of the values for IC50 and the
LOD. The eﬀect of the matrix cannot be determined by this experiment, as the hypothesis of analyte
being lost is creating an unbalanced assay. However, this experiment can give an indication of where
the analyte could be in the sample matrix and which consequences the preparation method could have
on the ﬁnal concentration.
This experiment show that even when the analytes are structurally similar, they can act diﬀerently
in a sample matrix, as alternariol clearly behaves diﬀerently in the diluted tomato juice than cortisol
does.
These observations also indicate, how important the sample handling is and how diﬀerent
analytes can have diﬀerent optimal preparation, as the wrong preparation method can give a wrong
ﬁnal concentration of the analyte and thereby give a false negative result. The main understanding
when the two analytes cortisol and alternariol were compared structurally, was that cortisol is more
hydrophobic than alternariol, but due to this experiment is seems to be the opposite. To this point it
is also believed that the more hydrophobic the analyte is, the more it would be bound to the particles
in the tomato juice to avoid the aqueous solution. To better understand the results, the Log P value,
which determines the lipophilicity of a compound, was found for both analytes and it turned out that
the main understanding was wrong. The value for cortisol is lower than for alternariol, as the value is
1.79 and 2.7 respectively [73, 74]. This could explain the loss of analyte, as there is a greater chance
that alternariol binds to the particles than there is for cortisol. A hypothesis is therefore, that the high
absorbance was because most of the analyte got bound to the particles in the tomato juice and got
washed away together with them in the assay. As the diluted tomato juice is spiked, it is possible to
verify the hypothesis and determine if the analytes are lost due to the particles. Such an experiment
would include a tomato juice solution where the particles are removed before spiking with analyte,
like the ﬁltrated supernatant.
Particle Eﬀect - Experiment for Alternariol
As the hypothesis was that alternariol was bound to the particles in the tomato juice, an experiment
was conducted with ﬁltrated supernatant from the juice, as it is the most particle free solution obtained.
One factor to consider was that this solution is suspected of having a higher concentration of harmful
compounds than the diluted version, as it is non-diluted and has been through centrifugation, which
could have released compounds from the particles into the supernatant. If the hypothesis about the
particles was correct, the result, especially the IC50 and the LOD, should be closer to the result
obtained with pure buﬀer solution, as the analyte should be able to compete equally with the tracer.
The ﬁltrated supernatant was prepared with the same procedure as described in section 4.1.4 Matrix
Eﬀect of a Tomato Product, with a centrifugation (13000 rpm, 5 min) and a ﬁltration (pore size 0.22
µm). The solution was pH adjusted before use, as the pH should be around 6.5-7.0. The start pH,
added amount of 1 M NaOH and the ﬁnal pH are shown in Table 6. It can be seen that more 1

M NaOH had to be added compared to the previous pH adjustment, this is due to the volume as it
increased from 8 mL to 15 mL.

This experiment of the particle eﬀect was performed with ﬁltered supernatant from tomato juice with
protein G (1/160 ∼6.25 µg/mL), an antibody concentration of 1/400 ∼11.25 µg/mL and a tracer
concentration of 1/3000 ∼0.4 µg/mL. The analysed data is presented in Figure 25.
Figure 25
Analyte calibration curve with speciﬁc orientation by protein G for alternariol to view the
particle eﬀect of tomato juice for alternariol. The data presented are made from two diﬀerent competitive ELISA
with the matrix of pure buﬀer solution and ﬁltrated supernatant from tomato juice. The optimal antibody- and tracer

This experiment indicates that the particles in the tomato juice could have a signiﬁcant importance
when performing the assay with alternariol. As expected, due to the ﬁltrated supernatant probably
having a higher concentration of harmful compounds, the maximum absorbance went down from 0.6
for pure buﬀer solution to around 0.3, with the consequence that the absorbance at the IC50 and
LOD were also in the lower end of the useful absorbance range. Although the maximum absorbance
decreased, the IC50 and the LOD were close to the ones obtained with pure buﬀer solution, as the
Page 70 of 151
==PAGE:76==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
analysis gave an IC50 of 9.862 nM and a LOD of 1.446 nM. Both values were decreased a bit, as the
values obtained for pure buﬀer solution were an IC50 of 10.71 nM and a LOD of 2.155 nM. This result
indicates that the competitive assay is most probably in balance, and that the analyte and tracer are
competing equally. Large error bars for some of the data points are visible and are believed to be
due to practical problems during the experiment, as the ﬁltered supernatant solution tended to foam
when being pipetted. This foam could be caused by the proteins in the solution and the ﬁltration, as
it was observed that the solution foamed after going through the ﬁlter. The curve has a sharp form,
but the upper part could be even sharper, if the large errors were eliminated.
This experiment illustrates that the particles in the tomato juice attracts the analyte alternariol and
traps it within their matrix. This experiment also showed that diﬀerent preparation methods can have
an inﬂuence on the ﬁnal alternariol concentration and have diﬀerent advantages and disadvantages.
The dilution preparation has the advantage that the harmful compounds are decreased, wheres the
disadvantage is that the particles can aﬀect the ﬁnal alternariol concentration. The ﬁltered supernatant
from the tomato juice has the advantage of not having any particles aﬀecting the ﬁnal alternariol
concentration, but have the disadvantages of having a high concentration of harmful compounds,
which has an eﬀect on the absorbance and having a centrifugation step, where analyte could be lost.
This experiment could indicate that alternariol is trapped into the tissue of the tomato, when the
fungi is growing on it and exporting the toxin, which points to that the best preparation methods
could be to isolate the toxin from the sample to avoid getting false negative results.
4.1.5
Regeneration of the ELISA Setup
A regeneration experiment of the ELISA setup with the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2
was performed to determine where the acid would cleave. This experiment could also determine which
theory that is correct and which goal for the biosensor could be made a reality. This experiment was
made with ELISA, since it is easier to determine the result than using an electrochemical response.
Since protein G can bind strongly to every Ig antibody and since the antigen-antibody complex
should work in the same way, it is assumed that the regeneration performs equally for all assays.
This experiment was performed as identical as an experiment on the electrode, but one diﬀerence is
that protein G was covalently bound to the electrode, whereas the protein is attached to the well by
physisorption. The covalent binding should be stronger than physisorption. An initial experiment
was run to get an idea of the time needed for a full regeneration, and the experiment showed that 12
minutes were not enough (data not shown). Therefore, the time was increased to 30 minutes, but this
time could be optimised, since the precise regeneration time is probably between these limits.
Results for Cortisol
This experiment was performed with the biological elements for cortisol detection, which will create
the fundamental understanding of the regeneration.
Only tracer was used to save analyte.
The
experiment was divided into three scenarios depending on where the solution could cleave: either
between the plate and protein G or between protein G and the antibody or between the antibody and
the tracer/analyte. The enzyme reaction was not stopped with acid, since the setup had to be reused
and the absorbance is detected at 650 nm (colour blue) [75]. The results are presented at Figure 26.
Page 71 of 151
==PAGE:77==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Figure 26
Regeneration in three diﬀerent scenarios: focus on protein G, focus on antibody and focus on
tracer. The three diﬀerent scenarios are displayed with the reference signal, the signal after 30 minutes of regeneration
solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 and the restored signal after either adding new protein G, antibody and tracer or adding
new antibody and tracer or only adding new tracer. The experiment had the following conditions: protein G:1/160 ∼
6.25 µg/mL, antibody: 1/800-1/51200 ∼1.5 µg/mL-0.023 µg/mL and tracer: 1/9000 ∼0.11 µg/mL. The results are
presented by the absorbance and with standard deviations.
The results were a bit surprising, since the hypothesis was that the antigen-antibody complex was
stronger than the binding between protein G and the antibody. The results indicated that the antigen-
antibody complex was the primary target for the regeneration solution. This is shown in Figure 26
- focus on tracer, since the signal could almost be restored by only adding new tracer. However, the
non-identical signal could be due to missing antibodies or simple variation. The scenario with focus on
the antibody, Figure 26 - focus on antibody, shows a higher restored signal after adding new antibody
and tracer, which could indicate that more antibodies were attached to protein G than before. This
could imply that the 30 minutes of incubation was too short or that the antibody concentration was
too low to ﬁll in every binding site on protein G. Both results demonstrate that protein G remains
Page 72 of 151
==PAGE:78==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
attached to the plate, since the restored absorbance had the same tendency. The scenario with focus
on the protein G, Figure 26 - focus on protein G, shows that by adding new protein G, antibody and
tracer, the same absorbance could be restored, where the small diﬀerence in the absorbance could
be due to variation in the experiment. From this experiment, it was proposed that the regeneration
solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 primarily cleaved oﬀthe tracer/analyte in the ELISA setup and left
protein G and antibody attached.
A hypothesis could be that applying a force could rip oﬀthe
antibody from protein G. This idea is based on existing protein G antibody puriﬁcation kits which
use almost the same regeneration solution or elution buﬀer (0.2 M gly-HCl buﬀer pH 2.5), but uses
spin columns and a centrifugation step (10 min, 10000g) to isolate the antibodies [48]. As the time
limit in the project was short, this hypothesis was not veriﬁed.
The Potential Regenerative Eﬀect of Substrate
As a side experiment, it was interesting to see if a weaker acid like the substrate solution itself with
a pH of 5.6 could function as a regeneration solution. If the substrate could cleave of the tracer and
wash it away before the substrate could be converted and detected, this could have an inﬂuence if
using a ﬂow system for the biosensor. This experiment was only performed in continuation of the
regeneration experiment using the same plate setup, where biological elements for cortisol detection
and the standard substrate solution were used. The experiment to determine the eﬀect of the substrate
was performed only with focus on the tracer, as this experiment only had to validate the hypothesis
and since it was observed that the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 mainly cleaved between
the antibody and the tracer. The result is present in Figure 27.
Figure 27
Regeneration eﬀect of the substrate with focus on the tracer. The tracer scenario is displayed
with the reference signal, the signal after two additions of substrate (60 minutes incubation time), the signal after three
additions of substrate (90 minutes incubation time) and the restored signal after adding new tracer. The experiment
had the following conditions: protein G:1/160 ∼6.25 µg/mL, antibody: 1/800-1/51200 ∼1.5 µg/mL-0.023 µg/mL and
tracer:1/9000 ∼0.11 µg/mL. The results are presented by the absorbance and with standard deviations.
Page 73 of 151
==PAGE:79==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
The results from this experiment demonstrated the same pattern as for the regeneration experiment
with 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2, where the signal could be restored by only adding new tracer to the well.
However, what this experiment also showed is that the substrate decreased the signal signiﬁcantly
after two additions (60 minutes of incubation time) and even more after three additions (90 minutes
of incubation time).
The absorbance was almost as low as the one obtained after 30 minutes of
incubation with the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2.
Thereby, the substrate solution
could act as a regeneration solution and actually regenerate the setup by removing the tracer over
multiple additions and 90 minutes of incubation.
In the search for literature, describing a TMB-H2O2 solution with a more neutral pH, it was found
that the HRP enzyme actually can shift pH optimum to a more acidic pH range, when the H2O2
concentration is increased in the substrate solution. It was demonstrated that the highest HRP enzyme
activity was achieved with a H2O2 of 1.3 mmol/L and at a pH range between 3.8 and 4.4 [75], which
is a lower optimal pH than found in other sources. This could mean that the substrate solution could
be optimised, and simultaneously optimise the HRP enzyme performance. However, performing the
assay at this pH range could inhibit the antigen-antibody binding and regenerate the setup by cleaving
oﬀthe HRP enzyme, while the detection is happening. In a ﬂow system, this would mean that the
HRP enzyme could be washed away before it has converted the substrate into product. Nonetheless,
this could change the view of the incubation time when using the electrochemical detection, since it
could be possible to only incubate a few minutes at a low pH and still get an acceptable signal without
inhibiting or regenerating the setup. This has to be investigated and validated.
4.2
Electrochemical Detection - Cortisol and Alternariol
In this section all of the results for the electrochemical detection are presented including the clean-
ing eﬀect by plasma treatment, modiﬁcation characterised by impedance measurements and cyclic
voltammetry, maximum signal and analyte exposure in a competitive assay detected by absorbance
and amperometry. As the area of the electrode chamber is of diﬀerent size compared to the wells in
a 96-well plate used in ELISA and since protein G is bound diﬀerently, the results cannot directly be
correlated. It is important to note that these experiments were performed to prove the concept of the
project, and are not optimised.
4.2.1
The Eﬀect of Electrode Cleaning
The plasma treatment with air plasma was used to clean the electrodes before the modiﬁcation. The
electrochemical methods impedance spectroscopy and cyclic voltammetry were used to characterise
the cleaning process from only cleaning the chip with acetone, ethanol (70%) and MilliQwater to use
of plasma treatment for 1 run (16 minutes) and 4 runs (64 minutes). The electrode cleaning is an
important step, as it sets the starting conditions for the construction of the biointerface.
Page 74 of 151
==PAGE:80==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
General Approach for All Electrodes
To show the eﬀect of the plasma treatment compared to cleaning with acetone, ethanol (70%) and
MilliQwater and see the eﬀect between how long the electrode was plasma treated, the impedance
spectra and CVs are presented for one working electrode (WEc) on one chip (02) in Figure 28 and
Figure 29, respectively, and the CV parameters are summarised in Table 7.
Figure 28
Impedance spectra (Nyquist plots) evaluation of electrode cleaning (chip 02, WEc). The eﬀect
of the diﬀerent cleaning methods: 1) Acetone, ethanol and MilliQwater, 2) Plasma treatment 1 run (16 minutes) and 3)
Plasma treatment 4 run (64 minutes). The impedance spectra were acquired by using Ferri-Ferrocyanide.
It can be seen that the charge transfer resistance, Rct, (diameter of the semicircle) is decreasing during
the cleaning process from around 5 kW after cleaning with acetone, ethanol (70%) and MilliQwater
to around 1 kW after using plasma treatment for 16 minutes to around 0.4 kW after using plasma
treatment for 64 minutes.
It can be seen from the CVs in Figure 29 that the shape of the CVs is getting better, i.e. the peak
values are getting more identical and that the peak to peak separation decreases after each cleaning
process, the details of which are presented in Table 7.

It can be conﬁrmed that 1 run of plasma cleaning (16 minutes) is improving the response in both the

However, it can be seen that 4 runs of plasma cleaning (64 minutes) improved the results even further,
but the optimal time for the plasma treatment probably lies in between and if known could save
time and energy. The general trend for all the electrodes was that they were cleaned very well when
using plasma treatment for 64 minutes. These results indicate that the plasma cleaning is working as
expected and that electrodes are as clean as possible before the modiﬁcation. Plasma treatment for
64 minutes was chosen as the cleaning method, but in one case (chip 09) the plasma treatment was
increased to 80 minutes, as the cleaning was not proper at only 64 minutes.

==PAGE:82==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
4.2.2
Biological Modiﬁcation of the Biosensor Setup on the Electrode
This experiment follows the modiﬁcation to make sure that the setup is constructed correctly. Impedance
measurements were used as the main method for characterising the modiﬁcations of the biosensor
setup. However, impedance, speciﬁcally Rct, does not always increase for each modiﬁcation, even
though when more elements are immobilized on an electrode, it could be expected to increase. This
is due to that impedance is also aﬀected by how many negatively charged groups that are present. If
there is an excess of positive charge present, the Rct can actually decrease due to the modiﬁcation.
What is important is that one sees a change. Cyclic voltammetry was also used to characterise elec-
trode modiﬁcations, since the current peaks of CVs tend to decrease when the number of modiﬁcation
layers increases. CVs are also good when evaluating if the system works as intended in voltammetric
detection, as the CV is plotting the oxidation and reduction current on the working electrode. This
experiment had the purpose of recording every step and seeing how the impedance spectra and CVs
change after each modiﬁcation. It has to be noted that documenting each step can have a negative
eﬀect on the overall performance of the electrode.
Due to the fabrication of the chips and the PMMA holder, the modiﬁcation is done on all the electrodes
(counter, reference and working electrodes) and the surrounding area, as the chamber in the PMMA
holder is designed to display all electrodes and as the silicon and electrodes (counter and reference)
are not manufactured with a protein repellent resist. This means that the entire area that is exposed
to the modiﬁcation can be modiﬁed, and not only the working electrodes, which is what is actually
desired. This has the consequence, when test controlling the modiﬁcation through absorbance and
comparing the absorbance with the results from electrochemical detection, the absorbance is measured
for the whole area in the chamber, whereas electrochemical detection only responds to the area of the
chosen working electrode. Thereby, the absorbance can be used as an indication, but not as a precise
quantitative value for only the working electrodes. This problem is not optimised in this project,
but has to be considered if the project is taken further.
It was also observed that especially the
impedance diﬀers between the working electrodes, which could be due to each modiﬁcation on the
working electrodes not being identical in relation to how many MPA-linkers and proteins that are put
on the surface, which can be seen in the following results and in Appendix A.7 Modiﬁcation of Chip
01 (WE a,b,c), Chip 02 (WE a,b,c) and Chip 03 (WE a,c) for Cortisol Detection - Impedance spectra
and CVs and in Appendix A.8 Modiﬁcation of Chip 07 (WE a,b,c), Chip 08 (WE a,b,c), Chip 09
(WE a,c) and Chip 10 (WE a,b,c) for Alternariol Detection - Impedance spectra and CVs.
Results for Cortisol
To get some practice and to test out the protocol for the modiﬁcation, the biological elements for
cortisol biosensor were used before experimenting with the biological elements for alternariol biosensor
as was done in the ELISA experiments.

1/320 ∼3.125 µg/ml (300 µL), antibody (10-C30D) concentration of 1/800 ∼1.25 µg/mL (100 µL)
and tracer (65-IC08, C-HRP) concentration 1/3000 ∼0.33 µg/mL (100 µL). After preparation, all

Results for Alternariol
As the modiﬁcation protocol showed good results for construction of the cortisol biosensor, the same
protocol was used for the electrode modiﬁcations for construction of the alternariol biosensor.
Modiﬁcation of Chip 07, 08, 09 and 10 and End-point Absorbance Measurement

ensure saturation together with 200 µL of tracer. The solution for protein G and the antibody were
old preparations, which for the antibody was due to a low total volume of the remaining antibody
stock. However, the tracer solution was freshly prepared. All the used biological solutions were stored
at 4°C. Each modiﬁcation step was characterised and washing by vacuum suction was applied.

Even though every modiﬁcation step was characterised, the end-point absorbance was very high (see

conformational changes to the protein, when bound to it, which causes the protein to be released
from the well in ELISA, as the binding by physisorption is not as strong as the covalent bonding.
However, to validate this hypothesis one has to investigate the surface when modifying. What can be
established from the results is that the biological elements for AOH are performing better on the gold
electrode than in the 96-well plate, which is very positive in the light of using the biological elements
for a biosensor detecting alternariol.
4.2.3
Biosensor Detection - Reproducibility, Regeneration and Analyte Detection
In the industrial detection, it is not necessary to reuse the chip, but what is important is that the
diﬀerent chips behave identically so that the output can be trusted when applying diﬀerent chips
each time.
However, for the biosensor system, it is necessary to have a calibration curve, as the
system requires a reference when determining unknown toxin concentrations in the product samples. A
calibration curve depends on repeatable data points and a stable electrode behaviour when regenerated,
and therefore is one of the goals to understand the trend of the electrode behaviour. Here absorbance
of the formed colour was widely used as it is an easy and tangible method.
This experimental setup was implemented so that detection is done at the modiﬁed electrodes where
the aﬃnity based biorecognition occurs. However, in many ﬂow systems, the site for biorecognition
and detection are separated, so that product formation is made in one chamber and the formed
product is transferred to the electrode located in another chamber, where the detection happens. This
divided system has its advantages and disadvantages, as the modiﬁcation cannot block the access of
the electrochemically active product from the electrode surface, but if the substrate has regeneration
properties, it can release tracer into the liquid causing the enzyme reaction to continue when the liquid
is transferred to the detection chamber. This can give variation in the results even in an automatic
system. However, what is important is to keep the standard deviations at a minimum. It is expected
that all chips do not have a good continuous signal range or continuous stability throughout the
Page 84 of 151
==PAGE:90==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
multiple regeneration runs, as the pH of the regeneration solution can be harmful for the biointerface.
It is therefore also expected that the standard deviations could be quite large.
However, around
10% is considered acceptable, as it is generally hard to get repeatable results from immunobiosensors.
The absorbance of the formed colour is mainly to check the system and how it is behaving before
introducing electrochemical detection.
Reproducibility and Biointerface Behaviour when Regenerated
The goal for these experiments was to get repeatable data and try to understand how the biointerface
behaves when using the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 and the substrate solution (pH
5.6), as the substrate can act as a light regeneration solution. The aim was to determine a trend of
stability and how the chips are regenerated in the best way and if there is a general method that is
applicable for all analytes.
Results for Cortisol - Chips 01, 02, 03, 04, 05 and 06
The biological elements for the cortisol biosensor, were also used to experiment on, before transferring
it to the biological elements needed for alternariol biosensing.
First, the washing with pipette was investigated to get repeatable maximum absorbance to evaluate
the method. Since the end-point absorbance was very low after the modiﬁcation of chip 01, 02 and
03, it was decided to increase the amount of the tracer to 200 µL instead of 100 µL, however the
concentration of 1/3000 ∼0.33 µg/mL remained. As the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2
was working in the ELISA experiments, it was also used to regenerate the setup on the electrodes.
Hence, the tracer was removed and the antibody and protein G were left on the electrode surface.
After the regeneration (reg. 1), new tracer (200 µL) and substrate were added and the absorbance
was measured. The absorbances are presented in Table 14. It can be observed that the absorbance
increased a lot, when a larger amount of tracer was used, which could indicate that there were more
binding sites available on the antibodies when only using 100 µL tracer (conc. 1/3000). To see the
eﬀect of the washing with a pipette, the setup was regenerated again (reg. 2) and new tracer (200 µL)
and substrate were added. These results are also represented in Table 14 for comparison.
Table 14

from 100 µL to 200 µL and the eﬀect of the washing with a pipette when comparing repeatable maximum absorbance.

between the chips in each experiment, as the RSD were under 10% for experiment 1 and experiment

a trend of an increased absorbance after the regeneration, the baseline for the regeneration solution
0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2. was checked, as it was suspected that the increased absorbance could be due
to a incomplete regeneration. However, to get the baseline, substrate needs to be added twice and is
expected to have an inﬂuence on the end-point absorbance. The baseline for the regeneration solution

The baseline for the regeneration of all the chips were almost around zero absorbance (background
absorbance is normally around 0.050), thereby removing almost every tracer from the setup. However,
this did not explain the large RSD in the experiment.
After this experiment it was not possible to restore the maximum absorbance (data not shown). It is
believed to be due to the long regeneration time with both the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl
pH 2.2 and substrate solution for over an hour had not only removed all the tracer, but also some of
the antibodies. It was therefore decided to add antibody (conc. of 1/800 ∼1.25 µg/mL, 200 µL) to
all the chips over night. Luckily, it was possible to restore the signal with almost the same absorbance
values. To eliminate the tracer being the problem or the limitation, a new solution was prepared with
an increased concentration 1/1000 ∼1 µg/mL, same amount added. Even though the experiment
was unplanned, observations could be retrieved from it. When comparing the results gained from the
maximum absorbance when using the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 and the regained
absorbance value when antibody was added anew, it can be seen that chip 01 had a better RSD (9.878
%), chip 02 still had the tendency to have an increased absorbance after each regeneration, and chip
03 had a very ﬂuctuating behaviour. The results are presented in Table 18 together with the relative
standard deviation in percentage (RSD %).

It was observed that the substrate solution had a similar regeneration eﬀect as the regeneration solution
0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2, which was an important information as it means that the substrate alone can
be used to regenerate the setup, which both saves time and material as the whole process with 0.4 M
gly-HCl pH 2.2 could be excluded. From the previous results, it can been seen that regenerating the
setup with only substrate solution gave better repeatable results than when using the regeneration
solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 together with the substrate, but the reason is unknown. However,
since the substrate solution could be used as the regeneration solution, it is possible to optimise the
procedure. It would be beneﬁcial for the biosensor if the electrode could be reused.
It was decided to test if the substrate could create a stable baseline with an absorbance close to
zero and regain a stable maximum absorbance, as this procedure then could be used to create the
calibration curve. However, it was believed that the chips 01, 02 and 03 could have been damaged
during the experiment, and that this experiment had to be performed on new chips. Chips 04, 05 and
06 were used for this purpose. For this experiment, the tracer amount was decreased to 100 µL, as it
was proved on the chips 04, 05 and 06, that the setup could be saturated almost as well as with 200
µl when using the tracer concentration of 1/1000 ∼1 µg/mL.

From the results, it can be seen that the substrate had the ability to regenerate the setup down to an
average absorbance around 0.100, which is quite good. It can be seen that the maximum absorbance
could be restored for all the chips with a RSD (on average) of around 10% after an almost completed
regeneration with only the substrate solution. For the baseline, the absorbance tended to increase
with the number of experiments for each chip which also lead to a high RSD for chip 04 and 05 on
around 22%. It can be seen that chip 06 had a great score through all three experiments with a RSD
for the maximum absorbance on 5.466% and a RSD for the baseline absorbance 4.055%. However,
the substrate solution only regenerated the chip to an absorbance around 0.150 and not to around
0.050, which was the desired value. This experiment proved that the substrate solution could almost
completely regenerate the setup for cortisol and restore the maximum absorbance. As this regeneration
procedure worked well for the experiment with cortisol, this procedure was used for to regenerate the
setup for alternariol detection.

for alternariol biosensing or if it could have a harming eﬀect. Only one chip was selected for the
experiment, as it was believed that the regeneration solution could harm the setup. The regeneration
solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 was added after the baseline was deﬁned. Hence, the chip had already
undergone substrate addition twice for 30 minutes. Therefore, this experiment could not deﬁne the
speciﬁc eﬀect for only the regeneration solution. However, it was expected that the ﬁnal absorbance
value from this experiment would be around zero. The regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2
was added (30 minutes) followed by the substrate solution (30 minutes) and the maximum absorbance

From these results, it can be seen that it was very diﬃcult to regenerate the setup for alternariol
biosensing compared to the setup for cortisol biosensing.
However, it appeared, though, that the
regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 did not have any harmful eﬀect as the maximum absorbance
was restored to around the same absorbance value, which was positive. In general, the regeneration
strategy with the substrate solution and the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 worked
diﬀerently when using diﬀerent biological elements. The reason why is unknown.
Maximum Signal and Analyte Exposure by Electrochemical Detection and Absorbance
The maximum signal and later analyte exposure were detected by amperometry, which is suitable for
detecting the continuous reaction of product formation. The product formation continues since the
enzyme reaction cannot be stopped without destroying the biointerface of the electrode. It is expected
that the product, TMBox, from the enzymatic reaction is reduced on the working electrode generating
the current in the negative range when the potential -0.15 is applied. The output is expected to have a
current peak, when the substrate solution is injected on the electrode using a pipette, and then settle
afterwards to a value that corresponds to the amount of TMBox present. It is expected that when
the setup is exposed to the analyte and thereby conducting a competitive assay with the tracer, the
settling current will be less negative, as there is less TMBox in the solution. As it is hard to determine
a correct value from these outputs, the proof of concept of the biosensor is based on the view and
comparison of the outputs. The electrochemical detection with amperometry was only done for the
alternariol biosensor setup as it was the main focus of the project. The aim of this experiment was
to prove the concept of the biosensor and deﬁne the maximum signal, the trend and the behaviour
when exposed to the analyte. As it was proved to be diﬃcult to regenerate the setup for alternariol
biosensing, determination of the maximum signal by electrochemical detection and a full regeneration
were ﬁrst done on chip 10, as it was the one with the highest maximum absorbance. This experiment
would give an idea of the possibilities for a calibration curve.
Page 91 of 151
==PAGE:97==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Results for Alternariol - Chip 10
The main strategy was to ﬁrst measure the maximum signal by electrochemical detection to have the
reference where only tracer was introduced. Afterwards, the setup was attempted to be regenerated
and a competitive assay was introduced, where the setup was exposed to the analyte. The absorbance
was measured parallel with the electrochemical detection to have a reference that gives a picture of
what was happening on the entire chip, as amperometry only detected what was happening at the
chosen working electrode. The absorbance was also used during the regeneration to see the progress
and the maximum absorbance was also measured again after amperometry to see its eﬀect.
One
working electrode (WEb) in the middle of the chip was chosen to prove the concept. The maximum
signal measured by electrochemical detection for the working electrode WEb over multiple runs is
presented in Figure 34 together with the measured absorbance during the amperometry in Table 23.

Firstly, it can be seen that the runs I and II have a more unstable curve than the other runs, which
makes it diﬃcult to see a correct settlement. However, at around 400 seconds both runs (I and II)
do have a current of around -0.5 µA. In general, when observing all the runs with their respective
absorbance values there is a trend of decreased signal for the working electrode and absorbance for the
chip with the increased number of runs. Hence, the tracer may have been removed by the substrate
and washed away between the runs.
However, the tracer removal was random, as the decreased
amperometric measurement (see Figure 34) was not correlated with the overall drop in absorbance
for the entire chip (see Table 23). To see if it was possible to restore the maximum signal after the
loss of tracer during the multiple amperometry measurements, tracer was added for 15 minutes and
the maximum signal was again measured by amperometry. The results are presented in Figure 35
together with the measured absorbance after each amperometry in Table 24.

It can be seen that the maximum signal got restored and that the output had a more stable curve.
However, the absorbance value measured after amperometry was not restored, which supports the
fact that the electrochemical detection only detected on the chosen working electrode, whereas the
absorbance measurement gives the output for the entire chip. If the two measurements should correlate,
the design of the PMMA holder should be changed. After this the maximum absorbance for the chip
was tested to see if the biointerface had been damaged during the amperometric measurements. The
absorbance was measured to 1.179, which was bit lower than the average maximum absorbance of
1.344 for chip 10. This could be due to the tracer incubation was only 15 minutes and not 30 minutes.

The reason why is
unknown. The regeneration process was stopped at the absorbance 0.220, as it seemed to be the

last measurement also had an eﬀect.
A competitive assay with the analyte exposure was performed even though some tracer remained and
could interfere with the results, thereby having an advantage in the competitive assay. The aim was
to ﬁnd a middle point for a possible calibration curve and the analyte concentration was determined
based on the ELISA experiment to be 16 nM. The procedure was done in accordance with ELISA,

where the analyte and tracer was injected 50/50, hence 100 µL analyte solution and 100 µL tracer

signal caused by the analyte exposure. The end-point absorbance of the competitive assay after the
amperometric measurement was also determined for comparison. The result from the competitive

Even though the tracer had an advantage the analyte with the concentration of 16 nM decreased the
signal signiﬁcantly from around -0.5 µA to around -0.16 µA at 400 seconds. The absorbance for the
entire chip after the amperometry did however increase compared to the maximum absorbance, which
was unexpected, but supports that the absorbance for the entire chip did not correlate with the area
of the working electrode. The reason why is unknown. The same trend with the decreased signal after
multiple amperometric measurements did also appear here. However it is believed that both the tracer
and analyte were removed equally during the runs. The end-point absorbance after the amperometric
measurement of the competitive assay was determined to be 0.636, which indicates a reduction of 46%.
This experiment did prove the concept that the electrochemical signal was decreased when exposed
to the analyte alternariol in a competitive assay with the tracer.

Results for Alternariol - Chip 09
Chip 09 was chosen to attempt to create a calibration curve with regeneration containing at least three
points: one point of the maximum signal dominated by the tracer, one point of a competitive assay
containing 50/50 tracer and analyte and one point of almost no signal dominated by the analyte.
Before the regeneration and data creating for the calibration curve, one experiment was conducted to
investigate if there was any variation in the results when using the manual injection by pipette even
though it was the same pipette and the same person who performed the injection. The comparison of
the maximum signals by amperometry for chip 09 WEb are presented in Figure 38.
Page 97 of 151

Amperometry outputs and comparison for the maximum signal to view the variation in manual

to view the variation in manual injection even when the same pipette and person performed the injection.
It can be observed that the manual injection has its variations, especially in the current peak imme-
diately after the injection. However, the end current almost reached the same value between 400 and
500 seconds. This variation could be due to the substrate amount varying when manual injected, as a
droplet could get stuck in the pipette. This points to that an automatic system is preferred, so these
variations could be eliminated.
