Figure 39
Amperometry outputs for a calibration curve with maximum signal and analyte exposure (chip
09, WEb). The calibration curve for WEb on chip 09 by amperometric detection, where the maximum signal was tested
before and after the analyte exposure. The working electrode was exposed to ﬁve diﬀerent competitive assays where the
analyte concentration (AOH) increased and the tracer (T) concentration decreased.
In general, this experiment was a long process with at least 16 hours of incubation time, where the
regeneration process varied a lot. It was observed that the regeneration was easier or that the absorbance
dropped drastically, when the chip had been standing with PBS overnight. This could be due to the
time having an inﬂuence, but it could also be due to the PMMA holder possibly had been opened and
the PMMA holes cleaned with tissue paper, since the risk of a small leakage during the night was high.
The only record of this is between the dates 11.12.20 and 14.12.20. It was suspected that the tracer and
analyte were not washed away by PBST, but were more likely adhering on the insides of the PMMA
well or other places, as the analyte is highly hydrophobic. The Log P value of the analyte could also
be an explanation on the substrate regeneration properties, as the biosensor for cortisol could easily
be regenerated by the substrate whereas the biosensor for alternariol could not. Hence, that the Log
Page 101 of 151
==PAGE:107==
RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
P value could have an inﬂuence on the regeneration process, as it can aﬀect the washing. This could
also explain the unrestored maximum signal, as the analyte was not washed away and was blocking the
recognition sites for the tracer added anew. It is not only the buﬀer solution PBST and the regeneration
solutions that have to be investigated, but also the material used to make the PMMA holder to ensure
that the material can repel the analyte.
It can be seen that the calibration curve obtained with amperometry did not match with the calibration
curve obtained in ELISA, as the high analyte concentrations of 2 µM and 10 µM in combination with
1/3000 tracer should not have given any signal at all. However, these small signals at -0.035 µA and
-0.025 µA could be due to the starting advantage that the tracer had, as the absorbance could only
be regenerated to around 0.140 on average. To illuminate this point the surface of the chip could be
investigated before and during the competitive assay.
What can be observed from this experiment is the decreased signal as the analyte concentration was
increased, which proves the potential of the biosensor, as the setup and signal are aﬀected by the
analyte alternariol being present. However, it can be seen that there is a need for more experiments,
as the problems of optimal concentrations for a balanced competitive assay together with the possible
hydrophobic problem in the washing step are not solved. To make a good biosensor without taking the
sample matrix into account, these issues have to be investigated and resolved. A practical issue is the
measuring time. It can be seen for most of the amperometry measurements that the curve is rather
stable within the 500 seconds, but for the ’maximum signal before the exposure’, the curve is not as
stable as desired within 500 seconds. This could indicate that the measuring time should be longer than
500 seconds.
Page 102 of 151
==PAGE:108==
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
5
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
In this work, new commercial biological elements for alternariol i.e. antibody and tracer, were tested
and used to create an immunobiosensor for the detection of alternariol in food samples where the focus
was on tomato products. However, this biosensor could be used for various food groups if the right
sample handling approaches are found for the given products. As the biological elements for alternariol
were new, quite expensive and had not been tested in any scientiﬁc research before, a highly known
similar analyte, cortisol, with associated antibody and tracer was used as a reference and to test
out the protocols and experiments before transferring it to alternariol detection. To have a reference
analyte was important as the methods used could be evaluated for diﬀerent analytes involving the
various behaviours of the biological elements. The thesis had the focus of making a basic tests with
ELISA and afterwards try out the biological elements on electrode chips. It appears that the behaviour
in the two approaches were quite diﬀerent. The focus was on deﬁning the parameters, such as the
sensitivity, i.e., IC50 and the limit of detection (LOD), together with the matrix eﬀect of diﬀerent
preparations and the location of the analyte alternariol in the product sample of tomato juice. The
biosensor setup was tested electrochemically. The modiﬁcations were characterised with impedance
spectroscopy and cyclic voltammetry. The maximum signal together with an analyte exposure in a
competitive assay were tested by amperometry to see the concept and try to form a calibration curve.
This project served as a proof of concept and a lot of interesting research were discovered during the
thesis work. However, there is still a long way to go in the development of an industrial biosensor on
the market.
In this research, experiments were carried out with ELISA to ﬁnd the optimal antibody- and tracer
concentrations or dilutions and to see if a speciﬁc antibody orientation using protein G compared to a
random orientation using physisorption in the 96-well plate had an eﬀect on the eﬃciency of the ELISA
setup. When using random orientation, the results for both cortisol and alternariol in ELISA were not
remarkable, but did, however, indicate that the biological elements for alternariol were not as active as
the biological elements for cortisol. When shifted to speciﬁc orientation with protein G, it was possible
to conﬁrm that the protein improved the results especially for cortisol, whereas the improvement was
not as signiﬁcant for alternariol, meaning that the orientation of the antibodies could have an impact.
For cortisol, it meant that lower antibody- and tracer concentrations could be used in conjunction with
protein G. For alternariol protein G mostly increased the overall absorbance signal and did not lower
the required antibody concentration signiﬁcantly. What was interesting was the diﬀerence between
the improvement for the two analytes, as it seems like there is something still limiting the assay for
alternariol, that is not limiting the assay for cortisol. For alternariol the limitation could be due to
low activity of the biological elements or poor binding aﬃnity of the antibody, which only could be
solved in the production stage. However, protein G did enhance the results. Therefore, protein G was
considered as a central part of the biosensor setup.
To ﬁnd the sensitivity of the biosensor setup in the form of IC50 and the LOD, the analyte calibration
curve was produced, and the vision was that the setup could fulﬁl the given criteria for alternariol
from the European commission of 10 µg/kg ∼38.72 nM. The setup for cortisol had an IC50 of 1.463
Page 103 of 151
==PAGE:109==
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
nM and a LOD of 0.207 nM, which veriﬁes an overly sensitive setup that can determine low analyte
concentrations. For alternariol, the same trend was observed with a low maximum absorbance, and
the analyte calibration curve shifted to the right compared to cortisol, which meant an increased IC50
of 10.71 nM and a LOD of 2.155 nM, which indicate that the setup was less sensitive. The cortisol
setup was actually seven times more sensitive than the setup for alternariol in ELISA, but the assay
in buﬀer solution did still fulﬁl the criteria. However, this result was a bit worrying when the matrix
from a tomato product had to be introduced, as the normal tendency of the matrix was to decrease the
sensitivity even more. The preparation of the tomato juice was important to investigate and evaluate
its eﬀect. Especially the eﬀect of the particles, as they could aﬀect the measurements in various ways,
but in particular since the particles could attract the analyte, as the analyte AOH is hydrophobic.
This could have an inﬂuence, if the preparation involved a centrifugation or ﬁltration step. The key
factor for a correct measurement of the toxin is to know where it is located in the product sample. This
means that the biosensor would be useless if the right preparation were not found. The ﬁrst chosen
preparation method was 1:10 dilution of tomato juice with buﬀer as both the liquid and particles
were present and the eﬀect of both could be investigated. It was also chosen since it was the simplest
variant with no pH adjustment or other operations needed. However, the dilution could bring the
analyte concentration under the LOD and give false negatives if the biosensor is not sensitive enough.
This indicates that the preparation method also highly depends on the sensitivity of the biosensor.
The matrix eﬀect of the diluted tomato juice was not a problem for the setup with cortisol, as the
IC50 and LOD remained the same and only aﬀected the maximum absorbance with a reduction of
23%. Reasons for this are many. However, it is believed to be due to, as other scientiﬁc research
has hypothesized, that the reduced absorbance is caused by inhibition of the enzyme and its activity.
The compounds responsible could be heavy metals, as tomato products have diﬀerent heavy metals
in their contents and as the metals are proven to exert an inhibitory eﬀect represented as toxicity in
their interactions with proteins including enzymes. It is proved in literature that heavy metals can
aﬀect the enzyme without aﬀecting the antigen-antibody complex, as the IC50 was not aﬀected. It
is however believed that there are several reasons for the reduced absorbance, as a reduction of 23%
is a signiﬁcant amount when the metals are reduced 10 times. It has been seen in literature that an
eﬀective strategy used naturally by plants to counter various metals’ inhibitory eﬀects is an increase
in peroxidase expression as a defensive response to the decreased signal [33]. If the HRP can tolerate
the heavy metals, the preparation could stay simple as the heavy metals do not have to be removed.
As the particles were worth exploring, the particle eﬀect was investigated, and the results were unex-
pected, as the supernatant and ﬁltered supernatant from tomato juice had the highest matrix eﬀect.
This was suspected to be due to no dilution and due to that the force used in the centrifugation
could release compounds like the heavy metals from the particles, which was backed up by scientiﬁc
research. This suggests that the preparation methods should avoid involving centrifugation and ﬁl-
tration, as it is showed to have a negative inﬂuence if the product matrix was discarded without any
extraction of the analyte. The tomato juice diluted 1:10 with buﬀer had, however, a great impact
on the setup for alternariol, as the analysis of the data gave an IC50 of 28.269 nM, which is 3 times
higher than the assay performed in pure buﬀer solution. However, the LOD did decrease to 0.874
Page 104 of 151
==PAGE:110==
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
nM, which was abnormal as the usual trend is that when IC50 increases, the LOD increases as well.
This experiment was believed to be unbalanced and inﬂuenced by mathematical artefacts, which gave
incorrect results, as it is believed that a loss of the analyte caused disturbance in the assay and did
not give a suﬃcient indication. This indicated, however, two things: that the two analytes behaved
diﬀerently in the same matrix and a possible location of the alternariol analyte. The diﬀerences are
believed to be due to the Log P value, as the value is higher for AOH, indicating that the attraction
to the particles is higher. This was only found out since the diluted tomato juice was spiked with
alternariol before running the experiment. Even though, the eﬀect of the matrix was inconclusive,
the experiment was highly important, as a possible location of the analyte was found, which is a very
important factor. The analyte location together with the particle eﬀect was investigated further with
the ﬁltered supernatant of tomato juice and the experiment gave useful data and had an IC50 of 9.862
nM and a LOD of 1.446 nM, even though the absorbance was reduced as expected. This contributed
to the hypothesis that the particles in tomato juice could attract the analyte alternariol and traps it
within their matrix, concluding that the preparation has a great impact on the ﬁnal alternariol con-
centration. As the experiments are spiked with analyte after the preparation has happened, it cannot
be concluded where the analyte is located in the sample or how the preparation method is aﬀecting the
analyte concentration, but only give a hint of where it most probably is and how the matrix made by
the diﬀerent preparations aﬀects the biosensor setup. To determine this, the sample should be spiked
before the preparation, as that would be a more realistic picture of where the analyte would be in a
real product sample and how the preparation is aﬀecting the ﬁnal concentration. Other experiments
could also be made to investigate, which compounds are present in the solution and test their eﬀect
on the biological elements. This analysis has to be made for every product, even diﬀerent tomato
products, to evaluate the eﬀect of the matrix. Every product matrix can inﬂuence the biosensor dif-
ferently. The toxin detection is only made for the ﬁnal processed tomato product, but maybe it would
be good to know if the mass of peeled tomatoes is contaminated before using them and making them
into a product. Hence, the matrix eﬀect for raw tomatoes also has to be investigated. The matrix
eﬀect and the analyte location are key to a good biosensor and to have a correct indication of them,
the matrix should be investigated more. The regeneration process was also investigated by ELISA,
as the output is easier to determine in this method as compared to using electrochemical detection.
The experiment had the purpose of determining where the regeneration solution could cleave as it
has great inﬂuence on how strenuous the adding process will be after the regeneration. Using ELISA
for this purpose also has its downsides as the protein G is bound diﬀerently in the well compared
to the electrode, meaning that acid could have diﬀerent eﬀects depending on the experiment. The
results showed that the primary target for the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 was the
antigen-antibody complex when 30 minutes of incubation was used. In this process, the substrate also
showed regenerative properties and could regenerate the setup for cortisol quite well after 60 minutes
of incubation. It was concluded that both the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 and the
substrate solution could be used to regenerate the setup for cortisol and thereby also for alternariol,
as it was assumed to have the same eﬀect on various antigen-antibody complexes. The regeneration
process is not optimised, and both the solution and the incubation time can be investigated.
Page 105 of 151
==PAGE:111==
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
In the experiments done with electrochemical detection and absorbance as a reference, the ﬁrst part
was the cleaning process and its eﬀect.
The used cleaning method was air plasma treatment for
minimum 64 minutes, which had a great eﬀect on the electrodes compared to cleaning the electrodes
with a combination of acetone, ethanol and MilliQwater resulting in a small charge transfer resistance
in impedance measurements and a peak to peak separation of around 100 mV in cyclic voltammetry.
The biological modiﬁcation of the chips was done for both cortisol and alternariol, but the ﬁnal results
turned the view around as the data for alternariol performed better than cortisol when modiﬁed on the
gold electrode compared to the 96-well plate, as the end-point absorbance for the chips was higher.
In general, the modiﬁcation went well for both analytes and their associated biological elements.
However, the modiﬁcation behaved a bit diﬀerently, as the modiﬁcation for protein G did not exhibit
as signiﬁcant increases in charge transfer resistance for alternariol as compared to cortisol. This is
believed to be variance that was not related to the biological elements, but more a variance in the
detection, as the used protein G solution was the same. The better performance for alternariol could,
however, be due to how protein G was bound to the surface, as it is covalently bound on the electrode
compared to being bound by physisorption in ELISA. One hypothesis could be that the antibody for
alternariol induces conformational changes in protein G when the two connect, which may result in
release of protein G if it is bound by physisorption. Hence, the covalent binding made between the
gold electrode and protein G is stronger, being able to withstand when the antibody is bound to it.
This presumption is not validated, as the surface of the electrode has not been investigated in detail.
It could be established, however, that the biological elements for alternariol had a better performance
and exhibited higher activity with protein G on a gold electrode than in a 96-well plate, which in the
light of a future biosensor is very positive.
The reproducibility and behaviour of the biointerface when this was regenerated were also investigated
using absorbance measurements, as it is important for a possible calibration curve to have a low
relative standard deviation and precise signal repetition. It was possible to get repeatable maximum
signals with an RSD under 10% for the cortisol chips when only applying tracer after a regeneration
with substrate for 30 minutes. However, the RSD was over 10% when the regeneration solution 0.4
M gly-HCl pH 2.2 was used together with the substrate and an increased trend in the absorbance
was observed. The reason was believed to be incomplete regeneration. To check this, the baseline
absorbance after application of the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 was tested and found
to be quite low, indicating that the solution anyhow caused a complete regeneration after 30 minutes
with a followed two additions of substrate for 30 minutes. The reason for the large RSD is unknown.
As the maximum absorbance could not be restored, it was suspected that the long regeneration time of
1.5 h had disturbed the biointerface and removed not only the tracer but also the antibody. However,
the restored maximum absorbance, after incubating with antibody solution overnight, did give a
better picture of the trend, as one chip got a better RSD, another chip had the tendency to have an
increased absorbance after each regeneration, and the last chip had a very ﬂuctuating behaviour. As
the regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 seemed to have a “bad” inﬂuence on the biointerface
for cortisol detection, the regeneration eﬀect of the substrate was investigated with good results, as
the substrate alone could regenerate the chip to almost zero absorbance. Hence, the substrate can
Page 106 of 151
==PAGE:112==
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
regenerate the biosensor setup for cortisol and provide repeatable maximum absorbance with RSD
10%, although the baseline level varied from run to run. However, these experiments proved that
the substrate had the desired regeneration eﬀect with less damaging eﬀect on the biointerface for
cortisol. The same regeneration strategy was used on the biosensor setup with alternariol, but the
setup could not be regenerated by only applying the substrate solution. This proved that diﬀerent
biological elements for diﬀerent analytes behaved diﬀerently and can thereby not be regenerated by
the same procedure. The regeneration solution 0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 had to be tested on the setup,
but it could not regenerate the setup for alternariol detection in a combination with substrate solution
when they were applied once. This means that the regeneration strategy for alternariol biosensing had
to be a combination of the regeneration solution and the substrate solution added in multiple runs.
The maximum signal and later analyte exposure were investigated in terms of electrochemical detec-
tion by amperometry and by absorbance measurements, where a possible regeneration procedure was
tested. The main strategy was to measure the maximum signal to have a reference, whereafter the
setup was tried regenerated, down to an absorbance between 0.100 and 0.200, and a competitive assay
with analyte was introduced. The ﬁrst observation was that there was a trend of decreased signal for
the chosen working electrode and absorbance for the chip with increased number of runs of amperomet-
ric detection, as the tracer was being removed by the substrate solution and wash away between the
runs. It was observed, however, that the tracer removal was random, as the observed decreased amper-
ometric current response did not correlate with the decrease in absorbance. Another observation was
that the maximum signal could be restored, but that the absorbance could not, which proves that the
two measurement methods cannot correspond in the given design. The reason is that electrochemical
detection only addresses the chosen working electrode, whereas absorbance measurements address the
entire chip. The regeneration process was long with multiple incubations in the regeneration solution
0.4 M gly-HCl pH 2.2 and substrate solution (pH 5.6). It was hard to get the overall chip absorbance
down to zero absorbance, leaving the tracer with a start advantage in the competitive assay, which is
not optimal for a calibration curve. However, the purpose of this project was to prove the concept of
the biosensor and not optimise the conditions. It was proven that an analyte exposure decreased the
amperometric current response. The statistical variation in the amperometric detection between the
chips was also investigated, as the modiﬁcation could vary on the working electrode. Four diﬀerent
working electrodes (WEb), each on a diﬀerent chip, were tested. The variation in the maximum signal
was large in the maximum signal, indicating that the modiﬁcation of the working electrodes could
vary a lot and a batch of chips could have a huge variation after fabrication and modiﬁcation. A
potential calibration curve was generated, where an increasing exposure to the analyte was introduced
combined with a regeneration step in between. Again, the setup was not fully regenerated and the
overall average absorbance was on average around 0.140. This means that the tracer had a small
advantage in the assay. However, it was observed that the regeneration was easier when the chip had
been standing with PBS overnight, which could be either due to the incubation time or to the fact
that the PMMA holder possibly had been opened and the PMMA holes cleaned with tissue paper,
since the risk of a small leakage did appear quite often. This led to the suspicion that the tracer and
analyte were not washed away by PBST but had more likely been physisorbed on the sides of the
Page 107 of 151
==PAGE:113==
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
PMMA well or other places, as the analyte alternariol is highly hydrophobic. Furthermore, the Log P
value was also suspected of having an inﬂuence on the regeneration process, as the setup for cortisol
was more easily regenerated than the setup for alternariol. This was not clariﬁed, however, the reason
must be investigated. It could be seen that the calibration curve obtained with amperometry did not
match the calibration curve obtained in ELISA, but the concept of alternariol biosensor functioned as
expected, although it was tested only in buﬀer solution and not optimised.
This project showed that the commercial alternariol antibody and tracer from Abvigen Inc could be
used for a potential biosensor. However, considering the needed further development and optimisation,
it would be far in the future the biosensor could be on the industrial market. However, this project
could achieve its goal and be the starting point of the research leading to a potential alternariol
biosensor for applications in food industry. As this project had two analytes to investigate, it also
illuminated that even though the biosensor concept is universal, being able to utilise any antibody
and associated tracer, the regeneration process, behaviour of the analyte, and the localization of the
analyte in the product samples could diﬀer. In general, immunobiosensors have been investigated for
many years, but are still hard to develop as it is diﬃcult to make repeatable data with a low RSD with
these kinds of sensors. A biosensor setup like the one used in this project is not a new development.
However, the used biological elements for alternariol are new and have never been tested before in a
biosensor setup.
Page 108 of 151
==PAGE:114==
FUTURE PERSPECTIVE AND IMPLEMENTATION
6
FUTURE PERSPECTIVE AND IMPLEMENTATION
6.1
Industrial Biosensor-Based Monitoring
The goal for this project is to make the fundamental research that could be used in the development
of a fully automatic ﬂow-based biosensor system, that can be used and operated by anyone. The
ambition is that the system could automatically pick up the sample from any solution, prepare it and
perform the detection of the toxin within an acceptable time period. However, to achieve this goal a
lot of research has to be done for every part of the system, as each part, like sampling, sample handling
and detection, have to be studied and optimized to accomplish a good industrial biosensor. The most
crucial part is still, however, what sampling handling or preparation method that will contribute to
the most correct estimation of the toxin concentration and have less eﬀect on the biological elements.
The eﬀects of the sample matrix and the preparation method to segregate all of the toxin are the key
factors to develop a good biosensor. Unfortunately, diﬀerent product matrices can require diﬀerent
preparation approaches and thereby complicate the goal for an universal automated system, as each
product type and product brand diﬀer in content and structure.
Sampling and Sample Handling when used for Industrial Measurement
When measuring something industrially, the scale of the batch is always large, which means that the
sampling and sample handling also can become quite complicated, as the test should be representative
for the whole batch.
Sampling is about having a representative sample for the entire product volume tested and the
question is how to get that? This has to be investigated as the representative number of samples and
the volume of each have to be determined compared to the batch volume tested. Another question is
when to collect the samples as it depends on when the test is relevant for the industry. As the guideline
policy from the European commission states, a food group, such as ’processed tomato products’, could
refer to the processed tomatoes that are used for the production and not only for the ﬁnalised product
like tomato juice.
Thereby, it could be an advantage for the industry to test the raw materials
like the processed tomatoes before they are mixed into the ﬁnal product. This could save energy,
time, materials and money, as if the processed tomatoes are highly contaminated, the batch could
be eliminated before spending the eﬀorts of producing the end-product. Nonetheless, it is important
to remember that alternariol can be present in various food groups that could be used to make the
ﬁnalised product and the concentration of the toxin could therefore increase under production. This
means that the company has to test either every raw material before use or just the ﬁnal product with
the risk of discarding the batch if highly contaminated.
Sample handling is about how to isolate or extract the toxin most eﬀectively from the solid or
liquid part, and how to match the total extractable concentration with the sensitivity of the biosensor.
Sample handling can be determined by the available sensitivity of the biosensor or it can determine
which sensitivity the biosensor should achieve. As the maximum allowed concentration for processed
tomato products is 10 µg/kg, the measurement of the toxin has to be per kilogram. However, it is
important that the volume from the product is handleable for the biosensor. It is therefore essential to
Page 109 of 151
==PAGE:115==
FUTURE PERSPECTIVE AND IMPLEMENTATION
ﬁnd out where the analyte is located in the product and how to isolate it into an appropriate volume
that still has a toxin concentration in the analytical range of the biosensor. A rule of thumb here
could be that a higher sensitivity of the biosensor can decrease the volume needed for the detection,
as at least 1 kg of tomatoes with a high alternariol concentration is needed if the biosensor setup is
not sensitive enough.
The handling of the sample is deﬁned as a sort of pre-step, where the analyte is isolated or extracted
from the product, but the question is how to do this most eﬀectively and still have the simplicity
of the system in mind? How to get view of the potential toxicity of 1 kg product and how to bring
down the volume, but still keep the concentration over the limit of detection? To decide which sample
handling method that is the best to isolate this analyte, the ﬁrst questions to answer are how the toxin
is distributed in the tissue of the tomato and how to get the toxin out? The most common and highly
eﬀective way of extracting analytes is by an organic solvent, but due to the toxicity of the chemicals,
this project focused on how the matrix of a tomato product could aﬀect the biological elements in the
biosensor. This was done in order to see if it was possible for the biosensor to function even in the
presence of the matrix, as the future goal is to minimise the use of organic solvents and ﬁnd more
environmentally friendly extraction methods. However, by only using for example dilution, there is a
potential risk of getting too low a concentration of the toxin so that it gives false negatives and even if
it is a simple preparation, it also increases the volume. Experiments also proved that the toxin binds
to the particles in the tomato product, when it is only diluted, which causes the analyte cannot be
detected. To use an extraction pre-step seems beneﬁcial even if the process gets more complicated,
as the toxin can be extracted from the matrix e.g. particles and be pre-concentrated before entering
the biosensor. This ensures that both the volume can be decreased as the concentration of the toxin
is increased and that the matrix of the sample has no eﬀect on the biosensor setup or on the ﬁnal
toxin concentration. An extraction method such as Pressurized Hot Water Extraction (PHWE) could
be used, as the water transmutes into a non-polar state above a speciﬁc temperature and pressure,
making the water vapour able to extract non-polar/hydrophobic compounds like AOH. PHWE is a
feasible green extraction method which can trap the analyte and remove it from the sample matrix
[76]. Another method like solid phase extraction can also be used as the analyte can be trapped in
a column and be eluted into a smaller volume, hence extracting and concentrating of the analyte at
the same time. These methods can pre-concentrate the toxin and are more environmentally friendly
than using an organic solvent. However, the eﬀectiveness towards alternariol has to be investigated.
A pre-concentration step also depends on the activity and sensitivity of the biological elements used
in the biosensor, as it could be a necessary step if the sensitivity of the biosensor is low.
All parts including sampling, sample handling and detection have to match together, as the usability of
the biosensor is not suﬃcient without proper representative sampling and eﬀective sample handling.
It has to be mentioned that even though a pre-step with pre-concentration is implemented before
detection by the biosensor, this approach would most likely still facilitate faster detection than using
HPLC or another analytical method that requires well-equipped laboratory with skilled personnel.
Page 110 of 151
==PAGE:116==
FUTURE PERSPECTIVE AND IMPLEMENTATION
Implementation and Use of the Biosensor at the Factory
What the industry is looking for is a ’point of care’ sensor that can be used on site and is so simple
that it can be used by anyone. This is a concept that requires minimal to no sample preparation
without any washing step. The premise is very much like a pregnancy test, where the urine facilitates
the washing and the ﬂow in one go [77]. However, in pregnancy test the transducer is not electronic,
but has the transduction in terms of the color formation which can be read with eyes, also called
a lateral ﬂow biosensor. Although, the analytical principle behind a pregnancy test is good as it is
simple to use with no sample preparation, it is hard to transfer it to this project, as the sample types
are very diﬀerent and it is very diﬃcult to determine anything without any sample handling. The
concept ’point of care’ is taken into account, as the biosensor should be present at the production site,
so that the measurement and result can be obtained within a short period of time. The biosensor will,
however, never fully realize the ’point of care’ concept.
Since the world is focusing on recycling, the question about the chip being disposable or be reused
multiple times arises in the future aspects. It is pointed out by the industry that they do not want
to reuse the chips in general, as the risk of getting a false negative or false positive result is highly
concerning [77]. Thereby, believing that one-time-use of the chips will give more accurate results.
When the sample is a food product, it includes both carbohydrates, fats and other compounds that
have the potential to stick to the electrode and make the detection imprecise, only making the error
larger for every reuse. There is also a risk when using the regeneration process, since it can leave
debris from the biological elements, that also can stick to the electrode and disturb the measurement.
All of these compounds can also bind to the biological elements and interfere with the accuracy of the
measurement. To make the reused electrode preferable in the mind of the regeneration process, the
process together with the amount of the biological elements have to be optimised, so that the error
is minimised in relation to occupying all the possible positions on the antibodies and ensure that the
solution only cleaves at one spot like between the antigen-antibody complex. However, the reuse of
the chips also depends on the sample handling. If the analyte is extracted from the matrix, there will
be less compounds that could stick to the chips and inﬂuence the measurement. For now, the chips for
a future biosensor are not reused, as the reused chips apart from having a higher risk of false negative
also are time consuming, since the chips have to be regenerated properly. However, for industrial use,
it is important to know the statistical behaviour of the batch of chips, meaning that the modiﬁcation
on each chip has to be nearly identical. As the regeneration process is not reliable yet, as seen in this
project, it would be best to have one-time-use electrodes, despite the fact that a chip could be reused
multiple times. A reused chip is in a better agreement with the world goal of trying to reuse materials.
To ﬁnd the best user-method, all of these issues have to be investigated and veriﬁed.
A biosensor for detection of a toxin like alternariol, could be used as an alarm system for the control
of the products and if the contamination of alternariol is around the maximum concentration limit,
the sample could go to an external laboratory to get another quantiﬁcation via HPLC before throwing
anything out, if the company needs a second opinion. However, the right sample handling of the
product and the toxin has to be found before one can be sure that the results are correct. While
seeking for funding for the biological elements used in this project, a lot of companies where contacted,
Page 111 of 151
==PAGE:117==
FUTURE PERSPECTIVE AND IMPLEMENTATION
it was made clear that a lot of the food companies get their raw materials, like peeled tomatoes, tested
by another organisation, which is responsible for the control and veriﬁcation, meaning that the ﬁnal
product is not tested. In the case of the brand Rynkeby, the organisation used by their juice department
is Sure Global Fair (SGF), which is assigned in the AIJN (European Juice Association) [78]. For a ﬁrm
like Rynkeby, a biosensor could be a great opportunity, as no external company has to be involved.
For other companies, where the ﬁnal product is a mixture of diﬀerent ingredients, the biosensor could
also be used as a ﬁnal test for their products, so they are reassured not to bring any toxic food to the
market. In all, an optimal biosensor system could be beneﬁcial, but would the companies like to have
the responsibility and test the product on site or are they paying their way out?
6.2
Optimisation of the Biological Elements
Use another Peroxidase Enzyme
In literature combined with the experimental results, it has been proved that the peroxidase enzyme
could be an important factor for the biosensor, as it seems to be inﬂuenced by the sample matrix and
by the content and pH of the substrate solution. Therefore, it could be beneﬁcial to use a peroxidase
enzyme that is more tolerant towards the matrix and has an optimal pH closer to the optimal pH of
the antigen-antibody complex. This could be one way of ﬁnding the optimal conditions for the entire
biological setup.
In nature, there are many diﬀerent peroxidase enzymes, that can oﬀer resistance towards diﬀerent salts,
organic solvents, temperatures and heavy metals, but also towards a wide pH range [33]. Especially,
the tolerance towards compounds like heavy metals could be useful in this project, as heavy metals
are suspected to be the cause of the decreased enzyme activity, when examining the matrix eﬀect
of tomato juice. However, it has to be considered that maybe not all the demands can be fulﬁlled,
as the enzyme could have high tolerance towards the heavy metals but does not have the right pH
optimum. One peroxidase found that could be considered is isolated from a citrus lemon tree. This
peroxidase is reported to have a pH optimum at pH 5, be activated by Mn2+, Na+, Mg2+, Zn2+ and
Ca2+, be tolerant towards Cs2+, Cd2+ and Ni2+, and can retain 30-50% of its activity in presence of
50% methanol, ethanol and isopropanol [33]. This peroxidase can withstand many diﬀerent metals,
which could help retaining the enzyme activity, but it has a pH optimum a little lower than requested.
One problem is that a tracer constructed with an alternative peroxidase like the one from the citrus
lemon tree could probably not be commercially available without having it specially designed for the
purpose.
Expand the Selection of the Antibody
As other similar mycotoxins both in structure and in toxicity are present in contaminated food together
with alternariol, it could be beneﬁcial to expand the selection of the antibody, so that the biosensor
could detect more than one toxin at a time and give an average measurement. This proposal was
suggested by Wang et. al. 2018 [20] for the toxins AOH and AME, which makes good sense, since
they are structurally similar, are potential health threats and are often found together, as they have a
common biosynthesis pathway. The idea is that instead of avoiding the other toxins, as there always
will be a cross reaction, it could be better to accept them. Even if, the biosensor becomes less accurate,
Page 112 of 151
==PAGE:118==
FUTURE PERSPECTIVE AND IMPLEMENTATION
this approach could provide a wider toxin range, which would be very useful, especially if the biosensor
should work as an alarm system. The only factor to be aware of is that the toxins can have diﬀerent
maximum concentration levels e.g. in processed tomato products AOH has the maximum allowed
concentration at 10 µg/kg, whereas AME has the maximum at 5 µg/kg, which the biosensor has to
take into account. The biosensor has to report an alarm at the lowest maximum concentration level,
where an HPLC can be included to determine the amount of each toxin and thereby determine if the
criteria are met.
6.3
Future Research and Outlook
As this project is a proof of concept project, there is a long way to a functional industrial biosensor
that can be marketed to companies. To take this project to the next level an outlook on the future
research is presented, where the ﬁnal goal is to test the toxin concentration in real life samples to
prove that the biosensor is working and can be utilized in practise.
• Find the best Sample Handling.
To determine the best way to handle the samples, it is important to determine the location
the analyte occupies in the product sample, meaning which part of the product that needs to
be kept. When knowing this it is also easier to determine the matrix eﬀect on the biological
elements. This research could also help determine how sensitive the biosensor has to become.
The location of the analyte in the product sample is a key factor. However, it is important that
the goal to keep the whole biosensor system as simple as possible is achieved.
• A better Chip Fabrication and PMMA Holder Design.
The electrode design or manufacturing process is highly important, as it determines the modi-
ﬁcation. Due to the design of the PMMA holder used in this project, the modiﬁcations made
during the preparation of the working electrode were replicated over the entire chip area, which
is unfavourable. To resolve this there are two approaches: one is to make the chip surface except
for the working electrode repellent towards proteins and molecules, so that the modiﬁcation is
only made on the working electrodes. Another is to design the PMMA holder in such a way that
the modiﬁcation only appears on the working electrodes, but when detecting electrochemically
can be opened up so that all electrodes (counter, reference and working) are present in the same
chamber.
• Observe and Inspect the Surface when Modiﬁed and Regenerated.
To get an idea of how the modiﬁcation and regeneration works and behaves, an observation
and inspection of the electrode surface would be preferable. To achieve this, a highly sensitive
instrument has to be used, which is maybe not available.
• Flow system, Regeneration process and Calibration Curve.
In a batch system there is a lot of manual pipetting and especially in the injection of the substrate
it causes some issues in the electrochemical detection, as the manual injection can be observed in
the signal. Therefore, also to get the biosensor closer to an industrial use, the biosensor should
be tested in a ﬂow system. Along with that, the concentrations for the antibody, tracer and
Page 113 of 151
==PAGE:119==
FUTURE PERSPECTIVE AND IMPLEMENTATION
analyte can also be optimised, so that the function of the setup is balanced. A ﬂow system
can test the biosensor setup and determine how it is regenerated and probably get a better
calibration curve, as the accuracy and reliability can be improved. When the rough model of
the ﬂow system is established and the optimal conditions are determined, the sample handling
and matrix eﬀect could be introduced together with a SLM membrane, if there is a need for it
or if the preparation method allows for it. To back up the gained data, an HPLC analysis could
be used, especially when testing unknown samples.

APPENDIX
7.6
A.6 Preparation and Storage of Biological Elements, Reagents and Chemicals
Biological Elements
Antibody
When the antibody arrives, the solution is divided into aliquots (20 µL) in 0.5 mL Eppendorf tubes and
stored at -20°C for long-term storage. When antibody is used in an experiment one aliquot is dried up,
used and then stored at 4°C for short-term storage. Should avoid multiple freeze-thaw cycles.
Tracer
The tracer is stored at 4°C or at -20°C depending on the companies recommendation and should be
used as quickly as possible during experiments so that the temperature is kept around 4°C. One could
divide the solution into aliquots (20 µL) in 0.5 mL Eppendorf tubes to avoid contamination, this was
done in case of alternariol, but not for cortisol.
Analyte
The analyte (cortisol and alternariol) arrives in powder format and is stored at -20°C before it is mixed
with an organic compound like DMSO or other, so that there is no risk of inhaling the analyte and
so that the analyte can be used in the experiments. The analyte cortisol is mixed with DMSO to a
concentration of 10 mM and stored at 4°C, and the analyte alternariol is also mixed with DMSO to a
main stock with the concentration of 40 nM and stored at -20°C, where the working stock of 1 mM is
kept at 4°C.
Example of mix the analyte alternariol: The main stock was mixed in the original bottle delivered from
the company, where it was decided that a main stock should be 40 nM, as this would be durable for the
experiments. The analyte amount obtained was 5 mg.
The standard formula:
m(g) = Q(mol/L) · Mw(g/mol) · V olume(L) →V olume(L) =
m(g)
Q(mol/L) · Mw(g/mol)
(4)
The calculation:
0.005 g
0.04 mol L−1 · 258.2 g mol−1 = 0.000 484 L ∼484 µL
(5)
Thereby, adding 484 µL of DMSO to the 5 mg alternariol powder for a main stock of 40 mM. The
solution is mixed by vortex and it is ensured that all the powder is dissolved in the DMSO. The main
stock is stored at -20°C.
To have a more suitable solution for the experiments, a work solution of 1 mM was made in a glass
bottle were 25 µL of the main stock is mixed with 975 µL of DMSO. The bottle was marked, covered
in aluminium foil and kept at 4°C.
Page 122 of 151
==PAGE:128==
APPENDIX
Reagents used for both ELISA and Electrochemical Detection
Tween20 al 5%
Tween 20 (5%) is made by adding Tween 20 to MilliQwater and if necessary add 10 g of NaN3. The
solution is mixed and kept at 4°C.
Example of mix:
25 mL of Tween 20 + 500 mL MilliQwater = 500 mL Tween 20 (5%)
(6)
PBST
PBST is made by mixing PBS (Phosphate Buﬀered Saline) from Sigma Aldrich and 5% Tween20 to a
concentration of 0.5%. PBST is stored at room temperature. PBS is kept at room temperature whereas
the Tween20 is kept at 4°C.
Example of mix:
495 mL of PBS + 5 mL of Tween20 (5%) = 500 mL PBST
(7)
Coating buﬀer
Coating buﬀer pH 9.6 (0.25 M) is made by dissolving 3.975 g of Na2CO3 and 7.32 g NaHCO3 in 500
mL of MilliQwater. The solution is adjusted to pH 9.6 and kept at 4°C for long-term storage. For
the experiments, the concentration of the coating buﬀer is decreased to 0.05 M with MilliQwater, pH
adjusted to 9.6, ﬁltered and kept at 4°C for long-term storage.
Example of diluting - 500 mL solution of 0.05 M:
100 mL coating buﬀer (0.25 M) + 400 mL MilliQwater = 500 mL coating buﬀer
(8)
Citrate buﬀer
Citrate buﬀer pH 5.6 (0.2 M) is made by dissolving 34 g of sodium citrate in 500 mL of MilliQwater.
The solution is adjusted to 5.5 with acetic acid, ﬁltrated and kept at 4°C for long-term storage. For the
experiments the citrate buﬀer is diluted to a concentration of 0.04 M by mixing with Milli-Q water.
Example of diluting - 200 mL solution of 0.04 M:
200 mL · 0.04 M
0.4 M
= 20 mL
(9)
Thereby, taken 20 mL of the 0.4 M citrate buﬀer and mix it with 180 mL of Milli-Q water to a solution
of 200 mL of 0.04 M citrate buﬀer.
3,3’,5,5’-tetramethylbenzidine
The stock of 3,3’,5,5’-tetramethylbenzidine (TMB) (concentration) is stored under vacuum in the dark.
For the experiment TMB powder is mixed with DMSO to a concentration of 0.6%. The solution is
Page 123 of 151
==PAGE:129==
APPENDIX
mixed in a glass tube, wrapped in aluminium folie and kept at room temperature in darkness. Only
exposed to light when mixing the substrate for the experiments.
Example of mixing - 1 mL solution:
Around 6 mg of TMB into 1 mL of DMSO = 1 mL of TMB 0.6%
(10)
The TMB powder and DMSO is mixed by vortex.
Hydrogen peroxide (H2O2)
For the experiments hydrogen peroxide (1%) is used. The solution is made by mixing with hydrogen
peroxide 30% to a 1% solution and the solution is kept in a plastic tube at 4°C for long-term storage.
Example of mix and dilution - 3 mL solution:
100 µL of H2O2 30% and ﬁll up to 3 mL with MilliQwater
(11)
Regeneration solution gly-HCl
The regeneration solution gly-HCl pH 2.2 is made fresh for the experiment and stored at 4°C for short-
term storage. The solutions is a mix of glycine, HCl and MilliQwater to a concentration of 0.4%.
Example of mix - 20 mL solution:
20 mL · 75.07 MW of glycine = 600.56 mg of glycine
(12)
20 mL · 10ln(2.2) = 122.8 µL of HCl
(13)
Thereby, mixing around 600.56 mg of glycine, 122.8 µL of HCL into 20 mL of MilliQwater. The solution
is mixed by vortex.
Ferri-Ferrocyanide solution
Ferri-Ferrocyanide solution made by a mix of K3Fe(CN)6 and K4Fe(CN)6 in PBS. The solution is covered
with aluminium foil and kept at room temperature.
Example of mix - 50 mL solution:
164.6 mg of K3Fe(CN)6 + 211.2 mg of K4Fe(CN)6 in 50 mL of PBS
(14)
MPA solution
The 3-mercaptopropionic acid (MPA) 80 mM is made by mixing MPA solution with MilliQwater. As
the buﬀer is mixed, it should be used, but else it can be stored at 4°C for a short term storage (not
recommended).
Page 124 of 151
==PAGE:130==
APPENDIX
Example of mix - 2 mL solution:
1982.58 µL of MilliQwater is mixed with 17.42 µL of MPA = 2 mL of MPA solution
(15)
MES buﬀer
MES buﬀer (mix of MES (2-(N-Morpholino) ethanesulfonic acid) from Sigma Aldrich and NaCl from
Sigma Aldrich in MilliQwater) adjusted to pH 6. When a large solution is made, it is ﬁltrated, covered
with aluminium foil and stored at 4°C. For the solution the ﬁnal concentrations is 100 mM for MES
and 200 mM NaCl in MilliQwater.
Example of mix - 2 mL solution :
Of MES (MW: 206.73 g/mol) →41.346 mg in 1 mL MilliQwater
(16)
Of NaCl (MW: 58.44 g/mol) →11.688 mg in 1 mL MilliQwater
(17)
The two solutions of 1 mL is mixed into one solution of MES buﬀer of 2 mL.
EDC and NHS in MES buﬀer
N-ethyl-N-(3-dimethylaminopropyl) carbodiimide (EDC) and N-hydroxysuccinimide (NHS) are both
from Sigma-Aldrich and are stored at -20°C under vacuum. For the solution the ﬁnal concentrations
are 200 mM for EDC and 50 mM for NHS in MES buﬀer and the concentration is therefore doubled in
the calculations as the two separate solutions are mixed into one. The compounds are taken out from
the freezer and put in vacuum for around 30 minutes before the amounts are measured and noted. The
compounds are put under vacuum again before going into the freezer for long term storage.
Example of mix - 2 mL solution:
Of EDC (MW: 191.70 g/mol) →76.68 mg in 1 mL MES buﬀer
(18)
Of NHS (MW: 115.09 g/mol) →11.509 mg in 1 mL MES buﬀer
(19)
The two solutions of 1 mL are mixed into one solution (EDC-NHS) of 2 mL.
As there is no need for 2 mL solution and since it is hard to take the exact amount of each, the volume
can be adjusted in relation to the amount.
Equation used:
The volume needed =
Amount (mg)
Concentration (mM) · MW (g/mol) · factor from L to µL = Volume in µL (20)
Page 125 of 151
==PAGE:131==
APPENDIX
Example of mix:
Of EDC (MW: 191.70 g/mol) →amount taken 41.1 mg
(21)
Calculating the volume needed =
41.1mg
400mM · 191.70g/mol · 106 = 535.99 µL ∼536 µL
(22)
Thereby, 536 µL of MES buﬀer is added to the 41.1 mg of EDC.
Of NHS (MW: 115.09 g/mol) →amount taken 4.5 mg
(23)
Calculating the volume needed =
4.5mg
100mM · 115.09g/mol · 106 = 390.99 µL ∼391 µL
(24)
Thereby, 391 µL of MES buﬀer is added to the 4.5 mg of NHS.
To make the EDC-NHS solution with the ﬁnal concentration of 200 mM EDC and 50 mM of NHS, the
limiting volume is deciding the total volume of the solution. The limit is the NHS solution of 391 µL.
Thereby, the EDC-NHS solution is made by taking 391 µL of the EDC and mixing it into the 391 µL
of NHS.
Citrate buﬀer with 0.1 M KCl
Citrate buﬀer (0.04 M) containing 0.1 M KCl pH 5.6 is made by dissolving 3.73 g of KCl in 500 mL of
0.04 M Citrate buﬀer. The solution is adjusted to 5.6 with acetic acid, ﬁltrated and kept either at 4°C
for long-term storage or at room temperature for short-term storage.
Example of mixing - 500 mL solution of 0.04 M with 0.1 M KCl:
Calculating the grams needed of KCl = Molar needed (mol/L) · MW (g/mol) · volume (L)
(25)
→0.1 mol/L) · 74.5513 g/mol · 0.5 L = 3.73gKCl
(26)
